A. Program Purpose and Project Development Objective
1. Program purpose and program phasing:
The Brazil Family Health Extension Project (FHEP), an operation under an Adaptable Program Lending
Instrument (APL) in three phases of three, two and two years each, seeks to improve utilization and quality
of publicly-financed health services by:
(a) Expanding coverage of the government's Family Health Program (Programa de Sauide da Familia, or
PSF) in about 100 large municipalities, establishing well-articulated referral and counter-referral
systems, and introducing performance-based financing and management arrangements;
(b) Establishing family health as a core element of health professional and para-professional training; and
(c) Strengthening MOH capacity to monitor and evaluate PSF health services, policies and training
activities on a systematic basis.
During the last 15 years Brazil has implemented a series of health policy reforms that have transformed the
organization, financing and provision of health services. In the late 1980s, Brazil converted its federal
public health financing system to a single national health fund. In the mid-1990s, it instituted a per capita
payment for primary care services that was distributed directly to municipalities. This simple reform
caused a vast improvement in the equity of the system - poor municipalities suddenly had funds for
primary health services on a scale they had not seen before. More recently, this capitation system was
enhanced by the Family Health Program, through which the federal government (the Borrower) transferred
additional funds to municipalities that agreed to implement a proactive primary health care model. During
the 1990s, the Brazil Federal Government focused on assisting rural municipalities to implement these two
programs. In both cases, the programs provided additional funds to municipalities for earmarked purposes,
and as long as the money was spent for that purpose, the municipalities fulfilled their obligation.
Policy Reforms: With the support of the proposed APL, Brazil is ready to take the next step with these
policy reforms. This step involves: (a) expanding PSF coverage significantly by focusing on large urban
municipalities; (b) transforming basic care delivery to the outreach-oriented PSF approach; (c) changing
the character of the PSF through shifting from input- to performance-based financing, monitoring and
management through federal-municipal agreements; and (d) improving municipal capacity to organize and
manage health services while strengthening the overall fiduciary framework at the municipal level.
Family Health Program (PSF): Initiated by the Ministry of Health (MOH) in 1994 as a relatively small
initiative, the Family Health Program (PSF) has successfully and rapidly expanded coverage of primary
care in rural and peri-urban areas, reaching about one-fifth of the Brazilian population (38 million) by
2000. In that same year, there were 10,000 PSF teams working in over 3,000 municipalities in all of
Brazil's states. The PSF is now one of the largest federally-funded health programs, spending US$380
million. GOB estimates federal spending on the PSF will reach US$410 million in 2001 and $550 million
by 2004. The proposed program will partially finance the annual marginal increase in federal PSF
financing, estimated at 10 percent annually during the seven-year proposed program. PSF coverage will
increase from 10 to 60 percent of the population in large, urban municipalities upon Program completion.
PSF Conversion: This policy reform concerns the conversion of the current primary care delivery system to
one based on PSF. Government seeks to convert the current, passive-provider, facility-based delivery
system to an active-provider, outreach model in which family health care providers deliver quality basic
health care to households and communities. This model of service provision aims to be more efficient by
focusing on prevention and early treatment, and using PSF as the gateway to higher-level services.
- 3 -



PSF is not merely another federal health program. Rather, PSF represents the platform on which the
government seeks to restructure how publicly-financed primary health care is organized and provided.
MOH authorities envision PSF as an approach to basic health care provision that will anchor the future
primary health care system in Brazil, which accounts for approximately US$1.8 billion in combined federal
and municipal health spending in 2001. The proposed APL will support the PSF Conversion process in
about 100 of the largest urban municipalities where the traditional delivery model is still dominant.
Performance-based Financing: Conversion represents much more than a bridge between the traditional
and PSF delivery models. It also constitutes a bridge between input- and performance-based financing.
Through the introduction of a new transfer mechanism to finance implementation of the PSF Conversion
process, MOH will link financing to achievement of performance benchmarks. Municipalities selected to
participate in the proposed Program will agree to a "Conversion Plan" for reforming their health services
and achieving specific performance targets in implementing the Family Health Program. Compliance with
these benchmarks will "trigger" municipal participation in each phase of the APL, and continued receipt of
the transfer. If successful, the intention is ultimately to combine all major transfers into a single transfer
modeled on the performance-based Conversion approach. As PSF approaches full coverage (during the
next 5-7 years) the MOH envisions the merging of the heretofore separate federal grants transfers that
currently finance primary health care under the traditional health care model and the Family Health
Program under the new model. The merging of these separate transfers will also prevent the continuation of
parallel systems with separate funding mechanisms, and therefore ensure PSF's sustainability.
Decentralization: The fourth reform entails decentralization of management responsibilities to the
municipalities for the implementation of primary care services and activities. In accordance with recent
regulations, NOAS 01/01, MOH seeks to complete the transfer of full management responsibility to the
municipalities for the delivery of primary care. Decentralization of management responsibilities began in
the early 1990s. Certification for "full management of basic care" entails installing: (i) the necessary
capacity to technically plan, implement, supervise and monitor primary care services; and (ii) fiduciary
arrangements and support systems such as auditing, accounting and information management to ensure
proper use of funds. The proposed Program will support decentralization through strengthening municipal
management capacity, introducing performance-based management systems, and establishing solid
monitoring and evaluation capabilities.
Innovative Pooling and Fiduciarv Features: For Component 1 Bank financing will be pooled with federal
grant transfers for three MOH programs: Basic Health Care (AB), Family Health (PSF) and PSF
Conversion. Both AB and PSF are large, on-going government programs financed through separate federal
grants transferred to municipalities which are responsible for service delivery. Under existing Borrower
arrangements, transfers for the AB and PSF programs flow through a National Health Fund, maintained by
the MOH, to a common bank account at each municipality. These grants essentially finance a similar set of
activities and services but under different models. The grant for AB finances services delivery under the
existing, traditional model while the PSF grant supports services provision under a proactive, outreach
model. The Borrower will also create a new grant to finance the PSF Conversion Program, or specifically,
the transitional costs of AB-to-PSF conversion. The new grant will also be transferred through the National
Health Fund to the municipalities.
To enable Bank's financial support of the PSF through the existing federal-to-municipal grant system, the
Borrower requested that the proposed project incorporate a special pooling arrangement together with
innovative fiduciary features. Under Component 1, Family Health Municipal Conversion and Expansion,
federal grant transfers under the existing AB/PSF programs are the pool of funds, the Programmatic Pool,
from which the smaller Conversion Subproject Pool would be derived. Through this Subproject Pool the
- 4 -



proposed Project would co-finance implementation of Conversion Subprojects. Conversion Subprojects
would consist of a set of pre-identified activities necessary to extend Family Health service delivery and
convert the health care model of the Basic Care Program to the model of the Family Health Program. Loan
proceeds would finance 30 percent of the cost of goods, works and services required to implement
Conversion Subprojects in selected municipalities. Expenditures would incur against pre-defmed and jointly
agreed subproject implementation arrangements and procurement plans specified in federal-municipal
contracts. Bank financing would be attributed to pre-defined, eligible expenditures of the Subproject Pool
and thus distinguished from the larger, AB/PSF Programmatic Pool financed solely by the Borrower.
Within the Subproject Pool, loan proceeds will be distributed as advances for expenditures to be incurred at
a rate of 30 percent of the grants transferred by the Borrower to pre-qualified municipalities to cover the
cost of goods, works and services of implementing Conversion Subprojects. Transfers to the municipalities
will be made according to an agreed timeline in accordance with the procurement plans for the Conversion
Subprojects.  Details regarding both the total amount of the transfers as well as the 30 percent
reimbursement claimed will be recorded on a Statement of Transfer (SOT), specifying projected eligible
expenditures of the Bank's share of financing of the Conversion Subprojects.
Under the proposed program, the idea is for each municipality to enter into a two-year agreement with the
federal government to implement Conversion Subprojects to convert its primary health care system to the
Family Health model, backed up by a procurement plan that is updated annually. The overall disbursement
schedule for Component 1 will be based on Conversion Subproject procurement plans,.agreed with the
Bank. Through the procurement plan, the Bank will have a good idea of eligible expenditures prior to
making disbursements into the pool that will finance the plan. Although the grants that finance each
municipal subproject will consist of eligible and ineligible expenditures, at least 30 percent of pooled
expenditures will be for eligible items. Preliminary projections suggest that the Subproject Pool will contain
a higher percentage of eligible expenditures than the Bank's 30 percent share.
To satisfy the Borrower's request regarding Bank participation in the above-described municipal financing
system, the proposed project will apply three innovative fiduciary features:
(i) Extension of period (to one year) for which advances (disbursements) would continue to be made
before the eligibility of expenditures are reconciled through Project Monitoring Reports (PMRs);
(ii) Loan proceeds would finance 30 percent of the Subproject Pool instead of 30 percent of each
transaction, as is normal practice;
(iii) Application of national rules for the procurement of small packages of goods and works, with
compliance assessed on an ex post basis.
The borrower's existing financial management and procurement rules and systems have been assessed and
deemed sufficiently robust to govern the use of Bank funds included in the pool (see Annex 6).
Nevertheless, during the life of the proposed APL the Bank will also work with the federal government to
supervise and strengthen municipal fiduciary processes. This means that a number of fiduciary oversight
and control measures (such as reporting requirements, record keeping, audits and remedies) will be applied
to the much larger AB/PSF Programmatic Pool of federal grant resources transferred to the municipalities,
and thereby increase the Project's development impact. The following are brief descriptions of
arrangements agreed with the Borrower to mitigate the potential risks of pooling (see Annexes 6 and 2G for
details):
(i) Federal assessment and certification of financial management and procurement capacity of each
participating municipality as a condition for Bank financing; based on risk assessment results,
- 5 -



municipalities will agree to an action plan to improve fiduciary practices;
(ii) Outputs and budget (with corresponding procurement plans and disbursement schedule) agreed on
an annual basis for each municipality prior to funding; a uniquely assigned chart of account codes
would specify those activities and corresponding goods, works and services required for
implementation of Conversion Subprojects;
(iii) Quarterly submission of Project Monitoring Reports (PMRs) by participating municipalities and
reviewed by Bank for monitoring purposes; full reconciliation would occur annually as part of
financial audit;
(iv) Participating municipalities would be required to meet fiduciary performance benchmarks
(together with health performance benchmarks); failure to reach performance targets would affect
their future eligibility for participation in the Program;
(v) Subproject Pool would finance ICB goods and QCBS consultants above defined thresholds
established according to results of the risk assessment; but procurement would follow Bank
guidelines and disbursements would follow traditional investment lending procedures;
(vi) Annual technical and fiduciary reviews carried out by the Borrower; and post-reviews carried out
by the Bank;
(vii) Specific remedies would be applied annually for misprocurement, financial mismanagement and
ineligible expenditures.
The pooling feature offers a number of advantages to both the Borrower and the Bank. For the Borrower,
the feature permits the Bank to mingle its funds with established grant transfers to facilitate the smooth
flow of funds to a large number of widely-dispersed and diverse municipalities. It also creates a single
reporting system for the grants rather than parallel and redundant systems for Borrower and Bank
purposes. Bank assistance is refocused to strengthen the fiduciary framework for the MOH programs
which will contribute to improved health services management. Finally, the feature reorients the partnership
between the Borrower and Bank, permitting the Bank to assist the MOH in realigning federal-municipal
relations to focus on performance and results.
For the Bank, pooling of funds affords the opportunity to: (i) form a long-term partnership with the
Borrower in the health sector since grant transfers are the only method used by the Borrower to finance
primary health care; (ii) participate in the most important reforms of the sector, and (iii) enhance
developmental impact by shifting Bank supervision from tracking transactions financed through loan
proceeds to oversight and improvement of the Borrower's fiduciary systems.
The APL Program: Table I presents Summary data on the APL phases while Table 2 shows the
dimensions of each Component by APL phase. Trigger indicators for APL Phase II are described in
Attachment 2 to Annex 1
Phase I. Initiating Conversion to PSF Model in Urban Municipalities, and Strengthening Training
Programs and Monitoring and Evaluation Systems. The first three-year phase will extend the PSF to
large urban municipalities, expand training opportunities for PSF team members, and develop core systems
to enhance quality and performance of PSF provision. Expected results include: (i) agreements approved
and under implementation in about 40 urban municipalities with greater than 100,000 residents to convert
primary care organization and delivery to the family health approach, increasing average PSF population
coverage from about 10 to 35 percent; (ii) establishment of a federal transfer mechanism linking financing
to performance in support of PSF conversion in participating municipalities; (iii) pre-service and in-service
training of about 14,000 family health professionals and para-professionals; (iv) development of regulatory
frameworks for the accreditation of PSF/Basic Health Units and PSF training institutions, and agreement
reached with the Borrower on the Phase II implementation plan; (v) PSF protocols for referrals and
- 6 -



counter-referrals and care practices developed and implemented for 50% of PSF teams in participating
municipalities; (vi) performance-based management system in support of PSF developed and tested in all
participating municipalities; (vii) monitoring and evaluation system for the PSF, Maternal and Child Care
(MCH) referral-counter - referral network approved, developed and tested, and agreement reached with
Borrower on implementation of Phase II.
Phase 11. Expanding PSF Coverage and Training and Impact Evaluation. The second two-year phase
will support the roll-out of the PSF in up to 60 additional urban municipalities, strengthening the quality of
institutions providing PSF-related training, and implementing systems to improve quality and performance
of PSF provision. Expected results include: (i) agreements approved and under implementation in up to 60
additional urban municipalities with greater than 100,000 residents to convert primary care organization
and delivery to the family health approach, increasing average population coverage from about 10 to 35
percent; (ii) continued implementation of the PSF program in the approximately 40 municipalities
supported under Phase I, increasing average population coverage within them to 50 percent; (iii)
application of the accreditation system to a representative sample of participating municipalities and to
about 50% of all PSF training centers; (iv) referral system operational for all PSF teams in participating
municipalities; (v) monitoring and evaluation system for PSF and MCH referral-counter-referral network
implemented in all participating municipalities; and (vi) performance-based management systems in
support of PSF developed and implemented in about 50% of participating municipalities.
Phase III. Reaching Municipal Coverage and Training Targets, and Transforming Evaluation into a
Permanent Process. The final two-year phase will support the consolidation of the PSF expansion and
conversion in urban municipalities, produce PSF trainees on a systematic basis, and institutionalize support
systems for quality and performance. Expected results include: (i) continued implementation of the
program in the approximately 100 urban municipalities supported under previous phases, increasing
average population coverage within them to 60 percent; (ii) monitoring and evaluation system expanded to
entire referral / counter-referral network (not just MCH) in all municipalities; (iii) impact evaluation of
PSF expansion and model conversion in municipalities supported in Phases I and II; and (iv)
performance-based management and monitoring and evaluation institutionalized.

2. Project development objective: (see Annex 1)
The project's development objectives during Phase I are:
(a) Increase coverage of health services among populations in urban centers with populations of
100,000 or greater, especially among those with limited access to and utilization of basic health
care, through expansion of and conversion to the PSF approach, development of a referral and
counter-referral system, and improvement in the management and organization of basic health
services;
-7 -



(b) Improve the quality of family health service provision through developing and strengthening
in-service and pre-service training of human resources in the PSF model; and
(c) Improve the performance and effectiveness of basic health care services through strengthening
monitoring and evaluation, information management and accreditation systems.
Coverage extension activities will focus on large urban areas, which are characterized by poorly integrated,
traditional service delivery systems and large numbers of families living in poverty and confronting severe
social risk. Of the 223 municipalities with populations of 100,000 or greater in the country, approximately
40 will participate in the Phase I project (and about 100 will participate by the end of the seven-year
program). The strategic aim is to raise PSF population coverage from about 10 to 35 percent within these
municipalities. Support for decentralization reforms and management improvements will also focus on
urban municipalities. Quality enhancement activities related to training, monitoring and evaluation,
information management and accreditation will yield nationwide benefits.
3. Key performance indicators: (see Annex 1)
Progress toward achievement of development objectives will be measured through a monitoring and
evaluation (M&E) system (see Annex 2e), which will build on established health information systems. The
M&E system will be developed under the activities of Component 3, and will collect and compare data
before (baseline), during and after project implementation. For some outcome measures, comparisons will
also be made between geographic areas that were affected by project interventions and those that were not,
adjusting for confounding factors. The evaluation system will provide the basis for determining whether
(and when) the triggers for subsequent phases of the APL have been met.
The project's key output/impact-related performance indicators include the following:
PSF Municipal Conversion and Expansion (Component 1)
Average population coverage in about 40 urban municipalities raised from 10 to 35 percent;
40 percent of matemity admissions from PSF catchment areas in the 50 participating municipalities are
referred by PSF teams;
30% reduction in number of hospital admissions for ARI for children under five from participating
PSF catchmient areas;
Performance-based management and supervision system developed and piloted in each municipality;
and
Performance-based, federal-municipal transfer mechanism approved and under implementation.
PSF Human Resource Development (Component 2)
In-service training provided to 10,000 PSF physicians, nurses and community health agents;
3,000 professionals trained in health services planning, management and organization; and
Family health residency programs established with at least 100 enrolled residents.
Monitoring and Evaluation (Component 3)
Development and approval of a regulatory framework for the accreditation of PSF/Basic Health Units
and PSF training institutions;
-8 -



* Evaluation completed of a sample of PSF teams and PSF training institutions, implemented between
1997 and 2001; and
Monitoring and evaluation system for PSF, including local feedback mechanism and performance
targets developed and tested.
B. Strategic Context
1. Sector-related Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) goal supported by the project: (see Annex 1)
Document number: R2001-0057(IFC/R2001-0069)     Date of latest CAS discussion: May 24, 2001
The objective and design of the proposed project is fully consistent with the themes of the Bank's Country
Assistance Strategy for Brazil, as well as the Bank's HlVP Sector Strategy.
The World Bank's Brazil Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) of March 30, 2000 (Report No. 20160-BR),
reiterated in the Progress Report of May 24, 2001, highlights poverty reduction as the overarching
objective of the Bank's work in Brazil. As indicated in the CAS (p. 32), health services for those with
limited access to and utilization of basic health care are among the several targeted interventions for
poverty reduction that receive funding from both domestic and international sources. Basic health services,
such as those supported and strengthened under the proposed project, would directly benefit the urban poor,
increasing the effectiveness of public subsidies and the health conditions of those in poverty. The CAS
notes that to support targeted poverty reduction, the Bank is increasing its efforts in the health sector, and
specifically "...the Bank supports health system improvements in the areas of health financing, regulation
of the pharmaceutical sector, decentralization, access to health care, health surveillance and management of
public facilities" (pp. 33-34) - most (but not all) of which are addressed by the proposed project. The
2001 CAS Progress Report confirmed the importance of attention to the social sectors in the larger agenda
of poverty reduction, and described the Family Health Project as "strategically important" (p. 15). In
addition, the CAS indicates that the Bank is "supporting the institutional strengthening of municipalities
and management of urban problems" (p. 38), which is also an important focus of the proposed project.
The proposed project is also fully consistent with the HNP Sector Strategy. It is designed with a
recognition of: (a) the Bank's comparative advantage in the areas of system strengthening; (b) the
importance of supporting reforms that separate the functions of finance, purchase and provision of health
services; and (c) the overall mission of the World Bank to address the needs of poor and underserved
populations, with a particular emphasis on cost-effective basic health services that meet the needs of
women and children.
2. Main sector issues and Government strategy:
Main Sector Issues
This section outlines the key challenges facing the Brazilian health sector. See the Sector Profile in Annex
2b for a more comprehensive description of health conditions, structure and financing of the health system,
and characteristics of the PSF.
Inequities in Health Outcomes Remain a Challenge. The most recent poverty assessment for the country
estimates that 23 percent (35 million) of the population lives in extreme poverty and 45 percent lives in
poverty. Extreme poverty (the cost of a minimum food basket) is defined as an individual living in a
household with a per capita income less than or equal to the equivalent of R$65 per month at Sao Paulo
Metropolitan prices. A poverty line that incorporates non-food expenditures has been determined at
$R132. Income is measured by the national household survey undertaken in 1996 (PNAD 1996).
-9-



Approximately half of the poor live in urban areas. (The relative importance of the urban poor might be
higher than the figure presented. PNAD income data tends to overestimate rural poverty because PNAD
questionnaire is unlikely to adequately capture income other than regular employment). Data from the last
Demography Health Survey (1996) shows that the health status of the Brazilian poor is significantly worse
than that of the non-poor. Children of poor families have three times higher risk of dying before the age of
5 than children from the wealthiest segments of the population. Fertility rates of poor teenage girls are 6
times higher than those of better-off teenagers, and poor girls face much higher risks of matemal morbidity
and mortality. The impact of violence and chronic diseases is also higher among the poor.
The Demographic Health Survey data suggest that (at similar income levels) the urban poor have slightly
worse health outcomes than the rural poor. Approximately 40 percent of Brazilians in the poorest wealth
quintile and 75 percent in the second poorest quintile live in urban areas. Health indicators among the urban
dwellers in the poorest quintile are as bad or worse than among those in rural areas. Infant mortality was
87.8 per 1,000 live births among the poorest quintile living in urban areas and 80 among those in rural
areas (Gwatkin et.al.). Among the fourth quintile, the rates are 49.1 per 1,000 among urban infants,
compared to 38.0 per 1,000 among rural infants.
While poor urban children have better vaccination rates and are more likely to be treated for diarrhea and
ARI than their rural counterparts, the prevalence of these childhood illnesses (diarrhea and Acute
Respiratory Infections) among the urban poor is higher. Although the urban poor have better access to
health services during childhood than the rural poor, the higher urban infant mortality raises questions
about the timeliness, continuity and quality of care received, and/or the severity of the underlying illness. In
general terms, the poor have utilization rates 25 percent lower than the non-poor in rural and urban areas.
These inequities - also found in OECD countries - reflect higher transaction costs and behavioral
barriers and have led the Government to introduce proactive community and family health delivery systems
such as the PSF, and to provide financial incentives for their adoption by municipalities.
Improving Performance Through Decentralization Depends on Improved Manaeement and Fiduciary
Capacity at the Municipal Level.  Brazil has taken an incremental approach to decentralization and
health system reform. Initiated with the constitutional reforms of 1988 and supported by laws, decrees, and
regulations spanning the 1990s, decentralization has transformed the National Health System (the SUS)
from a centralized system to one in which municipalities, and to a lesser extent states, are the main players
in the organization and delivery of public health services. Responsibility for primary care has been
transferred to nearly all of the country's 5,500 municipalities. They receive monthly block grants from the
MOH to finance primary care that are partially matched by the municipalities themselves. Under current
rules, about 10 percent of municipalities have qualified for "full management" status in which they receive
block grants to manage primary, secondary and tertiary care, including paying private hospital providers.
Although there is no hard evidence demonstrating the effect of decentralization on health status, service
quality or system efficiency and equity, the MOH as well as outside observers have noted a number of
shortcomings in the decentralization process that may threaten future system performance and PSF
expansion: (i) mismatch between delegated responsibilities and existing institutional capacity of many
municipalities; (ii) unclear division of responsibilities between states and municipalities particularly in
terms of planning, supervision and financial and information flows; (iii) weak municipal capacity to
organize and manage health service delivery, in part due to a dearth of trained management professionals;
(iv) absence of systematic monitoring and evaluation; (v) tendency of municipal managers to focus on
inputs rather than results and outputs; and (vi) inability of many states and municipalities to implement
functional referral systems.
- 10-



Recognizing these and other deficiencies of the decentralization process, the MOH issued new regulations,
Norma Operacional de Asistencia de Saude - NOAS/SUS 01/01, in January 2001. The NOAS 01/01
represent a mid-flight correction of previous decentralization regulations (in 1993 and 1996). MOH
authorities also perceive the regulations as closely linked to the PSF strategy, especially in terms of
facilitating municipal capacity building, strengthening monitoring and evaluation functions and moving the
system from its current uncoordinated, non-hierarchical structure to one that has functional and
cost-effective referral and counter-referral systems in which PSF serves as the entry point. The major
elements of the NOAS 01/01 are described later in this section.
The MOH also recognizes that without effective financial and procurement management by municipalities,
accountability of health providers and effective delivery of health services will be elusive. Use of sound
management practices will provide essential information to managers on program implementation,
contribute to efficient use of resources and deter fraud and corruption. The proposed project aims to
enhance development impact by assisting municipal governments to improve financial management and
procurement practices as applied to all municipal spending on primary care - independent of source of
funding - while strengthening federal capacity to oversee and monitor these practices.
Reforms in Financing of Health Services are Advancing but Remain Incomplete. Recent reforms in
health financing gave a strong impulse to the decentralization of basic care, strengthened the resource base
available for health and demonstrated the governnent's strategy of gradual improvement of the health
fnancing system. In 1998 the federal government replaced fee-for-service reimbursements for basic
interventions by a system of per-capita transfers for primary care (PAB -- Piso Assistencial Bdsico). The
amount capitated is augmented for municipalities that adopt priority programs promoted by the
government, such as the PSF.
Transfers are also larger for municipalities that achieve a wider coverage of the population with PSF.
Hospitals are reimbursed on the basis of production based on diagnosis-related groups and a fee schedule
set by the MOH. Because the demand for services exceeds the amount the federal government is willing to
spend on health, there is a rationing system whereby each state is allocated an annual ceiling for SUS
funding. In recent years, MOH has introduced other cost-containrnent measures, including the application
of physical and financial ceilings for inpatient services and direct control over high-cost interventions.
Although there is evidence that the rate of increase of the cost of hospitalizations has slowed, the system
may be overburdened with so many financial ceilings, floors and incentives that effective management is
difficult.
After years of struggling with temporary measures, in 2000 Congress approved Constitutional Amendment
29 (EC 29), mandating an increase in the funding levels for the health sector by the federal government
(linking growth in health sector financing to GDP growth), and earmarking 12 percent of state revenues and
15 percent of municipal revenues to the health sector. In practice, SUS finances about half of the 7 percent
of GDP spent in health, covering around 75 percent of the population. Two-thirds of SUS spending is
financed by the federal government, 18 percent by the states and 15 percent by municipalities.
Despite vigorous federal action to increase financing for health care and introduce alternative payment
systems, with the intention of creating an equitable and rationalized system, virtually all observers agree
that the reform agenda is far from complete. Currently, the Brazilian health system continues to be
characterized by: (a) instability in funding flows, and large but decreasing differentials in per capita health
care resources across states and municipalities, reflecting in part the persistence of historical financing
patterns but also uneven levels of commitment to health; (b) persistent bias toward high-cost hospital
- 11 -



services and, correspondingly, slow improvements in the quantity and quality of basic care; (c) insufficient
linkage between financial mechanisms and health priorities; (d) poor incentives for managers of primary
care units (mostly municipal) and hospitals (mostly private) to use public resources efficiently; (e)
management weaknesses at the municipal level, particularly in managing the PAB and applying these
resources to purchase effective service delivery, in using information for monitoring and evaluating
performance, and for taking strategic corrective actions; and (f) absence of rigorous analyses of resource
use.
The Family Health Program Has Major Potential, but Faces Obstacles to Future Expansion. PSF
was launched in 1994 building on the strengths of its 1991 predecessor, the PAC (Programa de Aten9do
Comunitaria). PSF was conceived as a linchpin in the transformation of the mainly curative and
hospital-centered health care system toward one that is oriented toward prevention, promotion and basic
care. The program seeks to develop high-quality primary care services that serve vulnerable populations in
a proactive manner; eventually these services are expected to serve as an entry point to higher level
services. PAC introduced the use of paid health agents who reach out to the community in an organized
fashion - for example, by creating a roster of the full beneficiary population in a given zone; identifying
pregnant women, young children, tuberculosis patients and others in need of frequent attention and
follow-up; and providing home-based care or actively encouraging patients to come to health facilities for
necessary services. PSF organized community agents into teams, which typically include a general practice
or family health physician, a nurse, one or two auxiliary nurses and more recently a part-time dentist and/or
dental technician. The PSF built upon the PAC by providing the teams with equipment (medical, transport,
and basic information technology), a standardized system of work and reporting requirements. Each team
is assigned the responsibility of providing care to a defined set of 600-1,000 families with use of
standardized protocols.
Today, the PSF covers nearly 38 million people (about one-fourth of the Brazilian population) in 3,124
municipalities with 10,900 Family Health Teams. Some municipalities have not yet implemented PSF but
have PACS. In total there are almost 150,000 community agents, in 4,621 municipalities. Under current
government plans, the PSF will provide coverage to 50 percent of the Brazilian population, which means it
will have to double in size. Most of the expansion of PSF to date has taken place in rural and peri-urban
areas where municipal primary providers were in short supply. Its implementation in urban areas is only
patchy, in part because PSF faces the dual challenge of extending coverage and converting the traditional
and often ineffective municipal primary care delivery system to one based on the family health strategy. A
comparison of summary features of the traditional model of providing primary care, common in urban
centers, and the family health model is presented below:
Table 2

- 12 -



Several evaluations and assessments of PSF have been conducted by the government and other agencies.
The main benefits found by these studies can be summarized as follows: (a) Increased coverage of basic
care in small size municipalities with high infant mortality rates; (b) High acceptance of the program by
health professionals and users; (c) PSF is a successful vehicle for channeling other government programs to
the community and enhancing multisectoral collaboration; and (d) Positive impact on performance and
outcome indicators, including: increased production of and access to basic health services; reduced hospital
admissions and hospital care; and reduced infant mortality.
Despite these accomplishments, the government's PSF strategy faces significant challenges, including:
Limited coverage in large urban areas: Program expansion in large urban areas and metropolitan
regions has been slower than expected despite recent changes in financial incentives. Approximately 10
percent of urban populations are covered by the PSF in municipalities greater than 100,000 inhabitants,
compared to about 50 percent in rural areas. PSF coverage is about 7 percent in the largest municipalities
with populations of greater than 500,000 inhabitants.
Inconsistent targeting and vrioritv-setting: A survey carried out by the MOH in 2000 showed that more
than half of the state program managers did not have clear criteria for prioritizing the introduction of the
program. The study also revealed that municipal health authorities prioritize program interventions and
geographical areas by applying a mix of risk criteria (epidemiological or social) combined with community
and political demands. Targeting the program is not only a matter of selecting the geographical area to
implement PSF within a municipality, but also relates to determining what health priority should be
addressed first, and with what health intervention. A recent World Bank study suggests that PSF teams
may become overburdened with tasks as they respond to federal, state and municipal health campaigns,
programs and demands, and thus weakening their effectiveness. Explicit criteria based on sound community
health data will be required for municipal authorities to target and prioritize PSF within their jurisdictions.
Inadequate referral systems: The referral and counter-referral system in most of the SUS is vague and
functions badly. This is a critical problem for maternal and child health. For example, medical records on
antenatal care are not linked to treatment at the time of delivery or to postpartum care. This situation also
compromises the capacity of the system to diagnose and then rapidly treat obstetric emergencies. One
factor that contributes to the weakness of the referral system is the lack of a defined entry point for the
population and the absence of a provider who assumes responsibility for the total care of the individual and
family. In addition to serving as the gatekeeper to higher levels of care, the PSF addresses this issue by
making primary care teams representatives of their patients as they move through the health delivery
system. Experience has shown, however, that insertion of the PSF strategy into the traditional systems
(especially in urban areas) has been a slow process in part because of health worker resistance to adopting
new care practices, work habits and workloads. MOH authorities have concluded that the delivery system
and corresponding working environment evident in many urban municipalities is not conducive to meeting
system coverage and quality objectives, and therefore successful adoption of the PSF strategy will require
converting or replacing the traditional, facility-based model.
Uneven quality of PSF services:  The MOH surveys of PSF teams revealed that, still, a significant
proportion continue to lack adequate equipment and supplies. Family health teams report shortages of
certain vaccines and basic medicines. In addition, laboratory support services are inadequate for key
exams such as urinary analysis, cytopathology, and obstetric sonogram, among others. A 2001 survey of
523 teams revealed that 40 percent do not have half of the standard equipment and medical supplies
- 13-



stipulated by the MOH for PSF teams. Quality of care can be improved through investment in
infrastructure and supply/support systems, better treatment protocols/standards, quality control and
systematic supervisory mechanisms, and training. About 67 percent of state program coordinators reported
that they did not have clinical protocols. Protocols are defined by the MOH as instruments that guide
family health teams on best strategies and clinical practices that result in improved quality and
effectiveness of the program. Finally, health care providers and administrators lack adequate knowledge on
providing humane care and effectively promoting behavioral change in the community.
Human resource supply bottlenecks in terms of quantitv and auality: Municipalities report difficulties
in hiring family health professionals to expand coverage. In terms of qualifications, 37 percent of PSF
physicians have completed their medical residency in family health (compared to a national average of 75
percent for other disciplines) and only 40 percent of PSF physicians and 36 percent of nurses have
specialty training. A recent World Bank study found that the skills of PSF teams vary considerably.
Medical schools train few generalists, and students do not demand such training because labor markets do
not reward general medicine, and the specialization garners relatively low professional status. Family
medicine training programs at medical and nursing schools are scarce, and specialized curricula have yet to
be developed. Only seven university hospitals offer family health residency programs for physicians
desiring to specialize in family health, graduating about 25 specialists annually.
The MOH has taken the initiative to sponsor the development of 30 training centers, known as P6los de
Capacitaciio, in universities, technical schools and government agencies throughout the country. These
centers train new personnel for PSF teams as well as provide in-service training to upgrade the skills of
existing teams. Between 1997 and 2000 the MOH invested $22.8 million in these centers, and projects to
spend about $4 million annually between 2001 and 2004. A rapid assessment of 12 training centers in 2001
found that they were supplying an important service to PSF teams in their respective regions, providing
training courses and continuing education to PSF teams on a large variety of health-related themes and care
practices. However, impact was impossible to gauge since none systematically monitors or evaluates
knowledge acquisition and skill application, or assesses satisfaction with the program. These centers have
not been formally evaluated or accredited.
Unstable contractual arrangements: According to the government assessment, most PSF personnel are
hired under temporary contracts (49 percent of doctors, 44 percent of nurses), or are paid on a piecework
basis (27 percent of doctors, 24 percent of nurses). These arrangements are made either directly by the
municipalities or through NGOs, cooperatives, or other autonomous entities that then sub-contract the
health professionals. A 2001 survey found that between one-third and one-half of PSF workers do not have
formal or signed contracts with their employers. These arrangements have resulted in numerous legal and
political challenges which in turn may slow extension of the PSF. Although the contracting arrangements
for staff under the PSF promote flexibility in personnel management, decrease the size of civil servants
payroll and benefits, and may be an important driver of PSF success to date, they also result in high
rotation of personnel (compromising program continuity and doctor-patient relationship) and job
dissatisfaction. The federal government is interested in retaining the successful elements of these
contracting arrangements while also addressing some of the more serious problems that have arisen.
Government Strategv
The federal Government is taking specific actions to advance the reform agenda in general, and to improve
the quantity and quality of core health services that will benefit those with limited access to and utilization
of basic health care services, in particular. The actions are manifested in policy statements, budgetary
- 14 -



allocations, the introduction of financial and institutional incentives, and exhaustive consensus-building
with stakeholders in the health sector.
Basic health care is very high on Brazil's development agenda. The federal government's four-year
development plan (Avance Brasil - Plano PlurianualiPPA 2000/2003) states that expansion of the PSF is
the leading priority for the health sector. PSF is also a key component of the government's IDH-14
program. In 2000, the federal government started an initiative of targeting key existing social programs to
the 14 poorest states of the country (all 9 states of the NE region and 5 states of the N region). The
methodology for selecting the municipalities of the 14 poorest states is based on UNDP's Human
Development Index. The government prioritized the following programs in the health sector: reducing
infant mortality through improved water and sanitation services; expanding coverage of Family Health
programs; and combating maternal and neonatal mortality. PSF was one of the health programs protected
by the Bank's Special Social Protection SECAL (IBRD) during Brazil's 1999 economic crisis. A widely
publicized government target is to expand PSF to provide coverage to 50 percent of the Brazilian
population by the end of the year 2002. To advance this goal, as well as the reform agenda, the Borrower
has taken a number of initiatives that are supported by the proposed project:
(i)  Established clear priorities for the health sector: The primary health care interventions that are
most cost-effective and beneficial to the poor are being strongly promoted. In November 2000, the
XI Congreso Nacional de Saude- an event that takes place every four years and is the result of
consultations involving professional associations, local and regional governments and popular
organizations-produced a list of National Health Priorities (with measurable indicators) focused
on communicable and non-communicable illnesses that primarily affect the poor.
(ii) Secured financing for the sector and PSF: In addition to the passage of EC 29 that has increased
financing for the health sector, the Plan Pluriannual assigned 14 percent of the MOH budget to
PSF over this period, second only to the amount budgeted for hospital services. MOH spending on
PSF more than doubled between 1997 and 2000 (from US $140 to US $380 million).
(iii) Adjusted the decentralization process and corresponding financial mechanism: The MOH has
approved new regulations that seek to correct observed deficiencies in the decentralization process
while strengthening institutional arrangements to facilitate PSF expansion. Municipalities and
states that comply with the regulations will qualify for higher federal transfers. Broadly, with the
objective of organizing and rationalizing access to and use of higher level services; these
regulations (NOAS 01/01) require: (i) municipalities to emphasize PSF as a gateway to rationalize
the use of higher levels of service; and (ii) states and municipalities to form Health Regions
consisting of clusters of municipalities that would provide the minimum market size required for an
efficient operation of tertiary care facilities.  Sustainability also requires the reduction of
cross-subsidies to private insurers. After a decade of unregulated growth, the government has
recently passed legislation and created an agency to regulate the buoyant private health insurance
industry. One of the objectives of the regulators is to reduce SUS subsidies to private insurers. A
computerized clearing house could bring significant savings if regulatory policies are developed
appropriately. The regulations also strengthen management requirements for municipalities to
quality for "autonomous management" status and grant a stronger role to the states in overseeing
municipal resource management. States and municipalities are also required to develop health plans
and sign performance agreements that specify performance targets. Municipalities that comply
with new regulations will be "certified" by the MOH to receive an increased per capita payment
(PAB fixed). Importantly, the PAB fixed covers a broader range of services than current PAB
resources because it includes resources for specialty ambulatory centers heretofore paid
- 15-



retrospectively according to an established price list. The expanded per capita payment will
provide a strong incentive to municipalities to comply with NOAS 01/01 conditions.
(iv) Prepared a human resource policy: MOH has drafted a human resource policy (for approval in
early 2002) that supports the development of personnel skilled in health services management,
health planning and family health strategies and practices. The new policy also seeks to standardize
and regulate contractual arrangements affecting PSF teams.
3. Sector issues to be addressed by the project and strategic choices:
The proposed project aims to support the Government's vision of a health sector that places the highest
priority on quality basic health care, and rationalized, integrated financing and delivery of higher-level
services. In broad terms, the project will address essential inputs for a better-functioning and more
responsive health system: first, municipal capacity for the expansion of PSF and for the organization and
management of basic and higher-level health care; second, the quantity and quality of human resources for
the delivery of basic health services; and third, policy- and program-relevant information and knowledge to
improve system performance. These have been identified by both Brazilian authorities and the Bank as
catalytic in nature-areas that are high-priority for the Government, and where targeted actions, supported
by strong policy and technical work, will have large payoffs in improved performance and sustainability of
PSF and broader reform efforts. The sector issues to be addressed by the project are highlighted below.
Expansion of basic care, development of referral networks and strengthening of municipal capacity
will be supported under Component 1. Much of the expansion of the PSF has been achieved through
the addition of new health teams to municipalities where basic care was in short supply. The introduction
of the PAB and corresponding PSF financial incentives and their rapid growth transferred significant
amounts of fresh money to these municipalities, and these resources were used to hire, train and equip the
new teams. Three types of challenges have been identified for the next stage of PSF extension: (a) In many
urban municipalities PSF has yet to become established. The next phase needs to deal not only with
expanding PSF coverage into areas with new epidemiological needs (e.g. violence, alcoholism), but also
with converting traditional primary care facilities into PSF units. In many cases, conversion will require
upgrading these units with inputs necessary to provide quality services. The ultimate goal, however, is to
replace traditional primary care systems with strengthened PSF-based ones; (b) Successful horizontal
(population) expansion will require vertical integration of PSF into the broader service system. This will
require the organization of service networks through clustering facilities across municipal lines (per NOAS
01/01) while setting clear protocols regarding referrals and the role of PSF teams in case management; (c)
Many municipalities lack experience and capacity to organize and manage services, administer resources
and oversee referral systems. Most focus on managing inputs with little notion of outputs or results. A
major challenge is to introduce performance-based management systems that make municipal managers
more effective purchasers of services by linking resources to performance. The project will address these
obstacles by providing incentives for municipalities to develop full-scale but practical plans for
transforming the model of care - Conversion Plans - and by investing in upgrading the service network,
and enhancing the information, management and supervisory support functions necessary for the full
functioning of the PSF and referral systems.
Supply bottlenecks of family health professionals will be addressed by Component 2. A large-scale
effort is required to bring the supply of health professionals into line with the demands being generated by
the transformation in the care model. As noted above, there currently is a severe shortage of health workers
of all types, and deficits in the quantity and quality of skills among the members of existing PSF teams.
The project will address this problem by supporting, expanding and improving the work of training
- 16-



institutions whose mandate is to provide in-service, pre-service and specialty training in family health. It
will also support the development of specialty and residency programs to produce family health nurses and
physicians. Moreover, the project will provide instruments to permit the MOH to assess the quality of
training, and make adjustments as required. Shortcomings in monitoring and evaluation - essential for
sustained high performance - will be addressed by Component 3. The ultimate vision of the health sector -
one that has functional referral and counter-referral networks, and induces continuous quality improvement
at all levels - depends on excellent monitoring and evaluation systems. Currently, the lack of an
"evaluation culture," as well as shortcomings in the methods and processes for monitoring and evaluation,
are major constraints to the development of a quality- and performance-oriented service delivery system.
Under the project, significant investment will be made in developing monitoring and evaluation systems
and, importantly, in establishing systems for accreditation of Family Health units and training institutions.
4. Program description and performance triggers for subsequent loans:
The FHEP supports the Government's efforts to expand its successful PSF to large municipalities, with the
expectation that it will increase access to, utilization of, and health benefits from basic health services,
organized around the work of family health teams. The FHEP provides technical and financial resources
for the creation and strengthening of family health teams in most of the country's large metropolitan areas;
development of sustainable in-service, pre-service and specialty training programs for members of the
family health team; and large-scale, long-term improvements in monitoring and evaluation of PSF
performance across the country. The program will finance a variety of inputs, including small-scale
remodeling of primary care units, medical and non-medical equipment, vehicles, training and consultancy
services. Approximately 35 percent of the financing will be allocated through conversion subprojects of
Component 1; the remainder will be administered directly by the MOH, and will fund activities that yield
national benefits.
The seven-year APL has three phases. Consisting of three years, the first will support extension of the PSF
to large urban municipalities, expansion of training opportunities for PSF team members, and development
of core systems to enhance quality and performance of PSF provision. The second two-year phase will
support continued roll-out of the PSF in urban municipalities, strengthen the quality of institutions
providing PSF-related training, and implement systems to improve quality and performance of PSF
provision. The final, third phase will continue to support the roll-out of the PSF urban municipalities,
consolidate PSF expansion and conversion in urban municipalities, produce PSF trainees on a systematic
basis and institutionalize support systems for quality and performance. Performance triggers for Phase II
are presented in Table 2 below. A complete description of expected results for each phase is presented in
Attachment 1 of Annex 1.

C. Program and Project Description Summary
1. Project components (see Annex 2 for a detailed description and Annex 3 for a detailed cost
breakdown):
Component 1. PSF Municipal Conversion and Expansion (US$86.9 million)
This component will support the extension of basic care services through the expansion of the population
covered under the PSF model in about 40 municipalities with populations of over 100,000 inhabitants. This
will be achieved through the implementation of Basic and Family Health Care Conversion (Conversion
Subprojects) to convert the organization and provision of basic health services from a traditional model to
the prevention, outreach- and performance-oriented Family Health Model; strengthen the decentralization
regime, following the NOAS/01 regulatory framework; and introduce a performance-based transfer
mechanism.
In 2001, the Family Health Program (PSF) covers an estimated 10 percent of the population in the 223
municipalities with over 100,000 inhabitants (see Table 4 below). Of the 30 municipalities with over
500,000 residents, PSF coverage reaches only 7 percent in nearly half. Federal spending on AB and PSF
in these 223 municipalities reached $416 and $48 million, respectively, in 2000. Total primary care
spending, including municipal contributions was approximately $536 million in 2000.
The specific objectives of the component are to: (a) expand access to and utilization of basic health services
by populations at risk in urban areas, which currently are underserved, through the implementation of the
PSF model and upgrading of the service network; (b) establish functional referral and counter-referral
systems through establishment of integrated service networks; (c) strengthen the system of PSF
management and organization; and (d) improve the performance of the municipal providers of basic health
services through development of performance-based organizational and management models, and the
introduction of new health care practices.
Implementation and Financing Arrangements: Municipalities seeking support under the project will submit
a Conversion Plan (Plano de Conversao) as well as related Basic and Family Health Care activities that,
taken together, will constitute the Basic and Family Health Conversion (Conversion Subprojects). Criteria
for eligibility and selection of participating municipalities as well as guidelines for the preparation of the
Conversion Plans will be established by the Ministry of Health (MOH), and included in the project's
Operational Manual (OM). Annex 2G presents summary eligibility criteria, and outlines the selection
process. In addition to health-related criteria, eligible municipalities must meet financial management and
procurement requirements per risk classification methodology described in Annex 6. The Conversion Plan
guidelines will draw on PSF strategies, institutional requirements stipulated in recently approved
regulations (NOAS 01/01 -- regarding formation of networks and organization and management of health
services) and analyses of successful experiences in transforming traditional primary care to the PSF model.
The Conversion Subprojects will form the basis for Subproject Implementation Agreements signed between
the selected municipalities and the Ministry of Health, which will set forth the respective obligations
regarding subproject implementation, including: (i) broad description of a seven-year conversion program
including global end-of-program performance targets; (ii) detailed description of Conversion Subproject
- 20 -



activities to be implemented in the first three-year phase; (iii) technical and fiduciary performance targets
that will determine eligibility for participation in subsequent APL phases(Annex 2F presents the
performance indicators required for participation in Phase 2 of the APL); (iv) procurement plans and
corresponding disbursement schedule for subproject activities, specifying eligible expenditures; (v)
obligations regarding reporting, recording keeping, maintenance of accounts, and auditing; and (vi) terms
for cancellation of disbursements and/or the Agreements.
Bank financing will be pooled with federal transfers used by the Borrower to finance health program
implementation: Family Health (PABfam), Basic Health Care (PABfixed) and Conversion Plans
(PABconv). From a technical perspective, these three transfers essentially finance a similar set of activities
related to primary health care. PABfixed finances the Basic Health Care Program (AB) under the existing
model while PABfam finances basic care services under the Family Health Model through the Family
Health Program (PSF). PABconv will finance the transitional costs of converting primary care delivery
from the existing model to the family health-based approach. Based on the procurement plans of the
Conversion Subprojects, a virtual Subproject Pool will be dimensioned within the larger AB/PSF
Programmatic Pool of transfers. The Subproject Pool will exist only for selected (eligible) municipalities,
which will be responsible for implementing the Conversion Subprojects. The Subproject Pool would be
spent for activities constituting the Conversion Subprojects and related to Conversion Plan implementation,
PSF extension and Basic Care provision.
Given the solidity of the borrower's fiduciary framework and the agreed risk mitigation measures, as
described in Annex 6, the Bank has agreed to disburse into the Borrower's grant transfer system, advancing
funds against Statements of Transfers (SOTs). The Conversion Subproject procurement plans will be the
basis for establishing the subproject disbursement schedule for Bank disbursements into the grant transfer
system. Based on expenditure data supplied by participating municipalities, the MOH will submit quarterly
Project Monitoring Reports (PMRs) to monitor and compare reported eligible expenditures with
SOT-based expenditures. Full reconciliation (based on PMRs) will occur annually, and if necessary, agreed
remedies will be applied for misprocurement, mismanagement and ineligible expenditures. Loan proceeds
will finance 30 percent of the Subproject Pool for eligible small works, equipment, goods, consultant
services, training and operating costs for non-ICB goods and QCBS consultants. The Subproject Pool will
finance 100 percent of ICB goods and QCBS consultants, but not as advances. Rather, disbursements will
follow traditional investment lending procedures in which documentation such as contracts and invoices
will be required to support withdrawal applications.
Component Content: Once approved by the MOH, Conversion Subprojects will be implemented by the
selected municipalities. The following groups of activities, grouped into two subcomponents, will be
financed through the subproject pool.
(a)  Family Health Extension and Basic Care Provision: This activity will support the expansion of PSF
population coverage and the delivery of basic care in participating urban municipalities through
financing incremental operational costs involved in the organization, supervision and provision of
family health and basic care services, as specified in the Operational Manual. Annex 2A describes
these activities and services.
(b) PSF Conversion: This subcomponent will consist of the following groups of activities:
*    Institutional modernization: Through financing of consultancy services and training
workshops, seminars and materials, activities will seek to strengthen municipal capacity to
regulate, organize, manage and provide health services through developing and implementing
- 21 -



performance-based management agreements, policies and regulations for contracting human
resources, and evaluations of user satisfaction and community participation. Activities will
also support the preparation of municipal investment plans, referral and counter-referral
systems and family health practice protocols.
*    Upgrading of municipal health service provision: Activities are intended to improve the service
delivery capacity of municipal primary care providers through supporting remodeling of health
centers and polyclinics, procurement of medical and non-medical equipment, furniture, vehicles
and operating costs.
*    Strengthening of municipal information systems: Activities will support incorporation and use
of information technologies for planning and monitoring and evaluating health system
organization and performance. Financing will include consultancy services, computer
equipment and software and training workshops and materials.
*    Training and supervision: Activities will include development and implementation of training
courses in strategic areas and work processes in support of PSF expansion, the conversion
process and strengthening of supervisory systems. The subcomponent will finance consultancy
services and training, workshops, seminars, publications and materials.
Component 2. PSF Human Resource Development (US$37.4 million)
This component will support the implementation of a human resource policy that contributes to the
expansion of the PSF model; strengthens the qualifications and performance of family health professionals
and PSF teams; and introduces new contents and methods in the preparation of health professionals. This
component will have a national focus and be managed through the Project Coordination Unit (PCU).
The objectives of the component are to: (a) increase the number and quality of PSF human resources; (b)
expand and strengthen pre-service and in-service training for PSF human resources; (c) expand and
strengthen programs for professional training, including residencies in family health, specialty training for
physicians, nurses and dental hygiene assistants; and (d) introduce special programs (such as training
center consortiums and virtual networks to exchange information) and demonstration projects that support
and strengthen the human resource development activities.
Many of the activities financed under Component 2 will be implemented through the Polos de
Capacitacao, training centers that offer courses and training modules directed at professionals on the
Family Health Team. In addition, there are approximately 100 institutions of higher learning-universities,
faculties and medical and nursing schools-which together with the State and Municipal Health
Secretariats will be able to participate in the Program. Activities for the para-professional members of the
team, particularly in the case of dental technicians, will be implemented through Health Technical
Schools (Escolas Tecnicas de Satide), which are linked to the State and Municipal Health Secretariats.
Existing contracts with training institutions will be supported under this subcomponent, as well new
contracts, selected on a competitive basis.
Activities are grouped into three subcomponents:
(a)  In-service training of PSF professionals and para-professionals:  This subcomponent aims to
strengthen PSF service organization and provision by supporting in-service training for several types
- 22 -



of service managers, health professionals (doctors and nurses) and para-professionals (community
health agents). Through financing training (consultancy services and workshops) and training
materials and publications, training programs will be developed and implemented in clinical
practices, public health, health planning, clinical and non-clinical management. The subcomponent
will also support development of national strategies, plans, guidelines and training modules for
medical waste management for primary care facilities.
(b)  Pre-service and specialty training of PSF professionals: Through financing consultancy services,
scholarships, intemships, training workshops, conferences, materials and publications, this
subcomponent will provide pre-service training to professionals relevant to the organization and
provision of services under the PSF model. This subcomponent will support the development and
expansion of programs that prepare health managers, family health physician and nurse specialists,
dental hygiene assistants and community health agents.
(c)  Demonstration activities to support training and education: This subcomponent seeks to improve the
quality and quantity of training opportunities for current and future members of PSF teams. This will
be accomplished through the creation of regional networks of training centers, creation and
maintenance of a web-based clearinghouse for training materials, and piloting of curriculum
development programs in medical and nursing schools to develop new and improved educational
methods and materials relevant to the family health discipline. The subcomponent will finance
consultancy services, audio-visual equipment, training workshops, conferences, materials and
publications.
Project Component 3. Monitoring and Evaluation (US$ 7.1 million)
Under this component, rigorous and coordinated monitoring and evaluation methodologies will be employed
to assess the structure, processes, outputs and impact of the PSF. This will entail an effort to build on,
improve and integrate several existing information systems in the health sector. In addition, evaluation
methodologies, instruments, strategies and plans developed under other FHEP components will be applied.
The primary objectives of the component are to: (a) create systems for permanent, cost-effective monitoring
and evaluation of the PSF and the training institutions' performance; and (b) develop and implement
systems of accreditation of basic family health units and PSF training centers.
The project will provide a central source of financing for four subcomponents, described below. Similar to
Component 2, activities will be conducted on a national basis, and will be managed through the PCU within
the MOH. As necessary, the MOH may enter into subcontracts with universities and/or technical
institutions for management of research.
(a)  Strengthening of monitoring systems: Activities will support the consolidation and standardization of
monitoring systems and instruments for basic health care. It will strengthen PSF monitoring,
incorporate performance measures into monitoring systems, expand and strengthen regional technical
support centers (Centros Regionales de Capacitacion para o Monitoramento de Atencao Bdsica)
responsible for collecting and synthesizing health system data and overseeing PSF performance,
development of integrated data systems and introduction of new information technologies. Financing
will support consultancy services, training, computer hardware and software, study tours and
training workshops, materials and publications.
(b) Evaluation of PSF implementation: This subcomponent will support the development of evaluation
methodologies and instruments to measure the acquisition and application of skills by individuals
- 23 -



trained in Family Health and the impact of training center networks, demonstration projects,
curricular and pedagogic innovations supported in Component 2. It will also support impact
evaluations of a sample of PSF teams and PSF training centers implemented between 1997 and 2001.
The project will finance consultancy services, publications, training workshops and materials, study
tours and seminars.
(c)  Accreditation of the basic family health teams, facilities and training institutions: Through financing
consultancy services, publications, training, workshops and seminars, this subcomponent will
develop an accreditation regulatory framework and corresponding systems for: (i) family health
teams and facilities; and (ii) PSF training centers (Polos de Capacitacdo).
(d)  Research and Evaluation Fund:  This subcomponent will support evaluations and operational
research on topics related to the development and performance of the PSF and the related training
institutions, the process of decentralization of the SUS, human resources in the health sector,
efficiency and effectiveness of the PSF, and other strategic topics identified by the MOH. Awards
will be made on a competitive basis. The project will finance consultancy services, publications,
workshops and international seminars.
Component 4: Project Management (US$3.8 million)
This component would support project management for all components, including the design and
implementation of a monitoring information system to monitor and supervise project activities. This
component would also support issue-specific studies related to seeking solutions to implementation
problems, including qualitative as well as quantitative techniques. The component would finance consultant
services, facilities and equipment for the maintenance of the PCU and per diem for supervision in the field.
