2. Key policy and institutional reforms supported by the project:
The FHEP supports the major lines of policy and programmatic reforms currently underway in the
Brazilian health sector, including:
(a)  Transformation of a traditional, curative-oriented model of care to a preventive, outreach-oriented
model of care.
- 24 -



(b)  Decentralization of health system management responsibility to the municipal level.
(c)  Regionalization of health systems, to optimize hospital and other high-level resources over a defined
geographic area.
(d)  Introduction of performance-based financing mechanisms for primary care services.
(e)  Development of a culture of evaluation, quality and performance-based management.
3. Benefits and target population:
The main project benefits will include improved health outcomes for the population of large urban centers
who currently have limited access to and utilization of basic health care services; greater access to high
quality basic health care services; greater municipal, state and federal capacity for management of the
health care system, including development of information, monitoring and evaluation systems; more and
better-trained professionals and other health care workers prepared to deliver basic, outreach-oriented
health care.
It is important to note that the proposed project does not target low-income beneficiaries per se. The PSF is
not a program exclusively for the poor. It is a model for providing basic care for the entire Brazilian
population. Government social programs are universal and will remain universal, but the government
recognizes that the barriers to universal access are felt primarily by low-income populations. In addition to
devoting the extra resources, the Government aims to reduce access barriers by extending the PSF-based
outreach model. The proposed project will benefit low-income populations, since they are the main users of
SUS through self-targeting. The Living Standard Measurement Survey conducted in 1996 and 1997
showed that the poor rely heavily on public providers, particularly for basic health care. For example,
nearly all prenatal care received by poor women in urban areas is delivered by public providers. A 1998
survey found that 75 percent of Brazilians with an incomplete primary education -- a proxy for poverty --
and 62 percent with incomplete secondary education were frequent or exclusive users of public facilities.
4. Institutional and implementation arrangements:
A Project Coordination Unit (PCU) will be established in the MOH within the Secretariat of Health
Policies and will report to the Director of the Department of Basic Health Care (DAB) for the
implementation of the PSF. It will work closely with the existing organizational structure of management,
technical, human resources and evaluation in the DAB which is in charge of the implementation of the
FHEP at all levels. The main function of the PCU is to manage all aspects related to project
implementation, to guarantee that project components are executed according to terms and agreed
timetable. It will be staffed by individuals who have had experience in implementing the FHEP or other
Bank-funded projects. The results of an institutional analysis carried out by the Secretariat of Health
Policies (SPS) and the DAB showed the need for strengthening the Department with additional qualified
staff, and for providing training to existing staff to facilitate internal coordination and implementation of
project activities. The Family Health Project would finance these new staff, the functioning of the PCU and
additional support staff for the Unit. The functional structure of the PCU is outlined in more detail in
Annex 2D. An organization chart is presented in Attachment 2 of Annex 2D.
An Operational Manual (OM) will detail procedures for monitoring of compliance with performance
benchmarks, reporting, procurement, financial management, disbursement, and auditing procedures for
each of the components. The OM would also contain: Project Implementation Plan for Components 1, 2
and 3; eligibility criteria to select municipalities under Component 1, performance indicators, and a draft
of federal-municipal implementation agreements as well as regulations related to the approval of
Conversion Subprojects. It will also specify eligibility requirements and implementation processes for
Research Grants financed through Component 3. Regarding procurement, the OM will specify: (i) the
- 25 -



content of the procurement plans; (ii) the procedures to be used by the PCU and the Bank to classify the
risk level of the participating muncipalities; (iii) the methodology to be used by the PCU to assess the
capacity of the participating municipalities; (iv) the procedures to conduct and coordinate procurement
audits of Component 1 activities; (v) the procedures for monitoring aggregate amounts for non-ICB
procedures; and (vi) the application of standard bidding documents under Components 2 - 4. The issuance
of the final version of the OM will be a condition for effectiveness.
D. Project Rationale
1. Project alternatives considered and reasons for rejection:
No project alternative: As a result of recent government financial forms, the health sector will benefit
from increased funding during the foreseeable future. Bank financial participation in PSF expansion is
important but not a critical factor. The Borrower and Bank agreed, however, that the value added of the
loan and Bank involvement would be to assist the MOH to: (i) introduce outputs- and performance-based
approaches toward PSF implementation and expansion; (ii) transform primary care practices to a PSF
model; (iii) insert PSF implementation into recently approved decentralization reforms (NOAS 01/01); and
(iv) develop and implement strong monitoring and evaluation in support of PSF implementation.
Standard investment operation: After considerable analysis, both the Borrower and the Bank agreed on
utilizing the APL to support the proposed project given the following considerations: (i) the long term
horizon for meeting the program's developmental objectives; (ii) the existence of a sound sector reform
program developed by the government; and (iii) the GOB's decision to stage the PSF expansion and
decentralization reforms because of the level of complexity of these activities and the need for piloting new
arrangements not fully evaluated yet in the sector. The APL will support the GOB's incremental approach
to PSF expansion and sector reform, while "locking in" long-term institutional and political commitment to
the program.
Focus on rural vs. urban areas: The project will focus on extending PSF to urban areas. There is already
substantial coverage among small municipalities with large rural areas as MOH focused PSF expansion
there during the first implementation stage (1997-2001). Coverage extension in rural and small urban areas
also was relatively easy due to weak presence of municipal providers, population density and interest from
political leaders. One of the goals of the FHEP is to reach populations with limited access to and
underutilization of basic health care services. In this context, the urban focus of the proposed Bank
operation is also justified because 38 percent of those in the poorest income quintile live in urban areas, and
health indicators among the urban poor are as bad or worse than indicators among the rural poor.
Standard vs. flexible disbursement scheme: To satisfy the request of the Borrower regarding Bank
participation in the grant-based municipal financing system, the proposed project will apply two fiduciary
innovations under Component 1 that, taken together, result in a more flexible disbursement scheme. First,
it would extend the period (to one year) for which advances (disbursements) would continue to be made
before the eligibility of expenditures are reconciled. Second, loan proceeds would finance a share of the
Subproject Pool instead of a share of each transaction, as is normal practice. Applying more standard
disbursement arrangements would compromise effective flow of funds and jeopardize Bank financial
participation in the PSF or any federally-funded health program. Given the special remedies and risk
mitigation arrangements agreed with the Borrower, and specified in Annex 6, the Bank team chose to craft
a more flexible disbursement scheme.
- 26 -

3. Lessons learned and reflected in the project design:
The design reflects lessons related to (a) the impact of the PSF in Brazil; (b) international experience with
the implementation of primary care models; and (c) previous Bank work in health operations and
evaluations. The main lessons and design responses are:
The PSF has great potential to improve the coverage and qualitv of basic care, as well as health
outcomes. Several assessments and formative evaluations have been carried out by the government and
other institutions (including an assessment done by the Bank) regarding the performance of the PSF
program. The main benefits found by these studies are: (i) increased coverage of basic health care in small
size municipalities with high infant mortality rates; (ii) high satisfaction with the program by users; (iii)
positive impact on registered infant mortality rates, vaccination coverage, childhood nutrition, and
hospitalization rates; and (iv) increased production of basic health services. The evaluation findings provide
the rationale for supporting expansion of the PSF. At the same time, they point to the fact that the positive
- 27 -



experiences of the PSF have occurred primarily in rural or small urban areas where basic care supply was
irregular at best. Expansion to large urban areas may require adaptation to a different set of supply and
demand conditions. These adaptations can only be made on the basis of a thorough understanding of
household and provider characteristics in the municipalities, and continuous, rigorous, program-relevant
monitoring and evaluation. Design of activities under Component 3 of the project reflects an understanding
of the importance of monitoring and evaluation to ensure that the PSF reaches its potential during the
expansion period.
Successful implementation of new primary care models depends on major changes in underlying
incentives. Family health or similar (outreach) models of primary care can be found in other countries
including the United Kingdom, Canada, Honduras (nutrition) and Cuba. Other countries in Latin America
also are trying to develop new models of primary care, such as Mexico, Guatemala and Honduras
(nutrition). Some of the implementation lessons obtained by these experiences include the following: (i) The
most difficult part of the reform is to effectively change the culture of both the health providers and users.
(ii) While there are numerous experiences of development of new primary health care programs that have
been successful in expanding access to the poor, the team found fewer examples of successfully linking
these programs to higher levels of care; (iii) New programs tend to be developed in years of economic
growth and fiscal expansion based on additional money. When the expansion of these programs is not
accompanied by a simultaneous reform of the traditional programs, the new programs tend to be
short-lived: thus, for example, when there is a fiscal contraction, the new programs are the first to be cut as
the traditional programs tend to have stronger legal and political means to defend themselves. As a response
to these lessons, the proposed is supported by and strongly reinforces the financing and institutional
reforms already under implementation. Developing a robust monitoring and evaluation system that provides
continuous feedback on performance is a key element contributing to program success.
Given the dynamic nature of the Brazilian health system. allow for flexibilitv - but within an agreed
upon framework that emphasizes results. Implementation experiences of other World Bank projects,
including the Second Northeast Basic Health Services (98), AIDS and STD Control (98), Amazon Basin
Malaria Control (97), and Endemic Disease Control (97); MTR of the ongoing REFORSUS project (99),
as well as broader health sector and country portfolio reviews, indicate that project success can depend on
flexibility in the face of changing economic and political conditions, and ability to reorient when new
knowledge becomes available. The instrument selected for the project - an APL - provides the benefits of
a three-year planning horizon, permitting mid-course corrections. At the same time, its focus on showing
results during each two-year interval, and maintaining an overall development framework, keeps "big
picture" issues at the forefront.
- 28 -



4. Indications of borrower commitment and ownership:
As explained in the text, the PSF has been a core element of the federal government's efforts since 1994 to
improve primary care and raise per capita health expenditures by municipal governments. There is a
long-term commitment to this program. Commitment by the federal Government is strong for PSF
expansion into urban areas, given that this highly visible operation fits squarely into its main policy and
programmatic priorities. As noted earlier, the four-year development plan (Avance Brasil - Plano
PlurianualiPPA 2000/2003) states that the PSF is the leading priority for the health sector. PSF is also a
key component of the government's IDH-14 program. In 2000, the federal government started an initiative
targeting key existing social programs to the 14 poorest states of the country (all 9 states of the
Northeastern region and 5 states of Northern region). The methodology for selecting the municipalities of
the 14 poorest states is based on UNDP's Human Development Index. The government prioritized the
following programs in the health sector: reducing infant mortality through improved water and sanitation
services; expanding coverage of the Community Agent and Family Health programs; and combating
maternal and neonatal mortality. The President and the Minister of Health have publicly announced the
targets for the expansion of PSF. The program has been well received in many municipalities, but has
encountered resistance in others. While the commitment of specific states and municipalities still needs to
be determined and would become a key factor for eligibility under the project (especially for Component I),
the project team received positive responses from leaders of opposition parties who run municipalities in
Sao Paulo and Bahia and have launched public efforts in support of PSF.
5. Value added of Bank support in this project:
The Bank's value-added to the project includes: (a) experience with adjustment and performance-based
lending, which can now be applied to municipal reform programs; (b) global international experience and
expertise on project design and implementation, particularly in the areas of financial incentives and transfer
mechanisms, program targeting and program evaluation; and (c) broad strategic perspective on the sector
and capacity to bring into the health sector experiences from other key government agencies and ministries,
as well as with other developmental agencies and donors.
E. Summary Project Analysis (Detailed assessments are in the project file, see Annex 8)
1. Economic (see Annex 4):
* Cost benefit    NPV=US$ million; ERR = % (see Annex 4)
0 Cost effectiveness
O Other (specify)
NPV=US$2.1 billion; IRR=91% (see Annex 4)
A detailed analysis of the estimated economic costs and benefits of the FHEP projected that the APL will
yield a net present value of benefits, after investment and recurrent costs, of some US$2.1 billion (about
US$2,058 million), and produce an internal rate of return above 91 percent over a 10-year period. A
summary of the main features of the analysis is provided in Annex 4; the full analysis is available in project
files.
In terms of the costs of implementation, the analysis considers two basic elements: (i) the external
investment costs of the Bank program; and (ii) the recurrent costs. The total cost of the program is of
US$3.314 billion. Of this amount, the Bank would finance US$550 million and the Borrower would
finance US$2.764 billion. The recurrent costs reach nearly US$700 million per year after the sixth year;
once the project is fully operational. This amount includes the recurrent and incremental costs of
- 29 -



investments in infrastructure, equipment and maintenance, as well as the cost of increase in coverage for
basic health services through the primary care network.
The expected benefits of the project have been estimated based on the evaluation of pilot projects in two
municipalities. The analysis distributes benefits into two major groups: direct and indirect benefits. The
direct benefits are the expected tangible benefits accruable to the project from the cost reduction due to a
decline in the number of hospitalizations by the improving management of morbidity at the primary care
level. These cost savings will be realized through two sources: First, the project will result in reduced
hospitalizations due to greater capacity to resolve medical needs at the local level, thus reducing referrals to
hospitals. Second, the project will reduce risk factors in the target population that increase the demand for
hospitalizations. Another probable source of savings, not explicitly quantified in this study, is the reduction
in hospitalization costs caused by the increased efficiency of health service networks due to improved
management practices. The project will shift healthcare provision from expensive hospitals to more
efficient and higher quality primary care centers.
Indirect benefits are related to the benefits obtained from the additional life-years for the population of the
targeted districts. The monetary value for this figure is calculated by projecting the present value of GDP
per capita over the total number of additional life-years obtained from the reduced mortality. The
cost-benefit analysis assumes that the strengthening and changes made to the health care system will be
targeted to 84.5 million people, and that a 10-year period is required to reflect the flow of costs and
benefits.
The analysis found that benefits outweigh investments by a ratio of more than two to one. Over a 10-year
horizon, the present value of the cost of the project is US$1,800 million, while the present value of the total
benefits is estimated at more than US$3,800 million, thus giving the project a net present value (NPV) of
approximately $2,058 million. The NPV of the project over a five-year time frame is estimated at US$321
million, thus demonstrating the robustness of the project. With the exception of the first three years, the
project is expected to generate annual savings to the system that outstrip the investment cost, thereby
paying for them. The internal rate of return (IRR) for the project is estimated at 91 percent, reflecting the
extremely high benefits relative to project costs. This ratio can be used to demonstrate the value of this
project relative to other projects in other sectors; any number lower than the discount rate (10 percent)
would imply that the project should not be carried out.
A sensitivity analysis was perforned, assuming delays of two and three years, as well as a reduction in
benefits of 20, 30 and 40 percent. The reduction in benefits may be derived from a lower than projected
impact on health indicators or from reaching fewer people than the a priori estimates. The analysis
demonstrated that the project would remain justifiable, even assuming a three-year delay in project benefits
or a 40 percent reduction in the benefits received. The analysis highlights the importance of focusing on
measurable results. A significant reduction in benefits of 50 percent would no longer justify the project,
assuning no additional benefits are generated other than those estinated conservatively in the economic
analysis.
2. Financial (see Annex 4 and Annex 5):
NPV=US$ million; FRR = % (see Annex 4)
Fiscal Impact:
Based on the current projections for health expenditures, once the project is fully implemented the
- 30 -



incremental cost is estimated to be equivalent to nearly 4 percent of the public expenditure, from year seven
onwards. (A summary of the main features of the fiscal impact analysis is provided in Annex 4; the full
analysis is available in project files.) More importantly, the sustainability of the project depends on the
availability of incremental spending in the sector that will sustain the recurrent costs attributable to the
project. If the sustainability of the project is analyzed in terms of the overall percentage of annual increases
in MOH spending absorbed by the project's recurrent costs, rather than the total public sector spending, the
percentage increases to 80 percent of the annual increases of MOH expenditures. Even with the increase in
resource expected from the implementation of EC 29, the full implementation of the project will imply a
continuous effort to target to family care the projected increases in health sector resources. Sustainability
should not be a problem, with incremental spending around 4 percent of public health spending. However,
the project's viability will be highly dependent on maintaining political support and a priority allocation of
incremental budget revenues.
3. Technical:
Project design is built upon the findings and recommendations of a series of technical studies and
evaluations of the current reform process. Presented in Annex 8, these include technical assessments of the
health system financing, decentralization, basic health care model (PSF), municipal capacity constraints,
characteristics and productivity of training institutions, and information systems at municipal, state and
federal levels. In addition, the World Bank's study of matemal and child health was used as the basis for
identifying key health problems, and recommended approaches to solve them. All information was used to
propose a design that would fit local conditions, needs, priorities and respond to sustainability issues.
4. Institutional:
The PCU staff at the federal level have had experience managing Bank-funded projects in Brazil. Project
management capacity exists at the DAB, but it is variable at the municipal level. A recent institutional
analysis of the DAB showed that to take on additional project implementation activities, the Department
will need to add a small number of additional staff members and provide training related to project
activities to the existing staff. In addition, a financial management and procurement assessment was
undertaken and recommendations have been incorporated into project design. The MOH agreed to take
additional steps to close some capacity gaps in financial management and procurement during
implementation, by providing extensive technical assistance principally to weak municipalities and
institutions to improve their procurement, financial management, program administration and institutional
capacity (see Annex 6 for proposed actions).
The Borrower is the federal Government of Brazil, and as per Risk Assessment Matrix in Annex 6, no
major risks were identified which would require special recommendations or actions, other than those
already in place and/or included in the action plan.
4.1 Executing agencies:
Ministry of Health
4.2 Project management:
A Project Coordination Unit (PCU) will be established within the Secretariat of Health Policies and will
respond to the Director of the Department of Basic Health Care (DAB) for the implementation of the PSF.
It will work closely with the existing organizational structure of management, technical, human resources
and evaluation in the DAB which is in charge of the implementation of the FHEP at all levels. The main
function of the PCU is to manage all aspects related to project implementation, to guarantee that project
- 31 -



components are executed according to terms and agreed timetable. It will be staffed by individuals who
have had experience in implementing the FHEP or other Bank-funded projects. The results of an
institutional analysis carried out by the Secretariat of Health Policies (SPS) and the DAB showed the need
for strengthening the Department with additional qualified staff, and for providing training to existing staff
to facilitate intemal coordination and implementation of project activities.
4.3 Procurement issues:
The Government of Brazil and the World Bank have recently completed the first stage of a study on
government procurement in Brazil and have concluded that the legal framework and implementation are
generally acceptable and guarantee adequate transparency. Other preliminary results of this review suggest
that the judicial protests or recurso continue to cause major delays in govemment procurement. Another
finding indicates that the use of COMPRASNET and of E-purchasing (pregdo eletronico) and other modem
procurement methods will make government procurement more efficient and transparent, and this Project is
likely to benefit from the use of such methods at the municipal level. The Bank has completed a capacity
assessment of the PCU and has developed an action plan for its improvement. There is no need to carry out
a capacity assessment of the Institucoes Polo (IP), as they will be subcontractors for the Ministry of Health
in carrying out training activities. Under the Conversion Subprojects, the Ministry of Health, through the
PCU, will be responsible for upstream, procurement-related activities, including assessing the implementing
capacity of the participants, preparing and reviewing procurement plans, developing and monitoring
improvement action plans, justifying risk classification, coordinating procurement reviews, and preparing
management reports on compliance and prior review activities. In order to discharge these responsibilities,
the PCU will organize a procurement team consisting of at least one senior procurement specialist and one
junior procurement specialist, both of whom should be already familiar with the Bank's procurement
guidelines. This team will assess the implementing capacity of the selected municipalities and determine the
level of risk involved in the case of each beneficiary municipality using a methodology acceptable to the
Bank. The team will prepare the procurement section of the Project's various directives and of its
operational manual.
4.4 Financial management issues:
On the basis of the assessment carried out by the team's Financial Management Specialist, it was
concluded that the project and the PCU satisfy the Bank's minimum financial management requirements
and will have in place a financial management system that can provide with reasonable assurance, accurate
and timely information on the status of the project required by the Bank. The project is capable of
disbursing using SOEs (for Components 2-4) and statements of transfers (SOTs) for Component 1 and will
also be able to reconcile SOTs with eligible expenditures to produce the financial reports in FMR format
on a quarterly basis.
The financial system will have the capacity to generate PMRs automatically, and will be adapted to the
particularities of this project, as per action plan (see Annex 6). The action plan includes the agreements
with the PCU on the standards of the Financial Statements in PMR format, and defines the timeframe for
their submission duly certified by Public Auditors.
5. Environmental:                  Environmental Category: C (Not Required)
5.1 Summarize the steps undertaken for environmental assessment and EMP preparation (including
consultation and disclosure) and the significant issues and their treatment emerging from this analysis.
The FHEP foresees no large civil works activities, but has potential for generating both positive and
negative environmental impacts. LCSES has determined that FHEP is a Category "C" project. In the first
stage of implementation of the FHEP, there will be increased demand for specialized outpatient services,
because the program will represent a new channel of access to medical services for that segment of the
- 32 -



population that did not previously have access to such services. In this stage, the negative impacts of the
process are concentrated mostly in increased generation of waste material at the health units, with the
consequent need for more effective controls. For the most part, these measures involve implementation of a
System of Waste Management that includes minimization of generation plus segregation of waste. At the
same time, however, the FHEP has great potential for producing positive impacts. Over the medium-term,
there will be a reduction in demand for urgent and emergency services, as health services are improved, the
number of hospitalizations due to avoidable causes also is expected to decline. In this case, positive impacts
will result from a drop in the volume of waste generated, together with improved health conditions for the
population.
Specific actions to be taken include the following:
As outpatient health service units generate approximately 80 percent less waste material than large
hospitals, and the waste produced at such facilities is considerably less dangerous, there is a need for
simplified waste management procedures suited to the specific circumstances of basic health care units.
Annex 2C provides an example of such a system, and cost estimates, from a system in Belo Horizonte-MG
(See Annex 2G). Under Component 2 the borrower will develop a national strategy, implementation
guidelines and manual, and training modules to aid in the task of managing the waste generated within
basic health care units. The purpose of the manual will be to orient all activities targeted at minimizing the
impact of waste generation at these units. The training module will focus on the effective implementation of
waste management practices, utilizing the waste management manual for basic health care units. The
manual and training modules will be field tested with Family Health Teams in a sample of municipalities.
The program will be gradually expanded in a systematic fashion during future APL phases.

6. Social:
6.1 Summarize key social issues relevant to the project objectives, and specify the project's social
development outcomes.
Among several social development issues related to the FHEP, four deserve special attention: (i) inequality
in access to health services and, consequently, the inequality of health status among different groups and
areas; (ii) the growing problem of diseases, such as AIDS, that have large behavioral components, and that
strongly affect vulnerable subgroups of the population, such as women, and those who are socially and
economically under-privileged; (iii) poor reproductive health status, with high matemal mortality and
teenage pregnancy rates; and (iv) a traditional emphasis on "curative" medicine, centered on hospitals and
on fragmented interventions by specialized professionals.
- 33 -



The PSF, supported in strategic ways by the FHEP, was designed to improve coverage, particularly for
populations with limited access to and utilization of health care services; promote comprehensive
interventions; diminish the need for hospitalizations; and disseminate a concept of health as well being,
de-linking it from diseases and hospitals. Among the core family health interventions to be strengthened are
those that yield the greatest improvements in maternal and child health. Thus, the project directly addresses
each of the problems listed above. In addition, another Bank operation (VIGISUS) is designed to support
AIDS control efforts. As the project will only support the expansion of the PSF to municipalities with
more than 100,000 inhabitants, OD 4.20 is not trigged. The VIGISUS Project has a specific component for
indigenous health, which has been successfully implemented.
Social development outcomes include: (i) alleviation of inequities in health outcomes as a result of
improved access to health interventions of low-income populations; (ii) civil society strengthening by the
incorporation of civil society organizations, service providers, universities and research institutions, and
communities as partners in the institutional arrangements of the project; (iii) gender equity by the
enhancement of women's health status as a result of comprehensive interventions for families; (iv)
enhancement of social risk management by strengthening information of families and communities; (v)
improvement of the country's safety net by strengthening the health care system and risk management; and
(vi) training and job creation that will disproportionately benefit women who make up the vast majority of
health personnel contracted through the PSF.
6.2 Participatory Approach: How are key stakeholders participating in the project?
The PSF is an ongoing program and its implementation has been accompanied by a variety of consultation
mechanisms with key social actors. For the preparation of the proposed project, specific participatory
evaluations were also carried out, including exploration of the reasons underlying both successful and
disappointing PSF experiences. The project's design benefited from extensive consultation through
workshops, group discussions, and qualitative and quantitative assessments with families, community
agents, nurses and physicians. A diagnosis of women's health status was also conducted, recognizing the
central role that women play in the health and well-being of their families.
6.3 How does the project involve consultations or collaboration with NGOs or other civil society
organizations?
The PSF is community-based, involving NGOs, universities and specialized research institutions for
training, monitoring and evaluation. A number of NGOs are involved in PSF personnel contracting and
management, and to a lesser extent, in service delivery.
6.4 What institutional arrangements have been provided to ensure the project achieves its social
development outcomes?
The project is participatory by design. Families and communities are the units to be targeted, and
community health agents are one of the key players of the program.
6.5 How will the project monitor performance in terms of social development outcomes?
To monitor performance with respect to social development outcomes, the M&E system will include
beneficiary assessments. Surveys on user satisfaction have often been used to evaluate the functioning and
impacts of the ongoing program. An analysis of "best practices" of some of the current experiences has
been initiated and, once finalized, will provide inputs into the design of the municipal Conversion Plans.
- 34 -

F. Sustainability and Risks
1. Sustainability:
Ownership: By design, the PSF is intricately woven into a well-established and extensive participatory
process in the health sector. In addition to the high priority bestowed by the MOH, the PSF has been
discussed and strongly endorsed by stakeholders through consultative mechanisms, including state,
municipal and local health councils (to secure client inputs) and the Intra-govemmental Management
Commissions (to secure inputs from federal, state, municipal health authorities and staff). Basic health
priorities and corresponding indicators that buoy the PSF resulted from a series of consultations with state,
municipal and local health councils during 2000, and published by the MOH (Portaria no. 393/GM) in
March, 2001. Also, one criterion for Conversion Plan approval is evidence of consultation with local
stakeholders and of close correspondence between Plan objectives and agreements made between
municipal authorities and health councils.
Financial: Recent financial reforms have facilitated stability of funding for the health sector. Current
legislation will result in increases in federal spending on health by 7 percent by 2001 and 2004, according
to analyses prepared during project preparation. Also, MOH has demonstrated strong and increasing
budgetary commitment to PSF, as described earlier. MOH budgetary allocations to PSF are projected to
increase by 10 percent annually between 2001 and 2004. The government's top priority in the health sector
is to extend PSF coverage to urban municipalities. Bank financial participation represents a fraction of PSF
financing. Finally, as PSF coverage expands, the MOH envisions the merging of separate transfers that
currently finance Basic Care and Family Health. The joining of these transfers will ensure PSF
sustainability.

G. Main Loan Conditions
1. Effectiveness Condition
1. Operational Manual adopted by the MOH, including financial and administration manual.
2. Project financial management arrangements satisfactory to IBRD are in place and functional,
including approval of terms of reference for financial audits.
Disbursement Conditions for Component 1
1. Signed Subproject Implementation Agreements between MOH and participating municipalities.
Agreements include technical and fiduciary performance indicators conditioning participation in the
second phase APL.
2. Regulations approved by MOH to finance Conversion Plan implementation, including operational
procedures and fiduciary requirements.
3. Bank would be authorized to request refunds of any ineligible expenditures financed as SOTs and
to suspend further disbursements for SOTs in the event that the audited financial statements
submitted yearly show any ineligible expenditures claimed under such SOTs.
2. Other [classify according to covenant types used in the Legal Agreements.]
The municipalities will comply with the procurement requirements of local law for minor goods, works and
services financed by SOTs and seek approval of changes to procurement plans. Misprocurement will
consist of a failure to award a contract to the lowest evaluated bidder, lack of records or information
pertaining to the bidding and award, the disqualification of lowest price without a sound reason, deviations
from the approved procurement plan without prior approval, and impeding audits or ex post reviews.
Failure to comply with any of these specific requirements will enable the Bank to declare misprocurment in
the amount of 30 percent of the contract found misprocured.

of project implementation.

quality.

all other applicable Bank policies.

The project contains three innovative fiduciary features that deviate from normal practice, but comply with
Bank policies:
(i) Extension of period (to one year) for which advances (disbursements) would continue to be made
before the eligibility of expenditures are reconciled through Project Monitoring Reports (PMRs);
(ii) Loan proceeds would finance 30 percent of the Subproject Pool instead of 30 percent of each
transaction;
(iii) Application of national rules for the procurement of small packages of goods and works, with
compliance assessed on an ex post basis.

Attachment H Annex I
Trigger Indicators For the Phase II of the Adaptable Lending Program (APL)
Phase I will support: (i) the implementation of PSF in urban municipalities with populations greater than 100,000;
(ii) strengthening and establishing of training institutions, medical and nurse specialty programs, residency
programs and corresponding curriculums that foster the family health model as a core element of professional and
specialty training; (iii) development of a regulatory framework for the accreditation of PSF/Basic Health Units and
PSF training institutions; (iv) building institutional capacity to plan, manage, monitor and evaluate basic service
provision, decentralization, and human resource development. Trigger indicators for Phase II are designed to
measure PSF coverage expansion, development of core regulations and support systems required for the successful
implementation of MOH policies and strategies, design and testing of monitoring and evaluation systems to
improve quality and performance of PSF provision. These indicators will be assessed as part of the monitoring and
evaluation system.

Annex 2: Detailed Project Description
BRAZIL: FAMILY HEALTH EXTENSION PROGRAM
APL Program Description
The Brazil Family Health Extension Project (FHEP), an operation under an Adaptable Program Lending
Instrument (APL) in three phases of three, two and two years, seeks to improve utilization and quality of
publicly-financed health services by:
(a) Expanding coverage of the government's Family Health Program (Programa de Sazude da Familia, or
PSF in about 100 large municipalities, establishing well-articulated referral and counter-referral
systems, and introducing performance-based management practices;
(b) Establishing family health as a core element of health professional and para-professional training.
(c) Strengthening MOH capacity to monitor and evaluate PSF health services, policies and training
activities on a systematic basis.
Background: During the last 15 years Brazil has implemented a series of health policy reforms that have
transformed the organization, financing and provision of health services. In the late 1 980s, Brazil converted
its federal public health financing system to a single national health fund. In the mid-1990s, it instituted a
per capita payment for primary care services that was distributed directly to municipalities. This simple
reform caused a vast improvement in the equity of the system - poor municipalities suddenly had funds for
primary health services on a scale they had not seen before. More recently, this capitation system was
enhanced by the Family Health Program, through which the federal government transferred additional
funds to municipalities that agreed to implement a proactive primary health care model. During the 1990s,
the Brazil Federal Borrower focused on assisting rural municipalities to implement these two programs. In
both cases, the programs provided additional funds to municipalities for earmarked purposes, and as long
as the money was spent for that purpose, the municipalities fulfilled their obligation.
Consonant with recent decentralization and health finance reforms, the federal government contributes to
financing PSF and basic care through two federal grant transfers to municipalities (see Attachment 1 for
summary description of the grant transfers):
*   Fixed, per capita payments (PABfixed) transferred to the municipalities to support basic care delivery
through the Basic Health Program (AB). The PABfixed is paid in equal monthly installments. Funds
are used to pay personnel (excluding family health teams) and purchase goods and services related to
the delivery of basic care. It is not performance-based. Nor is there performance monitoring.
*   Variable transfers (PABfam) to support the implementation of the Family Health Program (PSF)
The amounts transferred depend on populations coverage and the number of family health teams
established. The PABfam is also paid in monthly installments. This transfer is mainly used to pay for
personnel (community health agents, nurses, dental technicians and doctors) constituting the family
health teams. The transfer is slightly performance-based in the sense that it varies with the number of
health teams, a proxy for population coverage.
- 50 -



Municipalities are responsible for the organization and delivery of all publicly-funded primary care, which
is partially financed through the PABfixed federal grant transfer. Municipalities can opt to participate in
the PSF. The MOH provides additional financing through the PABfam to municipalities who elect to do so.
SF and basic care also benefit from buy-ins by participating states and municipalities. Although data on
municipal contributions specific to PSF are unavailable, municipalities contributed an estimated US$577
million to primary care services in 2000, representing about 30 percent of total spending on primary
services. In practice, municipalities use PABfixed financing to fund PSF implementation. However,
PSFfam funds can only be used to finance family health teams and inputs required by the teams.
Polico Reforms: With the support of the proposed APL, Brazil is ready to take the next step with these
policy reforms. This step involves: (a) expanding PSF coverage significantly by focusing on large urban
municipalities; (b) transforming basic care delivery to the outreach-oriented PSF approach; (c) changing
the character of the PSF through shifting from input- to performance-based financing, monitoring and
management through federal-municipal agreements; and (d) improving municipal capacity to organize and
manage health services while strengthening the overall fiduciary framework at the municipal level.
The Family Health Program: Initiated by the Health Ministry (Ministerio da Satide, or MOH) in 1994 as
a relatively small initiative, the PSF has successfully and rapidly expanded coverage of primary care in
rural and peri-urban areas, reaching about one-fifth of the Brazilian population (38 million) by 2000. In
that same year, there were 10,000 PSF teams working in over 3,000 municipalities in all of Brazil's states.
The PSF is now one of the largest federally-funded health programs, spending US$380 million. GOB
estimates federal spending on the PSF will reach US$410 million in 2001 and US$550 million by 2004.
The MOH aims to launch a new stage of PSF implementation to cover urban populations and strengthen
municipal health systems, using primary health care as an efficiency- and equity enhancing gateway into
the rest of the health care system. The proposed APL will support MOH efforts to expand PSF population
coverage while strengthening human resource, organizational and management infrastructure to support
effective and continued implementation of the Program. The proposed program will partially finance the
annual marginal increase in federal PSF financing, estimated at 10 percent annually during the seven-year
proposed program.
PSF Conversion: This policy reform concerns the conversion of the current primary care delivery system to
one based on PSF. PSF represents the platform on which the government seeks to restructure how
publicly-financed primary health care is organized and provided. To achieve this, the Borrower envisions a
transformed system at three levels: service provision, service organization and human resource
development. At the delivery level, the Government seeks to convert the current, passive-provider,
facility-based delivery system to an active-provider, outreach model in which family health care providers
deliver quality basic health care to households and communities. This model of service provision aims to be
more efficient by focusing on prevention and early treatment, and using PSF as the gateway to higher-level
services. At the organizational level and in support of the PSF delivery model and decentralization policies,
the Government aims to introduce performance-based management practices, make monitoring and
evaluation a core system feature, and establish formal relationships among primary, secondary and tertiary
care providers that now operate in an uncoordinated and nonhierarchical fashion. Finally, the Government
strives to implement its vision of a network of high quality in-service and pre-service training programs that
reflect the concepts and state-of-the-art practices of the family health discipline, while producing the human
resources required to expand and improve PSF services. In addition to yielding the direct benefits through
the "downstream" expansion and improvement of health services of the PSF model, the proposed program
will facilitate the long-term sustainability of the PSF through strengthening "upstream" institutional
- 51 -



capacity to manage, monitor and evaluate performance, and developing human resource capabilities and
commitment for family health.
Performance-based Financing: Conversion represents much more than a bridge between the traditional
and PSF delivery models. It also constitutes a bridge between input- and performance-based financing.
Through the introduction of a new transfer mechanism to finance implementation of the conversion process,
PABconv, to support implementation of the Conversion process, MOH will apply regulations and
performance agreements that will link financing to achievement of performance benchmarks. Compliance
with benchmarks will "trigger" municipal participation in each phase of the APL, and continued receipt of
the PABconv transfer. As described in Annex 2F, performance indicators would aim to monitor progress in
the transformation of the primary health care system to the PSF model, including the establishment of
functional referral systems, introduction of performance-based management systems. Consonant with the
pooled-financing approach, performance indicators would also focus on setting of standards for acceptable
fiduciary practice at the municipal level. As PSF approaches full coverage (during the next 5-7 years) the
MOH envisions the merging of the heretofore separate federal grants transfers that currently finance the
Basic Health Care Program (PABfixed) under the traditional model and the Family Health Care Program
(PABfam) under the new model. Building upon the implementation experience of the PABconv that will be
introduced in the proposed project, performance-based instruments would be incorporated into rules
governing future federal transfers for primary care upon the merger of the heretofore transfers supporting
the Basic Health Care and Family Health Programs. This measure will prevent the continuation of parallel
systems with separate funding mechanisms, and therefore ensure PSF's sustainability.
Decentralization: The fourth reform entails decentralization of management responsibilities to the
municipalities for the implementation of primary care services and activities. In accordance with recent
regulations, NOAS 01/01, MOH seeks to complete the transfer of full management responsibility to the
municipalities for the delivery of primary care. Decentralization of management responsibilities began in
the early 1990s. Certification for "full management of basic care" entails installing: (i) the necessary
capacity to technically plan, implement, supervise and monitor primary care services; and (ii) fiduciary
arrangements and support systems such as auditing, accounting and information management to ensure
proper use of funds.  The program will support decentralization through strengthening municipal
management capacity, introducing performance-based management systems, and establishing solid
monitoring and evaluation capabilities.
Pooling Feature: For Component 1 Bank financing will be pooled with federal grant transfers for three
MOH programs: Basic Health Care (AB), Family Health (PSF) and PSF Conversion. Both AB and PSF
are large, on-going government programs financed through separate federal grants transferred to
municipalities which are responsible for service delivery. Under existing Borrower arrangements, transfers
for the AB and PSF programs flow through a National Health Fund, maintained by the MOH, to a common
bank account at each municipality. These grants essentially finance a similar set activities and services but
under different models. The grant for AB finances services delivery under the existing, traditional model
while the PSF grant supports services provision under a proactive, outreach model. The Borrower will also
create a new grant to finance the PSF Conversion Program, or specifically, the transitional costs of
AB-to-PSF conversion. The new grant will also be transferred through the National Health Fund to the
municipalities.
To enable Bank's financial support of the PSF through the existing federal-to-municipal grant system, the
Borrower requested that the proposed project incorporate a special pooling arrangement together with
innovative fiduciary features. Under Component 1, Family Health Municipal Conversion and Expansion,
federal grant transfers under the existing AB/PSF programs are the pool of funds, the Programmatic Pool,
- 52 -



from which the smaller Conversion Subproject Pool would be derived. Through this Subproject Pool the
proposed Project would co-finance implementation of Conversion Subprojects. Conversion Subprojects
would consist of a set of pre-identified activities necessary to extend Family Health service delivery and
convert the health care model of the Basic Care Program to the model of the Family Health Program. Loan
procees would finance 30 percent of the cost of goods, works and services required to implement
Conversion Subprojects in selected municipalities. Expenditures would incur against pre-defined and jointly
agreed subproject implementation arrangements and procurement plans specified in federal-municipal
contracts. Bank financing would be attributed to pre-defined, eligible expenditures of the Subproject Pool
and therefore distinguishing them from the larger, AB/PSF Programmatic Pool financed solely by the
Borrower.
Within the Subproject Pool, loan proceeds will be distributed as advances for expenditures to be incurred at
a rate of 30 percent of the grants transferred by the Borrower to pre-qualified municipalities to cover the
cost of goods, works and services of implementing Conversion Subprojects. Transfers to the municipalities
will be made according to an agreed timeline in accordance with the procurement plans for the Conversion
Subprojects.  Details regarding both the total amount of the transfers as well as the 30 percent
reimbursement claimed will be recorded on a Statement of Transfer (SOT), specifying projected eligible
expenditures of the Bank's share of financing of the Conversion Subprojects.
Under the proposed program, the idea is for each municipality to enter into a two-year agreement with the
federal government to implement Conversion Subprojects to convert its primary health care system to the
Family Health model, backed up by a procurement plan that is updated annually. The overall disbursement
schedule for Component 1 will be based on Conversion Subproject procurement plans, agreed with the
Bank. Through the procurement plan, the Bank will have a good idea of eligible expenditures prior to
making disbursements into the pool that will finance the plan. Although the grants that finance each
municipal subproject will consist of eligible and ineligible expenditures, at least 30 percent of pooled
expenditures will be for eligible items. Preliminary projections suggest that the Subproject Pool will contain
a higher percentage of eligible expenditures than the Bank's 30 percent share.
To satisfy the Borrower's request regarding Bank participation in the above-described municipal financing
system, the proposed project will apply three innovative fiduciary features:
(i) Extension of period (to one year) for which advances (disbursements) would continue to be made
before the eligibility of expenditures are reconciled through Project Monitoring Reports (PMRs);
(ii) Loan proceeds would finance 30 percent of the Subproject Pool instead of 30 percent of each
transaction, as is normal practice;
(iii) Application of national rules for the procurement of small packages of goods, works and services,
with compliance assessed on an ex post basis.
Although Project Monitoring Reports (PMRs) will be submitted quarterly by the municipalities and
reviewed by the Bank for monitoring purposes, full reconciliation would occur annually. As part of the
annual financial audit, the auditor would be required to express an opinion on a reconciliation statement
summarizing all expenditures incurred by the municipalities in comparison with advances made during the
year, categorized by eligibility and including specification of the 30 percent attributed to the Bank. Given
the nature of the pooling arrangement, the annual audit would examine the much larger AB/PSF
Programmatic Pool as needed to fully assess the Subproject Pool financing Conversion Subprojects. The
ex-ante procurement plans and a uniquely assigned chart of account code would specify those activities and
corresponding goods, works and services required for implementation of the Conversion Subprojects, thus
facilitating the attribution process. If the reconciliation statement shows ineligible expenditures or that
- 53 -



eligible expenditures did not reach 30 percent of the amounts transferred during the year, the Bank would
request a refund or refrain from authorizing future disbursements against SOTs.
The Subproject Pool will mainly finance goods, works and services procured according to national
legislation It will also finance ICB goods and QCBS consultants, but following Bank Procurement
Guidelines, and not as advances. In this case, disbursement will follow traditional investment lending
procedures in which supporting documentation such as contracts and invoices will be required to support
withdrawal applications. Special procurement remedies would also apply to the Subproject Pool. Based on
the results of an annual procurement review, the Bank would be authorized to declare misprocurement and
seek refunds for 30 percent of any misprocured contract in the Subproject Pool, and financed through an
advance.
The borrower's existing financial management and procurement rules and systems have been assessed and
deemed sufficiently robust to govern the use of Bank funds included in the pool (see Annex 6).
Nevertheless, during the life of the proposed APL the Bank will also work with the federal government to
supervise and strengthen municipal fiduciary processes. This means that a number of fiduciary oversight
and control measures (such as reporting requirements, record keeping, audits and remedies) will be applied
to the much larger AB/PSF Programmatic Pool of federal grant resources transferred to the municipalities,
and thereby increasing the Project's development impact. The following are brief descriptions of
arrangements agreed with the Borrower to mitigate the potential risks of pooling include: (see Annexes 6
and 2G for details):
(i)  Federal assessment and certification of financial management and procurement capacity of each
participating municipality as a condition for Bank financing; based on risk assessment results,
municipalities will agree to an action plan to improve fiduciary practices;
(ii)  Outputs and budget (with corresponding procurement plans and disbursement schedule) agreed
on an annual basis for each municipality prior to funding; a uniquely assigned chart of account
codes would specify those activities and corresponding goods, works and services required for
implementation of Conversion Subprojects;
(iii) Quarterly submission of Project Monitoring Reports (PMRs) by participating municipalities and
reviewed by Bank for monitoring purposes; full reconciliation would occur annually as part of
financial audit;
(iv) Participating municipalities would be required to meet fiduciary performance benchmarks
(together with health performance benchmarks); failure to reach performance targets would
affect their future eligibility for participation in the Program;
(v)  Subproject Pool would finance ICB goods and QCBS consultants above defined thresholds
established according to results of the risk assessment; but procurement would follow Bank
guidelines and disbursements would follow traditional investment lending procedures;
(vi) Annual technical and fiduciary reviews carried out by the Borrower; and post-reviews carried out
by the Bank; and
(vii) Specific remedies would be applied annually for misprocurement, financial mismanagement and
ineligible expenditures.
The pooling feature offers a number of advantages to both the Borrower and the Bank. For the Borrower,
the feature permits the Bank to mingle its funds with established grant transfers to facilitate the smooth
flow of funds to a large number of widely-dispersed, and diverse municipalities. It also creates a single
reporting system for the grants rather than parallel and redundant systems for Borrower and Bank
purposes. Bank assistance is refocused to strengthen the fiduciary framework for the MOH programs
- 54 -



which will contribute to improved health services management. Finally, the feature reorients the partnership
between the Borrower and Bank, permitting the Bank to assists the MOH in to realigning federal-municipal
relations to focus on performance and results.
For the Bank, pooling of funds affords the opportunity to: (i) form a long-term partnership with the
Borrower in the health sector since grant transfers are the only method used by the Borrower to finance
primary health care; (ii) participate in the most important reforms of the sector, and (iii) enhance
developmental impact by shifting Bank supervision from tracking transactions financed through loan
proceeds to oversight and improvement of the Borrower's fiduciary systems.
APL Phases: Table 1 presents summary data on the APL phases while Table 2 shows the dimensions of
each Component by APL phase. Trigger indicators for APL Phase II are described in Attachment 2 to
Annex 1.
Phase I. Initiating Conversion to PSF Model in Urban Municipalities, and Strengthening  Training
Programs and Monitoring and Evaluation Systems. The first, three-year phase will extend the PSF to
large urban municipalities, expand training opportunities for PSF team members, and develop core systems
to enhance quality and performance of PSF provision. Expected results include: (i) agreements approved
and under implementation in about 40 urban municipalities with greater than 100,000 residents to convert
primary care organization and delivery to the family health approach, increasing average PSF population
coverage from about 10 to 35 percent; (ii) establishment of a federal transfer mechanism linking financing
to performance in support of PSF conversion in participating municipalities; (iii) pre-service and in-service
training of about 14,000 family health professionals and para-professionals; (iv) development of regulatory
frameworks for the accreditation of PSF/Basic Health Units and PSF training institutions, and agreement
reached with the Borrower on the Phase II implementation plan; (v) PSF protocols for referrals and
counter-referrals and care practices developed and implemented for 50% for PSF teams in participating
municipalities; (vi) performance-based management system in support of PSF developed and tested in all
participating municipalities; (vii) monitoring and evaluation system for the Matemal-Child Health (MCH)
referral / counter-referral network approved, developed and tested in a sample of municipalities; (viii)
baseline data (household and providers) collected and analyzed for a representative sample of participating
urban municipalities, and strategy agreed with the Borrower on incorporating indicators into the monitoring
system and evaluation plan in Phase II; (ix) evaluation completed of about 50% of existing PSF teams and
15 training centers, and agreement reached with the Borrower on how results will be applied in Phase II;
and (x) monitoring and evaluation system for municipal AB/PSF approved and developed, and agreement
reached with Borrower for implementation in Phase II. Trigger indicators for Phase II are described in
Attachment 2 to Annex 1.
Phase II. Expanding PSF Coverage and Training and Impact Evaluation. The second, two-year phase will
support the roll-out of the PSF in up to 60 additional urban municipalities, strengthening the quality of
institutions providing PSF-related training, and implementing systems to improve quality and performance
of PSF provision. Expected results include: (i) agreements approved and under implementation in an
additional 30 urban municipalities with greater than 100,000 residents to convert primary care
organization and delivery to the family health approach, increasing average population coverage from
about 10 to 35 percent; (ii) continued implementation of the PSF program in the 40 municipalities
supported under Phase I, increasing average population coverage within them to 50 percent; (iii)
application of the PSF accreditation system to a representative sample of participating municipalities with
PSF teams and to half of all PSF training centers; (iv) referral system operational for all PSF teams in
participating municipalities; (v) monitoring and evaluation system for the MCH referral / counter-referral
- 55 -



network implemented in all participating municipalities; and (vi) performance-based management systems
in support of PSF developed and implemented in about 50% of participating municipalities.
Phase III. Reaching Municipal Coverage and Training Targets, and Transforming Evaluation into a
Permanent Process. The final two-year phase will support the consolidation of the PSF expansion and
conversion in urban municipalities, produce PSF trainees on a systematic basis, and institutionalize
support systems for quality and performance. Expected results include: (i) continued implementation of the
program in the approximately 100 urban municipalities supported under previous phases, increasing
average population coverage within them to 60 percent; (ii) monitoring and evaluation system expanded to
entire referral / counter-referral network (not just MCH) in all municipalities; (iii) impact evaluation of
PSF expansion and model conversion in municipalities supported in Phases I and II; and (iv)
performance-based management and monitoring and evaluation institutionalized.

Project Description: APL Phase 1
The project's development objectives during Phase I are:
(a) Increase coverage of basic health service especially among population with limited access to all
utilization of health care services in urban centers with 200,000 or more inhabitants, through
expansion of the PSF, development of a referral and counter-referral system, and improvement in
the management and organization of basic health services; and
- 56 -



(b) Improve the quality of family health service provision through developing and strengthening
in-service and pre-service training of human resources in the PSF model;
(c) Improve the performance and effectiveness of basic health care services through strengthening
monitoring and evaluation, information management and accreditation systems.
Coverage extension activities will focus on large urban areas, which are characterized by poorly integrated,
traditional service delivery systems and large numbers of families living in poverty and confronting severe
social risk. Of the 222 municipalities with populations of 100,000 or greater in the country, approximately
40 will participate in the Phase I project (and about 100 will participate by the end of the seven-year
program). The strategic aim is to raise PSF population coverage from about 10 to 35 percent within these
municipalities. Support for decentralization reforms and management improvements will also focus on
urban municipalities. Quality enhancement activities related to training, monitoring and evaluation,
information management and accreditation will yield nationwide benefits. Table 3 presents estimated
project costs by component.

Project Component 1 - US$86.90 million
Project Components (First Phase - 2002 -2005)
Obiective. This component will support the extension of basic care services through the expansion of the
population covered under the PSF model in about 40 municipalities with populations of over 100,000
inhabitants. This will be achieved through the implementation of Basic and Family Health Conversion
Subprojects, or Conversion Subprojects, to convert the organization and provision of basic health services
from a traditional model to the prevention, outreach- and performance-oriented Family Health Model;
strengthen the decentralization regime, following the NOAS/01 regulatory framework; and the introduce a
performance - based transfer mechanism.
In 2001, PSF covers an estimated 10 percent of the population in the 223 municipalities with over 100,000
inhabitants (See Tables 4 and below). Of the 30 municipalities with over 500,000 residents, PSF coverage
reaches only 7 percent in nearly half. Federal financing of AB (through PABfixed) and PSF (through
PABfam) in these 223 municipalities reached $416 and $48 million respectively in 2000. Total primary
care spending, including municipal contributions was approximately $536 million in 2000.
