The specific objectives of the component are: (a) expand access to and utilization of basic health services
by populations at risk in urban areas, which currently are underserved, through the implementation of the
PSF model and upgrading of the service network; (b) establish functional referral and counter-referral
systems through establishment of integrated service networks; (c) strengthen the system of PSF
management and organization; and (d) improve the performance of the municipal providers of basic health
services through development of perfornance-based organizational and management models, and the
introduction of new health care practices.
Expected Results: It is expected that as a result of the activities under the first phase of the APL, the
following improvements will be achieved in participating municipalities (among others, as shown in the
Logical Framework, Annex 1):
*   Average PSF population coverage in participating municipalities increased from 10 to 35 percent 70%
of pregnant women attended by FH teams have had 6 prenatal visits and all the basic lab exams
recommended by the PNC protocol.
*   100 % of pregnant women attended by FH teams received a tetanus vaccine.
*   40 % of maternity admissions from PSF catchment areas are referred by PSF teams.
*   Performance-based management and supervision system  developed and piloted in 20%   of
municipalities to support PSF implementation.
*   Regulatory framework for performance-based transfer mechanism for financing municipal
*   About 40 Conversion Subprojects approved; transfers under implementation for participating
municipalities;
- 58 -



Implementation and Financing Arrangements: Municipalities seeking support under the project will
submit a Conversion Plan (Plano de Conversdo) as well as related Basic Care and Family Health activities
that, taken together, will constitute the Basic and Family Health Conversion Subprojects, or Conversion
Subprojects. Criteria for eligibility and selection of participating municipalities as well as guidelines for the
preparation of the Conversion Plans will be established by the Ministry of Health (MOH), and included in
the project's Operational Manual, (Annex 2G presents summary eligibility criteria, and outlines the
selection process). In addition to health-related criteria, eligible municipalities must meet financial
management and procurement requirements per risk classification methodology described in Annex 6. The
Conversion Plan guidelines will draw on PSF strategies, institutional requirements stipulated in recently
approved regulations (NOAS 01/01 -- regarding formation of networks and organization and management
of health services) and analyses of successful experiences to transforming traditional primary care to the
PSF model.
The Conversion Subprojects will form the basis for Subproject Implementation Agreements signed between
the selected municipalities and the Ministry of Health, which will set forth the respective obligations
regarding subproject implementation, including: (i) broad description of a seven-year conversion program
including global end-of-program performance targets; (ii) detailed description of Conversion Subproject
activities to be implemented in the first three-year phase; (iii) technical and fiduciary performance targets
that will determine eligibility for participation subsequent APL phases (Annex 2F presents the performance
indicators required for participation in Phase 2 of the APL); (iv) procurement plans and corresponding
disbursement schedule for subproject activities, specifying eligible expenditures; (v) obligations regarding
reporting, recording keeping, maintenance of accounts, and auditing; and (vi) terms for cancellation of
disbursements and/or the Agreement. The Conversion Subproject plans will be evaluated by a Technical
Committee organized by the MOH. In addition, the approval and commitment of relevant state and
municipal authorities is required.
Bank financing will be pooled with federal transfers used by the Federal Government to finance health
program implementation: Family Health(PABfam), Basic Care (PABfixed) and Conversion Plans
(PABconv). From a technical perspective, these three transfers essentially finance a similar set of activities
related to primary health care. PABfixed finances basic care under the existing model while PABfam
finances basic care under the family health model. PABconv will finance the transitional costs of converting
primary care delivery from the existing model to the family health-based approach.
Based on the procurement plans of the Conversion Subprojects, a virtual Subproject Pool will be
dimensioned within the larger AB/PSF Programmatic Pool of transfers. The Subproject Pool will exist only
for selected municipalities which will be responsible for implementing the Conversion Subprojects. The
Subproject Pool would be spent for activities constituting the Conversion Subprojects and related to
Conversion Plan implementation, PSF extension and Basic Care provision. Loan proceeds will fnance 30
percent of the subproject pool for eligible small works, equipment, goods, consultant services, training and
operating costs.
Given the solidity of the borrower's fiduciary framework and the agreed risk mitigation measures, as
described in Annex 6, the Bank has agreed to disburse into the Borrower's grant transfer system, advancing
funds against Statements of Transfers. The Conversion Subproject procurement plans will be the basis for
establishing the subproject disbursement schedule for Bank disbursements into the grant transfer system.
Based on expenditure data supplied by participating municipalities, the MOH will submit quarterly
financial management reports (PMRs) to tract and compare reported eligible expenditures with SOT-based
- 59-



expenditures. Full reconciliation (based on PMRs) will be occur annually, and if necessary, agreed
remedies will be applied for misprocurement, mismanagement and ineligible expenditures.
The Borrower will create a new transfer, PABconv, to finance the implementation of the Conversion Plans.
The main reason for creating this transfer is to introduce a performance-based financing mechanism and to
facilitate MOH technical and financial monitoring of conversion plan implementation by the participating
municipalities. Unlike other PAB transfers, PABconv will consist of the following characteristics that will
be specified in MOH regulations that will be approved prior to effectiveness:
* Temporary mechanism that will tenninate upon completion of conversion plans;
* Disbursements will be made according to timeline established by procurement plan included in the
conversion plan (rather than automatic monthly transfers as is the case for the other transfers);
* Compliance with agreed indicators of technical and fiduciary performance (see Annex 2F) will be
condition for continued participation;
Component Content: Once approved by the MOH, Conversion Subprojects will be implemented by the
selected municipalities according to the procurement plans and disbursement schedules there in. Activities
are grouped into two subcomponents. All activities will be financed through the subproject funding pool..
A. Family Health Extension and Basic Care Provision
Objective. This subcomponent aims to raise PSF population coverage from an average of 10 percent to 35
percent of the population in municipalities with more than 100,000 inhabitants. It will support the
expansion of PSF population coverage and the delivery of basic care in participating urban municipalities
through financing consultant fees and incremental operating costs involved in the organization, supervision
and provision of family health and basic care services, as specified in the Operational Manual. Annex 2A
describes these activities and services.
B. PSF Conversion: This subcomponent consists of four groups of activities:
(i) Institutional Modernization
Objective. This activity seeks to strengthen planning, management, organization, and regulation of basic
health services at the municipal level, in support of PSF expansion. To obtain these results, the pooled
funds will finance the provision of consulting services, training, workshops and seminars, and training
materials.
Main Activities. Depending on the needs identified at the municipal level and expressed in the Conversion
Plan, activities under this subcomponent would include:
*   Development and implementation of: (a) performance-based management systems; (b) instruments,
policies and regulations for contracting and human resources management; and (c) evaluations of user
satisfaction and community participation. All of these will be developed and implemented within the
guidelines of the NOAS/0 1 and the PSF model.
* Preparation of municipal investment plans and projects, to be financed in part under Subcomponent B.
* Development and implementation of matemal and child referral / counter-referral systems, resulting in
participation by 50% of PSF teams in an integrated referral network.
- 60 -



*   Development, implementation and dissemination of new health care practices and protocols.
*   Special studies to support implementation of the PSF and NOAS/01.
(ii) Upgrading of Municipal Service Provision
Objective. This set of activities is intended to improve the ability of municipal health services to meet
beneficiaries' health needs through strategic improvements in primary care provision. It will support
physical improvements to increase the capacity of municipal primary care service networks. In some
municipalities, the networks will be organized and funded under the project; in others, in which networks
already exist, the project will finance inputs to reinforce and improve their functioning. The pooled funds
will finance medical equipment, civil works, non-medical and office equipment, furniture, vehicles and
operating costs.
Main Activities. Depending on the needs identified at the municipal level and expressed in the Conversion
Plan, activities will entail the upgrading and equipping of physical infrastructure for Family Health Team
to support their tasks. This can include minor civil works (remodeling) to health centers and polyclinics and
the procurement of medical and non-medical equipment (including basic communications equipment),
furniture and vehicles. Transportation vehicles will be financed both for traditional purposes, and to
support the implementation of a new model of integrated and outreach-oriented health care services. This
means, for example, that vehicles such as fully-equipped ambulances would be obtained to respond to
urgent health problems. In addition, appropriate vehicles would be acquired for the transport of biological
materials to diagnostic laboratories within micro regions; distribution of sterilized instruments and other
clinical equipment from a central location to basic health units; movement of health team members to
communities for house-to-house and group outreach activities; and scheduled transport of users (patients)
to health promotion events and activities. It is expected that among the vehicles purchased would include
fully equipped ambulances, "kombis," omnibuses, vans and others.
(iii) Strengthening of Information Systems
Objective. Activities are intended to support incorporation and use of information technologies for
planning, monitoring and evaluating of health system organization and performance. The pooled funds will
finance consultancy services, computer equipment, and software, training, workshops and training
materials.
Main Activities. Depending on the needs identified at the municipal level and expressed in the Conversion
Plan, activities would include:
*   Development and upgrading of information systems to manage data related to the Family Health
Team work process and results. Analysis of such data will support performance-based management,
and referral and counter-referral systems.
*   Development of an information system to support various levels of the health services network,
including (but not restricted to) the organization of clinical consultations, inpatient stays, and
laboratory exams.
- 61 -



It is expected that during the first phase of the APL, information systems for the management of referral
and counter-referral arrangements will be established or strengthened in about 30 percent of the
participating municipalities. In addition, information systems to support monitoring and evaluation of
Conversion Plans will be developed and tested in a sample of municipalities.
(iv) Training and Supervision
Objective. The aim of this set of activities is to facilitate the transformation of traditional provider teams
and the inmmediate incorporation of these teams into new Family Health teams by providing training in
strategic areas, along with direct, routine on-the-job technical assistance during the period of conversion of
the model of care. It will also support the creation of supervisory systems in support of PSF expansion. It
is important to note that the activities under this line of financing will be undertaken only in the
approximately 100 participating municipalities. Training activities financed under Component 2 are
expected to yield longer-term and more profound changes in the training of human resources as well as
nationwide benefits. The pooled funds will finance consultancy services, training, workshops, seminars and
training materials.
Main Activities. Depending on the needs identified at the municipal level, as expressed in the Conversion
Plan, activities under this line of financing could include:
*   Development and implementation of training courses for municipal health workers in strategic areas,
including the PSF work processes, priority programs for the Sistema Unico de Sauide (SUS), and
management and direction of PSF activities.
*   Formation of interdisciplinary groups for technical supervision - called Supervisory Reference
Groups (Grupos de Referencia). It is expected that there will be one Reference Supervisory Group
will be constituted for every six Family Health Teams.
Project Component 2 - US$37.40 million
PSF Human Resource Development (US$ 37.4 million)
Objective. This component will support the implementation of a human resource policy that contributes to
the expansion of the PSF model; strengthens the qualifications and performance of family health
professionals and PSF Teams; and introduces new contents and methods in the preparation of health
professionals.
The objectives of the component are: (a) increase the number and quality of PSF human resources; (b)
expand and strengthen pre-service and in-service training for PSF human resources; (c) expand and
strengthen programs for professional training, including residencies in family health, specialty training for
physicians, nurses and dental hygiene assistants; and (d) introduce special programs (such as training
center consortiums and virtual networks to exchange information) and demonstration projects that support
and strengthen the human resource development activities.
By the conclusion of the first phase of the APL, it is expected that the following improvements will be
achieved (among others, as shown in the Logical Framework, Annex 1):
*   3,000 professionals trained in health services planning, management and organization.
*   In-service and continuing education provided to 10,000 PSF physicians, nurses and community
health agents.
- 62 -



*   500 PSF nurse and physician specialists graduated.
*   500 dental technicians graduated.
*   100 Family Health physicians enrolled in residency programs.
*   Alternative curricula and teaching methodologies for Family Health established in 10 medical and
nursing schools.
*   15 new in-service training centers (Polos de Capacitacion) created.
Implementation. This component will have a national focus and be managed through the Project
Coordination Unit (PCU). Many of the activities financed under Component 2 will be implemented through
the P6los de Capacita,do, training centers that offer courses and training modules directed at professionals
on the Family Health Team.    In addition, there are approximately 100 institutions of higher
learning-universities, faculties and medical and nursing schools-which together with the State and
Municipal Health Secretariats will be able to participate in the Program.  Activities for the
para-professional members of the team, particularly in the case of dental technicians, will be implemented
through Health Technical Schools (Escolas Tecnicas de Satide), which are linked to the State and
Municipal Health Secretaries. Existing contracts with training institutions will be supported under this
subcomponent, as well as new contracts, selected on a competitive basis. Activities are grouped into three
subcomponents:
Subcomponent A. In-service Training of PSF Professionals and Para-Professionals
Objective and Expenditure Categories. This subcomponent has the objective to strengthen PSF service
organization and provision by providing in-service training for several types of health professionals in the
Family Health model, including service managers, health professionals (doctors and nurses) and
para-professionals (community health agents).  Various types of training-on-site, distance and
others-would be supported.. The project would support the development and testing of innovative
programs for continuing education of PSF teams, such as "virtual tutors." PSF teams participating in the
program will have on-line access to professionals who will provide rapid technical support and backup on
specific clinical and case management issues. Expenditure categories includes training (consultancy
services, training and workshops) and training materials and publications.
Main Activities.  This subcomponent will be executed on a national basis through training centers,
including P6los, which will enter into contracts with the MOH. The subcomponents will finance the
development and/or strengthening of training and continuing education programs for PSF professionals, in
the following thematic areas:
*   Planning, organization and management of basic health services.
*   Clinical practices (such as urgent and emergency care; care protocols; respectful care during delivery;
child, women's and adult health; dental health; integrated family health, and clinical management
(such as case management and continuity of care).
*   Public health (such as epidemiological surveillance; health education and promotion; care for
individuals facing violence).
*   Development of national strategies, plans, guidelines, manual and training modules for medical waste
management for primary care facilities. Testing of manual and training modules in a sample of
municipalities.
- 63 -



Subcomponent B. Pre-service and Specialty Trainina of PSF Professionals
Objective. This subcomponent has the objective of providing pre-service training to professionals in areas
relevant to the organization and provision of services under the PSF model. The expenditure categories to
be financed are: consultancy services, scholarships, internships, training, workshops, conferences,
seminars, materials and publications.
Main Activities. Training programs will be carried out by a different institutions - primarily universities
(medical and nursing schools), technical schools (for community health workers and dental hygiene
assistants), and Municipal Secretariats - that enter into agreements and/or contracts with the MOH. In the
case of Municipal Secretariats, they may also enter into third-party contracts. The development of didactic
materials and methods, curricula, norms and so forth will be completed through consultancies. This
subcomponent also will support the expansion of the PITS Program (Programa de Incentivo a
Interiorizacao do Trabalho em Saude), which seeks to increase the volume and quality of physicians and
nurses specialized in the family health discipline where there is a shortage of qualified personnel -- usually
in municipalities with fewer than 50,000 residents. Approximately 200 municipalities have been targeted
for PITS expansion. The main activities will include development and implementation of pre-service
training programs in following areas:
*   Management of the organization, processes, work and profession of interventions foreseen by the
model.
*   Specialized medical and nursing training in Family Health, including the PITS Program.
*   Residencies (multi-professional, post-graduate level) dedicated to Family Health.
* Development of skills required by community health agents and dental hygiene assistants.
Subcomponent C. Demonstration Activities To Support Training and Education
Objective and Expenditure Categories. This subcomponent has the objective of improving the quality and
quantity of training opportunities for current and future members of the Family Health Teams. This will be
accomplished through testing innovative programs that support the development of curricula, teaching
methods and learning experiences conducted by training centers, technical schools and medical and nursing
schools. The subcomponent will finance consultancy services, audio-visual equipment, training, workshops,
conferences and training materials.
Main Activities. The demonstration projects described below will be executed by schools of medicine and
nursing, under contracts or agreements (convenios) with the MOH. The design of pilot activities, curricula,
pedagogic methodologies and other activities will be completed under contracts with consulting firms
and/or individual consultants. The main activities include:
*   Creation and development of management capacity for a network of training institutions (P6los).
*   The network will include a main training institution in a geographic area, which will provide technical
assistance, training materials, training-of-trainers courses and other services to smaller training
institutions in the same geographic area network.
*   Creation of approximately 10 demonstration projects in medical and nursing schools to develop and
implement new or improved curricula in Family Health, and/or innovative pedagogic methodologies to
support Family Health teams.
- 64 -



Project Component 3 - US$ 7.40 million
Monitoring and Evaluation (US$ 7.4 million)
Objective. Under this component, rigorous and coordinated monitoring and evaluation methodologies will
be employed to assess the structure, processes, outputs and impact of the Family Health Program. This
will entail an effort to build on, improve and integrate several existing information systems in the health
sector. In addition, evaluation methodologies, instruments, strategies and plans developed under other
FHEP components will be applied. The primary objectives of the component are: (a) create systems for
permanent, cost-effective monitoring and evaluation of the PSF and the training institutions' performance;
and (b) develop and implement systems of accreditation of basic Family Health units and PSF training
centers.
Implementation. The project will provide a central source of financing for four subcomponents. Activities
will be conducted on a national basis, and will be managed through the Project Coordinating Unit (PCU)
within the MOH. As necessary, the MOH may enter into subcontracts with universities and/or technical
institutions for management of research.
At the conclusion of the first phase of the APL, it is expected that the achievements under this component
will include (among others, as shown in the Logical Framework, Annex 1):
*  Baseline data (household and provider) collected and analyzed in all participating municipalities of
Component 1.
*  Regulatory framework for accreditation of PSF teams/primary care facilities and Polos approved by
MOH.
*  Evaluation of 50% of PSF teams implemented between 1997 and 2001 completed, and results
disseminated in 5 workshops.
*  Evaluation of 15 PSF training centers (P6los de Capacitacion) established between 1997 and 2001
completed, and results disseminated in 2 workshops.
* Development of methods, instruments and plans to evaluate training activities in terms of skill
acquisition and application.
*  Monitoring and evaluation system for the municipal AB/PSF, including local feedback mechanism,
approved and developed.
Subcomponent A. Stren2thenin2 of Monitorinu Systems
Objective.  The objective of this subcomponent is to consolidate and standardize the instruments and
systems for monitoring basic health care provision. Financing will support consultancy services, training,
computer equipment and software, fumiture , study tours, workshops and training materials.
Main Activities. The activities under this subcomponent will be executed and managed by the MOH's
PCU. They include:
* Strengthening of the monitoring systems for the PSF and for gauging compliance with
MOH-state-municipal agreements, known as the Pacto de Indicadores
* Incorporation of PSF team performance measures into municipal monitoring systems.
- 65 -



*  Creation, expansion and strengthening of approximately 5 Regional Collaborating Centers (Centros
Regionales de Colaboracdo para o Monitoramento da Atencdo Bdsica), which will provide technical
support for state and municipal authorities responsible for collecting and synthesizing health system
data, and for overseeing the performance of the PSF.
*  Incorporation of new information technologies in support of PSF monitoring.
*  Development of integrated data systems to support the performance, monitoring and evaluation of the
PSF, including the reformulation of the existing SLAB. This will rationalize and streamline the SLAB
so that it can serve 'as a coordinated and integrated system for monitoring and evaluating the
performance of the PSF. This will also ensure the compatibility of the SLAB with other existing data
systems, including.
Subcomponent B. Evaluation of PSF ImDlementation
Objective and Expenditure Categories. The objective of this subcomponent is to systematically evaluate
the PSF and training activities with respect to process, outcomes and impact. It would also develop
methodologies for evaluation of training institutions and activities, developed under Component 2(C). The
results of this work will yield operationally useful insights to guide the strategies of the MOH, as well as
State and Municipal Secretariats. Financing will support consultancy services, training, computer
equipment and software, furniture, study tours, workshops and materials.
Main Activities. The activities under this subcomponent will be executed and managed by the MOH's
PCU. They will include:
*  Evaluation of the process of implementing the Family Health Teams (described under Component 1)
and the related changes in the training institutions (described under Component 2) to identify "best
practices" as well as constraints within the work structure and processes.
*  Evaluation of samples of Family Health Teams and training centers (polos) established between 1997
and 2001 to assess their performance, cost-effectiveness, benefits and impact on the utilization of
services and health status of the beneficiary population.
*  Development and application of baseline household and provider surveys for all municipalities
participating in Component 1.
*  Development of evaluation methodologies, instruments and plans to measure: (a) the acquisition and
application of skills by individuals trained in Family Health; and (b) the impact of the development of
the networks of training institutions, demonstration projects, and curricular and pedagogic
innovations.
*  Impact evaluation of the pre-service and specialty training programs, residency programs and
curriculum development activities (already under implementation) to assess their performance,
efficiency and impact on trainees' level and application of skills.
Subcomponent C. Accreditation of the Family Health Teams. Facilities and Training Institutions
Objective. The objective of this subcomponent is to develop and implement accreditation systems for (a)
Family Health Program units, which deliver basic health services to a defined population under contract
with the government health authorities; and (b) training centers (polos de capacitacion), which deliver
in-service training and continuing education services for Family Health Team members, under contract with
government health authorities. The ultimate aims of the accreditation system are to guarantee that the
quality of basic health care meets an acceptable standard, and to introduce a culture of quality and high
- 66 -



performance in the delivery of health services and related training activities. The main expenditure
categories for this subcomponent are consultancy services, publications, training, workshops and
international seminars.
Main Activities. The activities under this subcomponent will be executed and managed by the MOH's
PCU, and will include:
* Development and testing of a regulatory framework and complete set of methods, instruments and
processes for accreditation of PSF units and training institutions.
*  Assessment and selection of institutions to conduct accreditation of PSF units and training institutions.
*  Gradual implementation of systems for the accreditation of PSF units and training institutions.
SubcomDonent D. Research and Evaluation Fund
Objective. This subcomponent has the objectives of: (a) stimulating analyses, evaluations and operational
research on topics related to the development and performance of the PSF and the related training
institutions, the process of decentralization of the SUS, human resources in the health sector, efficiency and
effectiveness of SUS, and other strategic topics identified by the MOH; and (b) developing the capacity of
professional researchers in the methodologies of research and evaluation, analysis of health systems, and
health economics and financing.
Main Activities.  The activities under this subcomponent would be implemented and managed by the
MOH's PCU through the establishment of a Research and Evaluation Fund (Fundo de Estudos e
Pesquisas). The Fund will have two accounts: the first to finance small studies (less than US$ 5,000)
conducted by individuals - both professionals as well as graduate and post-graduate students; the second to
finance major studies requiring a team of collaborators and staff (such as those requiring large household
or facility surveys and analysis of municipal and state data sets). Awards would be made on a competitive
basis. Activities under the first phase of the APL would include:
* Approximately 10 small research and evaluation activities within the relevant research areas.
*  Approximately 3 major research and evaluation activities within the relevant research areas.
*  Programming of regional symposia and workshops for presentation of methodologies, projects and
preliminary results.

Annex 2A
Brazil Family Health Project
Overview of Health Sector in Brazil
Introduction
This annex provides basic inforrnation about the dynamic and complex Brazilian health sector. Based on a
large number of published and unpublished sources, it seeks to provide sufficient background to permit an
understanding of the rationale for and structure of the proposed Family Health Project, rather than a
comprehensive or definitive perspective on the Brazilian health system. Main conclusions of the Sector
Profile are summarized in section B2 of the Family Health Project PAD, "Main Sector Issues and
Govemment Strategy."
The Sector Profile is organized as follows: In Section II, we present sentinel information about health
conditions and health services utilization in Brazil, with a focus on differentials by geographic region,
rural/urban residence, and household economic status. In Section III, we describe the structural features of
the system: who provides health services to whom, under what institutional and regulatory arrangements.
The issue of decentralization, which constitutes a core feature of the system's evolution, is taken up in
considerable detail. Sector financing and pattems of expenditure are covered in Section IV. In Section V,
the Family Health Program (Programa de Saude da Familia, or PSF) is described, with attention to its
objectives, evolution to date, special financing features, performance and future plans. In addition, the PSF
support functions of training and information systems are highlighted. We list the challenges facing the
PSF, which are addressed by the Family Health Project.
Basic Demographics, Health Conditions and Health Service Utilization
Basic Demographics
Brazil, a Federal Republic, has 26 states and a Federal District, and 5,507 municipalities within five
geographic regions (North, Northeast, Center-west, Southeast and South) that are distinguished by
demographic, economic and socio-cultural characteristics. Although there is large regional variation, on
average about 80 percent of the population lives in urban areas. While the vast majority (90 percent) of the
municipalities have populations of under 50,0000, most of the population resides in the larger cities: 10
percent of the largest municipalities account for 60 percent of the population.
The population growth rate was estimated at about 7.5 percent for the 1990s, dramatically lower than the
23 percent growth in the previous decade. Between 1960 and 1990, fertility declined by 50 percent. In
1997, the total fertility rate was estimated at 2.4 children per woman. Due to historically high fertility, the
population structure is still relatively young; however, the relative number of elderly is growing, and it has
recently been estimated that about 7.9 percent of the population is 60 years or older (IDB, 1998).
Brazil historically has been characterized by extreme income inequality and large numbers of households
living in poverty. According to the 1996 National Household Sample Survey (Pesquisa Nacional de
Amostra por Domicilios), the richest 20 percent of the population accounts for 65 percent of total income,
while the poorest 50 percent account for 12 percent (PNAD, 1996, UNDP, 1999). Poverty is highly
concentrated in the nine states of the Northeast region, home to 63 percent of the country's poor (see Table
1).
- 69 -

A World Bank report using 1996 PNAD household data defined extremely poor as people living in a
household with a per capita monthly income of less than $65 per month. It found that 22.6 percent of
Brazilians (34.5 million) are extremely poor and that a little over half of the extremely poor live in urban
areas (52.5 percent) (Table 2). Of the urban poor, 37 percent live in large urban or metropolitan areas.
Gwatkin et al (2000) used DHS data on household assets to define poverty and found that 38% of people in
the lowest quintile, and 56 (percent) in the lowest two quintiles live in urban areas.

B.     Health Conditions
Health status and living standards in Brazil have improved significantly in the last three decades. Today
people live longer, are less likely to die in their first year of life, and have fewer children than in the past -
the consequence of improvements in income, educational attainment and access to water and sanitation
complemented by increases in access and utilization of health care services. Despite these achievements,
Brazil under-performs other countries at similar income levels, and continues to face long-standing
problems in public health - infant mortality, child malnutrition and maternal mortality among the poor are
still high: Brazil is one of WHO's target countries for TB reduction due to its high prevalence, especially
among the urban and rural poor; and malaria and other endemic diseases threaten the North and Northeast
Regions. There are also relatively new concerns, including AIDS and the sharp increase in injuries and
chronic and degenerative diseases such as cardiovascular diseases and cancer among all income groups.
Cardiovascular diseases (disease of the circulatory system), injuries and cancer are the leading causes of
deaths in Brazil, accounting for about 60 percent of all deaths (see Figure 1).
- 70 -

Brazil's national infant mortality rate of 37.5 per 1,000 live births is higher than would be predicted at the
country's level of per capita GNP and literacy. The high infant mortality, which can be taken as a sentinel
of both socioeconomic and health conditions, holds true across all regions, as shown in Table 2.

As is found elsewhere, health conditions among the poor are far worse than among the better-off - and in
Brazil, the differences by income are profound. As shown in Table 3, infant mortality is estimated at more
than 80 per 1,000 live births among the poorest households, and less than 30 per 1,000 among the richest.
Similarly, approximately one-fifth of the poorest children have been found to be stunted, reflecting
long-standing nutritional deficits; stunting affects only about 2 percent of the highest-income children.
Poor teen age girls have 6 times higher fertility rates than the richest segment of the population and suffer
from most matemal deaths. The impact of violence and chronic diseases is also higher among the poor.
Comparing urban and rural populations, it is clear that economic status rather than place of residence is the
determinant of health status: the health conditions of the poor in urban areas have more in common with
those of the rural poor, than with those of higher-income urban residents. For some health conditions, data
also suggest that (at similar income levels) the urban poor have slightly worse health outcomes than the
rural poor.

C.      Health Service Utilization
Despite large recent investments to expand the supply of health services to populations with limited access
to basic health care services in rural and small urban areas, and despite the significant physical availability
of health services in urban areas, the inequalities in health outcomes partly reflect large differences in the
utilization of basic health services. In general terms, the poor have utilization rates 25 percent lower than
the non-poor in rural and urban areas (see Table 4). These low levels of utilization of services among the
poor -also found in OECD countries-reflect higher transaction costs and behavioral barriers facing the
poor.

Structure of the System
Evolution of the SUS
In 1988, a new Brazilian Constitution established - among other major social landmarks - the "right to
health," in which health (and access health services) are deemed to be the right of the citizen and the
responsibility of the state. While this ran counter to the contemporaneous international trends toward a
contraction of public sector obligations in the social sectors, it was consistent with a series of expansionist
reforms in Brazil, and set the stage for the formulation of a large-scale health sector reform that focused on:
(a) strengthening the public sector; (b) increasing and diversifying the sources of financing; (c)
decentralizing ownership and management of health services, and other functions; (d) rationalizing service
supply and delivery; and (e) redefining the relationships between
public and private sectors.                                             Box 1. Principles and Guidelines of the SUS
In 1989, the Sistema lInico de Satide, or SUS, was created with the
objective of forming a regionalized system of service provision by the  Principles

It is estimated that the SUS provides primary care for 85 percent of the population, secondary care for 70
percent, and highly complex care for 90 percent of the population, either directly or through contracts with
- 73 -



private providers. About 30 percent of the Brazilian population obtains secondary-level services from
group health institutions or traditional private providers (PAHO, 1998).
Decentralization
The SUS was created, in part, to transform a highly centralized, authoritarian and bureaucratic health
system that was characterized by a lack of social participation into one that was more efficient, and more
responsive to local conditions and needs. Thus, a major feature of the SUS reform has been the
decentralization of responsibility for the provision of health services.
Roles and Responsibilities. The reform redefined the roles of both executive and representative bodies at
Federal, state and municipal levels. The Federal level is responsible by law for formulating and
implementing national health policy, as well as for system planning, assessment and control. The allocation
of funds also falls within the purview of the federal government. The decision-making process involves:
(a) The National Health Council (CNS), comprised of representatives of government agencies, service
providers, health care professionals and users), and charged with strategic planning and monitoring
implementation of health policy; (b) The Tripartite Inter-managerial Committee (CIT), made up of
representatives from the Ministry of Health, the National Council of State Health Secretaries (CONASS),
and the National Council of Municipal Health Secretaries (CONASEMOH). This body approves rules for
the system's operation; and (c) The Ministry of Health itself, which manages the allocation of federal
funds, under the supervision of the CNS, and proposes operating rules to the CIT. (See Annex 2D for
additional information about institutional roles and responsibilities.)
State functions and responsibilities include service coordination, distribution of financial resources, and
certain decisions related to the provision of specialized (high technology) interventions. Reproducing the
Federal structure, the state-level decision-making bodies include the State Health Secretariat, Bipartite
Inter-managerial Committee and State Health Council.
Municipalities, which are the main subject of the decentralization efforts to date, have been given the
responsibility for delivering health-related goods and services to the population, including health promotion,
preventive health care, rehabilitation, and treatment. Thus, it is at the municipalities where the fundamental
actions are taken that determine the quality, quantity and type of health services for the population. The
Municipal Health Secretariat and Municipal Health Council are the key actors.
Norms Guiding Decentralization. Since the early 1990s, a series of administrative instruments and
financing reforms have been enacted to expand and deepen the decentralization process, mainly by (a)
establishing criteria for various levels of decentralization; and (b) providing incentives for municipalities
and states willing (and able) to take on increasing responsibilities, consistent with national health policy. In
particular, the Basic Operational Norms (called Normas Operacionais Bdsicas, or NOBs, in the earlier
years, and more recently Norma Operacional Assistencial a Saude or NOAs), have helped to shape the
evolution of decentralization.
In 1993, the first Basic Operational Norms (NOBs) defined two categories of management readiness -
intermediate and advanced - for states, and three categories - initial, intermediate and advanced - for
municipalities. A given state or municipality's responsibilities, requirements and financial incentives, set
by the NOB, depended on its stage of managerial capacity.
In 1996, another set of Operational Norrns sought to induce further development of managerial capacity.
The norms gave states a somewhat larger role in system coordination and monitoring than they had enjoyed
- 74 -



earlier. For municipalities, the norms established stages of decentralization, depending on the
municipality's ability to manage the system.  Under the norms, municipalities are classified into
management types through a process called habilitcdo, an assessment of municipalities' capacity to
provide services to the population, having installed the necessary control mechanisms such as auditing and
information systems. The habilitcdo criteria are described in the section below, along with information
about municipal entitlements under different levels of management.
The 1996 norms provided incentives for municipalities to place a higher priority on basic care - a
"package" of procedures to be defined locally (within a given framework). Through a subsequent (1998)
financing reform, the Federal Government replaced fee-for-service reimbursements for basic interventions
by a system of per-capita transfers, called the Basic Health Care Quota (Piso Assistencial Basico, or
PAB), which is distributed to municipalities according to population size, epidemiological risk and other
criteria. (Details about the PAB system can be found in Section IV.B). In the 1996-98 period, additional
financial incentives were introduced to strengthen and expand coverage of basic health services, and
reorient them to a prevention and outreach model, rather than a traditional curative one. Municipalities
obtain a higher capitated payment if they adopt a model of primary care promoted by the Federal
government - the "full PSF package" of the Community Health Agent Program (PACS), the Basic Medical
Program (PFB), the Program to Combat Nutritional Deficiencies (PCCN), and the Epidemiological
Surveillance (ANVISA). Municipalities achieving wider population coverage with PSF are also rewarded
with additional transfers, described in Section V.D.
In 2001, a new set of operational norms - NOAS/01 - were promulgated to correct some of the
shortcomings of the reform to date, including the persistent lack of integration and coordination among
levels of care, and the need for smaller municipalities to join with others to achieve the minimum size
required for the provision of secondary or tertiary services. The Operating Norms sought to establish a
regional planning level to improve the organization and distribution of health services - with a region being
a group of contiguous municipalities that could systematically share and exchange health care resources to
optimize investments. The NOAS/01 also sought to strengthen the municipal management of primary care,
and establish state plans for regionalization of ambulatory care and specialized hospital services.
Types of Municipal Management Arrangements under the Decentralized Regime.  Depending on a
municipality's installed capacity to manage the health system, it may qualify through the habilitq4o process
for Full Management of Basic Care (Gestdo Plena da Atencdo Baisica), Full Management of the Municipal
Health System (Gestdo Plena do Sistema Municipal), or Full Management of Expanded Basic Care
(Gestdo Plena da Atenqdo Bdsica Ampliada). The first entitles municipalities to receive block grants for
primary care, the second allows the transfer of grants for primary, secondary and tertiary care, and the
third entitles municipalities to somewhat larger grants for primary care. (The funding mechanisms are
described in Section IV.B.)
The process to qualify for a specific management arrangement - and the funding that accompanies it - is
shown below:
To qualify for Full Management of Basic Care:
a. The municipality applies to the state.
b. The municipal health council (CMOH) approves the application.
c. The CIB approves the application within 60 days and writes the terms under which the municipality
will be authorized to work; then it submits the necessary proving documents to the CIT.
d. If the municipality is located in a state already abiding by the criterios de habilitaciio, then the
- 75-



municipality is authorized by the state, and the Ato de Habilita,do (official authorization Act) is
published in the Diario Oficial da Unido of state with copy to the technical secretariat of CIT together
with terms of habilitaado to be ratified.
e. If the municipality is located in a state not abiding by the criterios de habilita,do, the terms of
habilitag,co are sent to the CIT to be approved.
f. CIT analyzes terms of habilita,do and informs the MOH.
g. A ministerial decree authorizes the municipality to operate within that management scheme, as long as
there are enough resources to make the transfers effective.
h. PABfixed transfers can then commence, subject to the municipality (or state in the cases that apply)
updating the following information systems: Sistema de Informa,co Sobre Mortalidade (SITM),
Sistema de Informa,cao Sobre Nacidos Vivos (SINASC), Sistema de Vigildncia Alimentar e
Nutricional (SISVAN), and Sistema de Informa,do Sobre Agravos de Notifica,do (SINAN).
In the case of type II management scheme, the CIB has no authority by itself to authorize the municipality
to operate under Gestdo Plena do Sistema Municipal, even if the state has already been habilita,do. It is
then up to the federal level to grant authorization to the municipality. All the other steps are identical to
previous case.
Human Resources
In recent years, Brazil has seen a real increase in the numbers of nurses and dentists, and a constant
number of physicians, relative to the total population (see Table 6). Distribution of health professionals is
uneven, with the heaviest concentration of doctors in the Southeast (18.13/10,000) and the lightest in the
North (6.02/10,000). As is seen elsewhere, health professionals are also highly concentrated in urban
areas-particularly state capitals.

During the reforms of the 1990s, when ownership of most health care facilities was transferred from the
Federal to state and municipal management, employer-employee relationships also changed.  Most
professionals in the public system continue their Federal careers, which are being phased out, but are
administratively subordinate to the lower levels of government. In 1992 (the most recent year for which
data could be found), slightly less than half of all physicians were employed in the public sector (see Table
7). In contrast, the government employed the majority of nurses.

Sector Financing and Expenditures
Public Financing Levels
Historically, Brazil's health sector has suffered from persistent problems of insufficient and inequitable
funding levels, regressive revenue collection, fluctuations in funding with economic cycles and
centralization. Despite some efforts to address these issues, including the introduction in the late-1990s of
an earmarked tax (codified in the Enmenda Constitucional, or EC-29). (The amendment will increase SUS
revenues starting in 2000 with an additional 5 percent over the level of the budget committed in 1999. For
the years 2001 and 2002, it increases the budget in the same amount that the nominal GDP. The
amendment also increases contributions from the states). These issues have continued to undermine the
health sector reform efforts. Of particular concem has been the significant fluctuations in the total Federal
resources allocated to the health sector.
Since the early 1990s, the financing of SUS services has been decentralized from the Federal to the state
and municipal levels. Federal contributions may be transferred from the National Health Fund (Fundo
Nacional de Saude, or FNS) directly to either a State Health Fund (Fundo Estadual de Saude, or FES) or
a Municipal Health Fund (Fundo Municipal de Satude, or FMOH). Similarly, a state can make transfers
from its FES to an FMOH. Each fund also receives contributions from its corresponding treasury.
Public Financing Flows
Total public spending is regulated through financing caps (Tetos Financeiros, or TF), which are reflected
in each year's budget. The budget and the sector targets for each calendar year are the result of a
negotiated, bottom-top, local-programming exercise (Programa,do Pactuada Integrada), involving all
levels of government.
There are three different ways in which funds from the federal government reach the states and
municipalities:
a.  Automatic transfers through the FNS to states, municipalities and the Distrito Federal according to a
series of norms and regulations like NOB 01/93 and 01/96. Money is transferred periodically from
the FNS to the FES and FMOH;
b.  Direct payments to health care providers, both public and private, who have been hired through
special contracts, convenios;
c.  Special contracts, convenios, with federal, state, or municipal entities, or NGOS, for specific projects.
- 77 -



In total, about 34 different programs use the fundo a fundo system of transfers. These fall under two
categories: the Piso de Aten,ao Basica Fixo (PABfixed), and the Piso de Aten,co Basica variave (PAB
var). PABfixed transfers a fLxed amount of money to municipalities or states (where appropriate) on a per
capita basis, for basic health care activities. It consists of a per capita transfer of R$ 10 to R$18 per year to
participating municipalities, or depending on eligibility conditions (habilitagao) to states, paid in equal
monthly installments. The variation in the amount of the per capita transfer is based on historical reasons:
before the PABfixed was created about 1,950 municipalities spent R$5,00 or less per person per year
(these municipalities now receive a R$10,00 grant per capita), while about 250 municipalities were
spending more than R$22,00 per capita (these municipalities now receive a R$ 18,00 grant per capita).
PABvar transfers are generally earmarked to a specific activities or program and funds can only be spent
on certain categories of spending. For example, the transfer for the Family Health Program can only be
applied to pay for family health teams and inputs they require. The amount transferred varies according to
the number of teams
Public Expenditures
Health expenditures nationally have grown quickly over the last two decades. Currently, total health
expenditures are estimated by the MOH at R$86.5 billion (US$47.7 million), or 9 percent of GDP. As
shown in Table 8, the public sector accounts for about 40 percent of all spending, with the rest nearly
evenly divided between privately-paid health insurance and out-of-pocket payments.
Table 8. Estimates of Brazilian Health Care Expenditure, 1999
Source: MOH, 2001
Sub-Sector       Total, R$ bln  Per capita   % Public     % Total     % GDP

Public               34.6         116.9         100         40          3.6

Looking only at public spending, in 1999 the Federal government was the source of approximately 59
percent of all spending, with states accounting for 17 percent, and municipalities for 24 percent. However,
the national average masks large regional variation: In the Southeast, municipal spending almost matches
the volume of Federal sending in the region; in the North, state spending outweighs both Federal and
municipal spending; and in the Northeast, South and Center-West, the Federal government accounts for far
more spending than either the states or the municipalities.
Over the past several years, Federal spending on health has increased in relative terms, from 6.8 percent of
total Federal spending in 1998 to 9.7 percent of Federal spending in 2000. There have been sirnilar
increases at state and municipal levels, across all regions. The implementation of EC-29, which is
projected to generate an increase of about 26 percent in health sector funding, will continue this trend into
the foreseeable future.
- 78 -



At all levels, health expenditures are concentrated in two categories: medical care, and administration.
M6dici (2001) estimates that medical care accounts for approximately 85-90 percent of all health spending
at the municipal level, and approximately 60-70 percent at the state and Federal levels. Administrative
spending accounts for about 20 percent of the total, also varying somewhat by level of government.
Financial sustainability of the health system depends on increasing the volume and reliability of sources of
financing - the goal underlying the passage of EC-29 - and containment of costs. In recent years, MOH
has successfully introduced several cost-containment measures, including the imposition of physical and
financial ceilings for inpatient services and the direct control over high-cost interventions instrumented
through the Fondo de Acoes Estrategicas e Compensacao (FAEC). As a result of these controls, the rate
of increase of the cost of hospitalizations and of high complexity interventions has slowed. Mid-complexity
interventions (which include a large number of outpatient services) have now grown to 54 percent of the
total costs of SUS and are now a priority area for cost-controls. The strategy to control these costs consists
of: (i) shifting some simple interventions from the mid-complexity category into primary care, and require
municipal governments to finance them as part of their responsibilities for receipt of a slightly enlarged
PAB capitation; (ii) shifting some high cost interventions from the mid-complexity category into FAEC and
subjecting them to direct control by MOH; and last but not least; (iii) developing the capacity of
municipalities to control mid-complexity costs by managing care based on PSF.
Private Expenditures
Little information is available about household expenditures on health. One study comparing household
spending over a decade, using data from the Family Budget Surveys of 1987 and 1996, demonstrated that:
(a) spending on health as a share of the total household budget at increased from 5.3 percent to 6.5 percent;
(b) the increase in relative spending was greatest for the lowest-income households; (c) medicines absorb
the largest share of household health spending; and (d) spending on health insurance appeared to have
increased over the time period (see Table 9).

I.     Programa de Saude da Familia (PSF)
In this section, we first describe the main features of the PSF, as envisioned by the Federal
government. Then, we highlight the achievements and shortcomings observed in the PSF Program
to date.
- 79 -



A.     Objectives and Activities
A.     Objectives and Activities
The expansion of the PSF is a top priority of the
MOH. The PSF is the Brazilian MOH's front-       Box 2. Brazil's Health Priorities
line in achieving its constitutional responsibility
of health care for all, and SUS's principles of  1. Reduce maternal and infant mortality
universality, equity and integrality. The PSF is  2. Control priority diseases
also the MOH's key to addressing the first four  3. Improve management, access and
of Brazil's six recently consensually defined   quality of health actions and services
health priorities (see Box 3). Finally the PSF is  4. Reorient health care model towards
the vehicle that is driving health sector reform.  primary care
It strengthens the decentralization process at the  5. Development of health professionals
local (municipal) level and is the vehicle for  6. Enhance social control
changing the health care delivery model.
The PSF was conceived by the government with the objective of reforming the traditional model of
delivering health services, which was passive, fragmented, and mainly curative and hospital-centered. The
program aims at restructuring the health services delivery model through a more efficient and effective
primary care system focused on the family and community, instead of the individual. The PSF provides
continuous, integrated primary health care for an enrolled population in a fixed geographic area, selects the
family unit as the focus of primary care, identifies risk factors and the individuals who might be exposed to
them, and emphasizes active outreach, preventive care, inter-sectoral collaboration, and community-based
information and education regarding health. (See Table 10 for a comparison of traditional and PSF
approaches.)
In the PSF, Family Health Teams, consisting of at least one doctor (generalist or family health doctor), a
nurse, a nurse auxiliary, and four to six Community Health Agents (CHAs). Community Health Agents
(CHAs) carry out the following tasks: registering all families in their area and visiting them at least once
per month, weighing and measuring all children under two years of age, ensuring vaccination, identifying
children of school-going age not attending school, teaching families how to prevent diarrhea and use oral
rehydration, provide information about preventing acute respiratory infections, promoting breast-feeding,
identifying pregnant women and encouraging them to receive antenatal care and tetanus vaccinations,
encouraging the prevention and diagnosis of cervical and breast cancer, providing information about family
planning and sexually transmitted diseases (including AIDS), monitoring treatment of family members with
chronic diseases, assisting ill family members, reporting deaths, and encouraging solutions to
environmental problems such as garbage collection and sewage disposal. Community Health Agents
(CHAs) provide basic care to a defined set of 600-1,000 families (up to a maximum of 4,500 individuals).
The teams provide both primary health care, and lead efforts in prevention and behavioral change. The
health care professionals work full-time in their public duties and accordingly receive higher salaries than
most public sector doctors and nurses. The CHAs live in the communities in which they work and receive
salaries close to minimum wage. A comparison between the traditional model of providing primary care
and the new family health model is presented below:
- 80 -

Several other features of the PSF are important to emphasize:
*  Rather than being an additional primary care program of the MOH, the PSF is a new strategy of
providing basic care to the population and organizing the delivery of services based on basic care.
*  The PSF is not a program exclusively for the poor. It is a model for providing basic care for the
entire Brazilian population. The government recognizes the priority of those with limited access to
and utilization of basic heath care services in implementing the program.
*  The PSF is not a mandatory program. Rather, municipalities can opt to subscribe to the program
or not. To start implementation of the program, however, municipalities have to comply with
minimum requirements.
*  The PSF replaces the existing traditional primary health care model.
* The PSF is not exclusively a maternal and child health program. However, maternal and child
interventions are among the most important and significant services provided by the program.
*  Where and when it is fully realized, the PSF will in theory be the principal point of entry into the
SUS, functioning as a "gate-keeper."
- 81 -

more of under age 5 child deaths. These include: antenatal care, family planning, case-management, breast-
feeding and the Hib vaccine (recently implemented in Brazil). The other two are: improved delivery care
(not directly a function of primary care; however, the FHP will be responsible for assuring that women are
referred to hospitals for delivery and that transportation is available when necessary) and improved birth
weight (no effective interventions available).
The FHEP minimal services that are not mentioned in the World Bank's "best buys" report include:
elimination of leprosy (a feasible objective with incidence and prevalence in Brazil decreasing annually due
to better treatment), hypertension and diabetes (important causes of preventable mortality from heart
disease and strokes), oral health (important to well-being) and health promotion generally. Brazil has
undergone an epidemiological transition and the PSF is expected to play an important role in preventing
cardiovascular diseases, strokes, diabetes, and injuries, and addressing alcoholism and drug addiction,
through the promotion of healthy behaviors.
B.      Program Implementation
Government Responsibilities. Municipalities generally manage PSF teams and provide non-personnel
inputs such as facilities and medical equipment. Municipalities also are responsible for defining and
making arrangements for referral systems for the PSF. As described in the earlier section on financing, the
Federal government provides partial financing - essentially matching grants - for municipalities that
implement the program. State Health Secretariats supervise and train municipalities on the PSF program,
and provide additional inputs such as medical equipment, pharmaceuticals and training for municipal
Family Health Teams. (Also see Table 11.)

Municipal Eligibility for PSF Fundina. It is the responsibility of the Health Policies Secretariat (SPS)
within its basic health department and the corresponding state health secretariat (SES) to determine if a
municipality is qualified to participate in PSF, and receive the corresponding additional funding. The
process by which a municipality is authorized to receive PSF is called Qualificacao. To qualify for PSF, a
number of steps have to be followed by the municipality:
a. Design an implementation plan for installing Family Health Teams, specifying geographical area to be
covered, estimated population, number of teams, description of equipment each team will have, actions
to be performed by the teams within the strategic areas defined in the NOAS 2001; describe the
recruitment policies to be followed for selecting professionals that will participate in the teams; and
describe the process to be followed to install the SLAB.The corresponding SES will participate in this
process.
- 83-



b. Submit the plan to the CIB to discuss the implementation plan. This commission is composed by
representatives of both municipalities and states.
c. The agreement is formalized through a legal instrument, the Declara,co de Incentivos, signed by the
municipality administrator and by the CIB coordinator, and is remitted to the MOH.
d. A decree by MOH authorizes the municipality to start receiving the PSF funding.
e. The number of family health teams and Community Health Care Agents (ACS) agreed upon is
published in Diario Oficial da Unido and in the Sistema de Captagdo de Dados para Pagamento de
Incentivos CAPSI, an information system that keeps track of the number of teams and health workers
in each team.
f. Input the number of acting teams in the SLAB;
g. Update monthly the SIAB to prevent the transfers from being interrupted.
The Declara,do de Incentivos does not specify a time period to implement the plan. Thus the agreement
between state and municipalities is only renewed when the planned number of teams has been installed, or
else, when the municipality decides to review its estimates concerning teams and ACS. The entire
application process may take a minimum of 90 days.
It should be noticed that the criterios de habilita,ao do not apply to determine eligibility of municipalities
to receive PSF funding. This is the only grant that can be provided to municipalities that do not satisfy any
criterios de habilitacdo, as long as they have been approved according to the criteria specified above. In
the event that a municipality is not approved for either type of management, but the state is, the PABfixed
is transferred to the state, to the FES, which will then take care of administering PABfixed in the
municipality. The same applies to PSF.
PSF Training and the Polos de Capacitacdo . The implementation of the PSF model requires both training
in specific technical areas and protocols and, more importantly, a reorientation in the entire perspective of
care delivery - from one that is passive to one that is active; from one that is fragmented to one that is
integrated; from one based on responding to health problems identified by patients, to one based on the
anticipation and prevention of health problems. This implies a wide set of training needs, not all of which
have yet been successfully addressed.
Currently, Family Health Teams receive an introductory training course as a group (except the CHAs).
CHAs receive one-week introductory courses and sporadic in-service training of shorter duration (one
half-day to two days), conducted either by the PSF nurse or a contracted trainer. The government
complements this training with in-service training through on-site specialization courses and long-distance
training. Finally, the government also offers post-graduate training and residency in collective health and
family health.
For the purpose, in 1997 the MOH invited existing medical and other training institutions to submit
proposals for new programs. Many ideas were presented, and the MOH selected 10 projects according to
established criteria. These became the first 10 P6oos de Capacita,cdo-training institutions or programs
designed specifically for the PSF; they were financed under the World Bank-MOH REFORSUS Project.
Most of the Polos are joint projects of institutions of higher learning (universities, faculties and schools of
medicine and nursing) and State and/or Municipal Health Secretariats. The programs take a range of
forms, variously concentrating on pre-service, in-service and specialty training in Family Health, and using
many different training methods, including on-site and distance leaming techniques.
- 84 -



In 1999, the MOH dedicated additional government resources for the development of 10 additional P6los,
based on the results of another competition, and in 2000 an additional 11 P6los were created. Currently,
there are 31 P6los, one in most states, and several in the states of Sao Paulo and Minas Gerais.
PSF Management Information. The Sistema de Informacdo de Atencdo Bdsica (SIAB) is the management
information system associated with the PSF, and has the objective of collecting and processing information
about the population covered by the Program, as well as monitoring and evaluating outcomes and impact of
PSF activities. The system includes eight separate data collection instruments, most of which are to be
completed by the Community Health Agents working with the Family Health Teams. These include, for
example, a family roster, and patient-specific forms to follow the progress and health service utilization of
pregnant women and children, as well as individuals with hypertension, diabetes and tuberculosis. The
system also includes instruments for the consolidation of data, including consolidated annual reports on
families (or households) covered by a given Family Health Unit (or Team); summary reports on health
conditions and treatment; and reports on the production of evaluation indicators. The consolidated reports
apply variously to the level of the microarea (400-750 residents, corresponding to one Community Health
Agent), catchment area (2,400-4,500 residents, corresponding to one Family Health Team), segment (group
of contiguous areas, corresponding to a sanitary district), and/or municipality. In concept, a fully operating
SIAB can provide continuous information about the size, distribution and characteristics of the covered
population; utilization of health services, including continuity of care; and health conditions and outcomes.
C.     Program Implementation Progress
First adopted in 1994, the PSF has expanded quickly recently during 1999 and 2000 (see Table 12). In
September 2000, there were 10,025 PSF teams working in 3,059 municipalities in each of Brazil's states.
Those teams, assuming a coverage rate of 3,450 people per team, provided care to 37.9 million people, or
about 23 percent of the Brazilian population. Under current government plans, the PSF will provide
coverage to 50 percent of the Brazilian population by the end of the year 2002, which means it will have to
double in size in about two years. As the PSF expands, the CHAs of the PACS (which was initiated in
1991) will be integrated into PSF teams.

D.     Program Financing Levels and Flows
As noted earlier, funding for the PSF is realized through the automatic, per capita "fund-to-fund" transfers.
Before November 1999, the incentive was based on the number of Family Health Teams implemented:
R$28,000 per FHT per year, excluding CHA. The incentive for CHA is R$2,200 per CHA per year.
After November 1999, Federal grants for the PSF range from R$28,000 to R$54,000 per PSF team per
year, depending on the coverage level reached (up to 70 percent). There is a basic incentivo determined by
a non-linear scheme that depends on the percentage of population covered by each ESF. Specifically:
C      Ex3450 x 100
p
is used to determine the percentage of population covered by PSF, where C is population covered in
percentage terms, E is the number of health teams, P is total number of inhabitants in the municipality, and
3,450 is the average number of people covered by a team. The higher C is, the higher the basic incentivo.
The subsidy is govemed by the following scheme, and paid in equal monthly installments (see Table 13).
In addition, the Federal government offers an initial grant of R$10,000 per Family Health Team for the
implementation of new teams. It is paid in two consecutive monthly installments of R$5,000 each after the
new team has been put in place.  Fewer than half of the states provide additional incentives to
- 86 -



municipalities for implementing the program.  State incentives include donation of equipment;
construction/restoration of physical infrastructure; donation of medicines; payment of health personnel; as
well as financial transfers.

In principle the money is transferred directly from the FNS to the FMOH (or FES where it applies), in
monthly equal installments both for the PABfixed and the incentivo basico of PSF. There is a special
account for each municipality for the fundo a fundo transfers, usually at the Banco do Brasil, and the
transfers specify the destination of funds (i.e. separate for PABfixed, PABfam for the PSF, etc).(See Figure
1 at end of Annex).
Since PABfixed is paid in per capita terms, the amounts paid change when the population base changes in a
municipality. The updating in each municipality is not a systematic procedure. As for PSF, the payment
depends on the number of ESF installed each month. The municipality must register the number of teams
in place in the previous month by no later than the fifth of the following month, in the SJAB, and submit
such information to the SES. This in turn transfers the information to the SPS, at the Ministry of Health.
There the information is crossed with the information contained in the CAPSI, an information system that
keeps a record of the original implementation agreement. The latter acts as an upper bound (i.e. if the
municipality has more teams in place than those accorded in the plan, the latter binds as a constraint for
payments).
Once the funds reach the FMOH, the money is administered either by the municipal health secretariat or by
the municipal finance secretariat. There is not systematic rule on this but it varies across municipalities
and states.
E.     Major Program Achievements
Several evaluations and assessments have been carried out by the government and other agencies regarding
the impact of the PSF program. The main benefits found by these studies can be summarized as follows: i)
The PSF has achieved increased coverage of basic care in small size municipalities with high infant
mortality rates. ii) There is a high level of acceptance of the program by health professionals and users.
iii) The PSF is a successful vehicle for channeling other government programs to the community and
enhancing multi-sectoral collaboration. iv) The PSF has had a significant positive impact on performance
and outcome indicators, including: increased production of and access to basic health services; reduced
hospital admissions and hospital care; and reduced infant mortality (see Table 14).
- 87 -
