F.     Challenges
Despite the above accomplishments, the government's PSF strategy faces significant challenges. These
challenges will be addressed by the proposed World Bank Family Health Project, and include:
*  Low coverage of the PSF in large urban areas and metropolitan rezions despite recent changes in
financial incentive structure. Program expansion in large urban areas and metropolitan regions has
been slower than expected despite recent changes in financial incentives.  In contrast, small
municipalities have been very successful in expanding the program. In addition, many municipalities
report difficulties in financing the program and maintaining key personnel (especially doctors) despite
Federal subsidies.
* Insufficient targeting of the program to the most disadvantaLged population groups and competing
health priorities at the local level. An evaluation carried out by the MOH in 2000 showed that more
than half of the state program managers did not have clear criteria for prioritizing the introduction of
the program. The same evaluation also revealed that municipal health authorities had a clearer criteria
for prioritizing the program: 82 percent referred to a risk criteria (epidemiological or social); 50
percent referred to responsiveness to community demands; and 23 percent referred to responsiveness to
local political leadership demands. The lack of clear criteria for targeting the program is not only a
matter of selecting the geographical area to implement PSF within a municipality, but is also apparent
in terms of determining what health priority should be addressed first, and with what health
intervention. A recent World Bank study on maternal and child health in Brazil estimated that
prioritizing cost-effective interventions such as case management of childhood illness; family planning;
nutritional education; antenatal, delivery, post-partum, and neonatal care; breast-feeding promotion,
and hepatitis B vaccine, could jointly avert about 33 percent of under 5 deaths, and cut one-third of
infant deaths over a period of 5 years.
*   Low integration of the PSF with other levels of care, and inadeauate referral systems. The
referral and counter-referral system in most of the SUS is vague and functions badly. This is a critical
problem for maternal and child health and particularly for delivery care. Medical records on antenatal
care are not linked to treatment at the time of delivery and to post-partum care. This situation also
compromises the capacity of the system to diagnose and then rapidly treat obstetric emergencies.
Several factors contribute to the weakness of the referral system in Brazil. First, political rivalries,
bureaucratic divisions, and undefined roles in the decentralized system lead to a lack of coordination.
Second, the authority of public sector managers over SUS-affiliated philanthropic and private
- 88 -



hospitals, which is not well delineated. Third, the payment system rewards high volume work on the
part of public and private philanthropic providers with little time for follow-up. Finally, the staff
turnover and part-time, itinerant status of doctors in SUS facilities complicates communication.
*  Low quality of services of the PSF despite si2nificant gains compared to the traditional PHC
system. The MOH evaluation revealed that, still, a significant proportion of Family Health Teams and
units continue to lack adequate equipment and supplies. For example, only 20 percent of Family
Health Teams had a glucometer and 15 percent had ophthalmoscope. A significant proportion of
Family Health Teams report shortages of certain vaccines and basic medicines. In addition, support
laboratory is inadequate for key exams such as urinary analysis, cytopathology of cervix, and obstetric
sonogram, among others. As a result, only 15 percent of Family Health Teams met the MOH's
standard for quality pre-natal care for example.
*  Low quality of care is not only the result of inadequate infrastructure and supply/support
systems, but also a combination of lack of protocols/standards, quality control mechanisms, and
poorly qualified personnel. About 67 percent of state program coordinators reported that they did not
have clinical protocols Protocols are defined by the MOH as instruments that guide Family Health
Teams on best strategies and clinical practices that result in improved quality and effectiveness of the
program. for the program. Health care providers and administrators lack adequate knowledge of local
practices and cultures, which are critical to providing humane care and effectively promoting
behavioral change.
* SiRniricant human resources supply bottleneck in terms of auantitv and aualitv. Municipalities
report difficulties in hiring high qualified professionals (mainly doctors) willing to work in localities
distant from big urban centers. As an example, about 22 percent of municipalities had to recruit
doctors from outside their state to fill positions. In some cases, states have made special arrangements
with the government of Cuba to import physician generalists to Brazil. In terms of qualifications, only
37 percent of PSF physicians have completed their medical residency (compared to a national average
of 75 percent) and only 40 percent of physicians and 36 percent of nurses have specialty training.The
lack of qualified health personnel starts at the university level. Medical schools train few generalists,
and students do not demand such training because labor markets do not reward general medicine, and
the specialization garners relatively low professional status.
*  Unstable contractual arranL'ements between PSF Dersonnel and emplovers (municipalities).
According to the government evaluation of the program, most PSF personnel are hired under temporary
contracts (49 percent of doctors, 44 percent of nurses), or are paid on a piecework basis (27 percent of
doctors, 24 percent of nurses). These arrangements are made either directly by the municipalities or
through NGOs, cooperatives or other autonomous entities that then sub-contract the health
professionals. Only 16 percent of PSF doctors and nurses are hired as full public servants, and an
additional 11-12 percent are hired by municipalities but work under the private sector labor codes. On
average, doctors are paid R$3,267 per month (66 percent of physicians are paid in the range of
R$2,000-4,000). Although these arrangements promote flexibility in personnel management and
decreases the size of civil servants payroll and benefits, they also produce problems. They promote
high rotation of personnel (compromising program continuity and doctor-patient relationship) and
dissatisfaction among personnel. About two-thirds of PSF doctors and nurses say that the job is too
demanding and consuming. Team members' main complaints refer to the weak contractual relationship
the employer, the high work load, lack of resources, difficulties in accessing work places, low
remuneration, and ineffective referral systems.
- 89 -

Annex 2B
Brazil - Family Heath Project
Social Assessment
This annex highlights the social dimensions of the Family Health Project (FHEP), which supports the
Government of Brazil's current health sector strategies. It also describes the extent of social participation
during implementation of the Family Health Program, and the design of the proposed project.
Following the establishment of the National Health System (Sistema Unico de Satude, or SUS) in 1988, the
Family Health Program (Programa de Saude da Familia, or PSF) was created in 1994 in the Ministry of
Health. The SUS aimed to integrate all health care institutions, to universalizing coverage and promoting
equal access. The PSF originated from two challenges faced by the SUS: First, how to conduct the fiscal
decentralization as mandated by the 1988 Brazilian Constitution; second, how to reach the goals of
universalization and equality by addressing the lack of responsiveness to the health needs of the population.
The PSF constituted in an incremental adjustment in the larger transformation of the SUS - a reform within
a reform.
The PSF is a territorial program, in which coverage of families is geographically determined. Families
living within boundaries of a given area are reached by a Family Health Team. In implementing the PSF,
each team follows the following steps: (a) maintains a data base (cadastro) of all families in its area; (b)
organizes and maintains a small group of professionals (a family doctor (generalist, rather than specialized
skills), a nurse, an attendant and five to six Community Health Agents); (c) promotes comprehensive
assistance to all families in the area through prevention, diagnosis, treatment and rehabilitation and, if
needed, refers the patient to specialized health services, including hospitals; (d) integrates health actions
and activities taking place in the municipality; and (e) promotes cross-sectoral preventive activities.
As currently designed, the following steps are undertaken by municipal governments to start the program:
(a) define priority areas and map the families in these areas; (b) undertake community diagnosis based on
their socio-economic characteristics and health profile; and (c) plan activities towards collectivities rather
than towards individuals. To obtain support and fiscal resources for the implementation of the PSF, the
municipal government is expected to: (a) seek approval of the Municipal Health Council; (b) establish
priority areas for the implementation of the program; (c) adequate the existing staff to the needs and
requirements of the PSF; (d) establish the referral system with area hospitals and higher complexity units;
and (e) define the modality through which new health professionals and agents will be hired.
Among several social development issues related to the Family Health Project, four deserve special
attention: (i) inequality in access to health services and, consequently, the inequality of health status among
different groups and areas; (ii) the growing problem of diseases, such as AIDS, that have a large behavioral
components, and that strongly affect vulnerable subgroups of the population, such as women, and those
who are socially and economically under-privileged; (iii) poor reproductive health status, with high
maternal mortality and teenage pregnancy rates; and (iv) a traditional emphasis on "curative" medicine,
centered on hospitals and on fragmented interventions by specialized professionals.
The PSF, supported in strategic ways by the Family Health project, was designed to improve access,
particularly for populations with limited access to and utilization of basic health care services; promote
comprehensive interventions; diminish the need for hospitalizations; and disseminate a concept of health as
well being, de-linking it from diseases and hospitals. Among the core Family Health interventions to be
- 91 -



strengthened are those that yield the greatest improvements in maternal and child health. Thus, the project
directly addresses each of the problems listed above.
According to the MOH, coverage of the PSF to date has shown: (a) the majority of municipalities (64
percent) with PSF have less than 44,000 inhabitants and about 50 percent have between 3,000 and 17,000
inhabitants. Coverage decreases as the size of municipalities increases: in small municipalities (population
less than 3,000) coverage is excessive, reaching 100 percent; in larger municipalities, coverage has faced
constraints reaching the target population, which lives in marginal areas.
Preliminary studies have shown that the PSF has modified the pattern for the consumption of health
services in the country through the selection of families as the main social actor, and at the same time
promoted changes the organization of the supply of these services through community-based teams. As a
result, in the municipalities where the program exists the number of hospitalizations decreased for children
and for adults for almost all different diseases.
Some good examples of the impact of PSF are Vit6ria da Conquista (Bahia) Sobral, (Ceara) and Sao Jorge
do Patrocinio (Parana), described below:
*   Vitoria da Conquista is particularly important for the project, given its size of 263,000 inhabitants. In
this municipality, the SUS became fully implemented in 1998, integrating a network of 112 health
service facilities, the majority of which (62 percent) are health centers and family units. The PSF began
to be implemented also in 1998, benefiting from strong support from the University of Bahia and the
State Southeast University. Forty percent of the population has been covered since then, and 100
percent of the rural population have been reached. With the SUS and the PSF, hospitalizations
decreased 29 percent between 1996 and 2000. Hospitalizations for dehydrated children also began to
fall quickly. The program, however, has faced two important challenges: (a) as the need for
hospitalizations decreased, the private sector became more vocal in its opposition to the program; and
(b) strikes in the private sector have affected the need for hospitalization through agreements (
convenios) with the SUS.
*  Sobral has 144,000 inhabitants, with 86 percent of the population living in urban areas. In 1991, it had
a .481 rate of human development as measured using the IPEA index, only roughly above the median
for the country. The PSF began to be implemented in 1997. In 2000, it covered about 87 percent of the
population, includes 31 teams and 183 Community Health Agents. According to the Municipal
Secretary of Health, "the PSF was conceptualized not as a program, but as a strategy for building a
new model, breaking the old logic of centering attention on the disease and on the hospital". Mothers
and pre-natal care was defined as a priority and in 1999, 100 percent of pregnancies were covered. At
least six visits to a health facility health are secured as part of the priority. As an incentive, clothes and
napkins for the baby are provided. Dissemination materials to pregnant women were prepared and
distributed, with basic information about reproductive health in its several aspects, including child care.
Two years later, the incidence of diarrheas has decreased as have episodes of violence within families
and neighborhoods. The decrease in hospitalization as consequence of malnutrition has also been
significant - from 418 in 1994 to 150 in 1999.
*  Sio Jorge do Patrocinio has 6,400 inhabitants, with 60 percent of them living in its rural areas. Since
the beginning of the implementation of the PSF, hospitalizations for children under one fell from 54 to
12. In 1998 there was no hospitalization due to infectious disease or malnutrition. Because of the rural
nature of the municipality, agro-toxins have been a major problem. The PSF, in partnership with the
- 92 -



state Secretary of Agriculture and Environment, developed a successful program of environmental
education, and with safe warehouses were built. As a result, the incidence of environmental diseases
has also decreased.
Institutional challenees. The rapid expansion of the program is a measure of its success. In about five
years, the PSF was implemented in 1,693 municipalities (of 5,506), with 4,405 teams, and covered about
17 million Brazilians.
Notwithstanding its achievements, the PSF has at least three main challenges: (a) it is a very labor intensive
program in a situation that is characterized by heavy fiscal constraints; (b) it requires a strong cohesion
between the Federal level, which orients the program, and the municipal level, which implements it; and (c)
it requires a clear mapping of families to health teams, an action that is difficult to achieve in populated
municipalities characterized by migration, spatial mobility and distance between place of residence and
place of working.
Social development outcomes. Social development outcomes of the PSF include: (a) alleviation of
inequities in health outcomes as a result of improved access to health interventions; (b) civil society
strengthening by the incorporation of civil society organizations, service providers, universities and
research institutions, and communities as partners in the institutional arrangements of the project; (c)
gender equity by the enhancement of women's health status as a result of comprehensive interventions
towards families understood as the unit for attention; (d) enhancement of social risk management by
strengthening information of families and communities; and (e) improvement of the country's safety net by
strengthening the health care system and risk management.
Participatory Approach
The PSF is an on-going program and its implementation has been accompanied by a variety of
consultation mechanisms with key social actors. For the preparation of the proposed project, specific
participatory evaluations also were conducted to identify reasons underlying successful and disappointing
PSF experiences. Project design has benefited from extensive consultation through workshops, group
discussions, and qualitative and quantitative instruments with families, Community Health Agents, nurses
and physicians. Given the family focus of the project, women have been given specific attention in the
qualitative work, and a diagnosis of the health status of women was undertaken. The project design also
benefited from several evaluations of the PSF, through staff, teams and its beneficiaries. For example,
Nuicleo de Estudos de Politicas Nblicas - Avaliacao Qualitativa de Programas Sociais Prioritarios;
UNICAMP (Campinas, 1999); Ana Luisa de Avila Viana, 0 Programa de Sauide da Familia no Brasil
(Ministerio da Saude, 2000); Niucleo de Estudos Sociais em Sauide/Fundacao Oswaldo Cruz, Avaliacao da
Implementacao do Programa de Sauide da Familia em Grandes Centros Urbanos - Vitoria da Conquista e
Goiania (Ministerio da Saiide, 2000). Some of the most important inputs from several consultations and
surveys that took place since the beginning of its implementation, and as part of the preparation of the
project follow:
On the positive side
* Access has been expanded. Although many of those consulted were not familiar with the program, and
some did not even know it exists, many respondents noticed a change in the approach of health services
provision. Community Health Agents have in fact expanded access through the provision of services
directly to families in their own houses (focus groups in Lauro de Freitas in the metropolitan region
of Salvador, Bahia, and Ilheus, Bahia).
- 93 -



* Specialized attention has been facilitated. When more specialized attention is needed, Community
Health Agents schedule appointments themselves. In this way, the system is not overloaded with
unnecessary demand, but patients requiring care have a much higher chance of receiving it (focus
groups in Lauro de Freitas in the metropolitan region of Salvador, Bahia, and Ilheus, Bahia).
* Time has been saved. In general, beneficiaries stress that before the PSF they did not have time to
look for health services, but now services reach out directly to them (focus groups in Lauro de Freitas
in the metropolitan region of Salvador, Bahia, and Ilheus, Bahia).
* Dignity and self esteem have improved. As one client mentioned, "There are some people who come
to our house, this is wonderful, no? One of them is a physician, a real physician who has come to my
house.. .this has never happened in our lives before. Where would we go before? In the city, we needed
to take buses." (focus groups in Lauro de Freitas in the metropolitan region of Salvador, Bahia, and
Ilheus, Bahia).
On the negative side
* Unfulfilled expectations have been created. In a highly medicalized culture, where health is
understood as lack of disease, medication and interventions are understood as a sign of attention.
Frustrations accompany the fact that the Community Health Agent is not a physician and may not
prescribe any medicine or follow-up medical interventions. Sometimes he will only talk about
reproductive care or nutrition. As one woman in Ilheus has mentioned, "Now there is no pediatrician in
the health facility"(there is generalist physician instead). Or, as another complained, "I do not even go
to this doctor, I take my son directly to the city."
* Increase in the demand for coverage has been difficult to control. Once families know they have a
team assigned to their houses, very often they begin to "import" family members and friends from other
areas, with the expectation that they would get the same attention. Community Health Agents mention
(and complain) that they face unexpectedly high demand for their services that they cannot refuse to
meet but that compromise the quality of their work. There are cases, as in Santa Catarina, where the
number of teams is insufficient and, each team is covering the twice the number of families assigned to
it.
* Dependence on political will and leadership capacity at the municipal level. Because of its design,
existence of the program is not automatically guaranteed once implementation begins. Indeed, it is
heavily dependent on the commitment of local leaders and officials with community based approaches
for public policies. In some cases, as in Vit6ria da Conquista (Bahia) and Volta Redonda (Rio de
Janeiro), the PSF has been very successful due to the stability of the political administration - the same
political party has won three consecutive elections - and the Municipal Health Secretary is a physician
who shares the same values of the program. In others, where there are too many changes in
govemment, progress has been slower.
* Requirement for a new kind of physician reveals barriers in medical school training. The medical
profession in Brazil is organized in specialized fields that do not respond to the generic needs of the
PSF. The switch from a specialist to a generalist faces cultural barriers from the side of families and
doctors, and requires costly training.
Gender. Because of their central role within families, women are disproportionately reached by the PSF
- 94 -



compared to men). Health indicators in Brazil show a recent improvement, but these improvements have
been particularly felt (and measured) by women and children whose fertility and mortality rates have
decreased. Moreover, teen pregnancies persists as a health problem and HIV/AIDs has increased more
among women than among men.
The MOH has several programs that address the needs of women, including HIV/ALDs interventions, the
Women's Health Program, the Breast-feeding Program, and the Program Against Breast and Cervical
Cancer. Notwithstanding these programs, the PSF has assumed a very central role for women, because of
its outreach capacity. In some municipalities - for example in Campina Grande (Paraiba) -- maternal
mortality in hospitals decreased to zero. In Sobral (Ceara), coverage for pregnant women is 100 percent,
and includes visits to families, at least six visits to physicians, nurseries and follow-up for babies and
children.
Barros, E. A Politica da Atencao a Sauide de Mulheres e Criancas no Brasil: algumas informacoes basicas.
(Ministerio da Saude, unpublished, prepared for the PSF program, 2001).In Sobral (CearA), coverage for
pregnant women is 100 percent, and includes visits to families, at least
Conclusions and recommendations. All the studies and consultations undertaken for the preparation of
the FHEP corroborate its importance and design. The following are specific recommendations from the
social assessment, the majority of which have already been proposed by the MOH and/or being used by
some municipalities (and should be expanded to others):
(a) Given the PSF's labor-intensive characteristics, to create economies of scale and avoid pressures on
municipal budgets, it is important to incorporate and provide training to NGOs and other civil
organizations for the hiring and monitoring of Community Health Agents and other team staff.
(b) In larger municipalities, where living and working spaces are far away and people leave their homes
very early in the morning and return late in the evening, the matching of teams and families should be
fine-tuned and implemented through participatory community diagnosis.
(c) Family Health Teams should be well trained on reproductive health issues, which should be dealt with
the delicacy it deserves, particularly when dealing with teens.
(d) For reasons of efficacy and costs, the several programs already dealing with women's health require
municipal coordination.
(e) Participatory evaluations, already used with frequency, should be discussed and understood by
policy-makers at all levels. Although significant resources have been spent on such evaluation, there
are indications that their dissemination has been fragmented.
(f) Although poor health indicators often are mentioned with regard to the black and "parda" families,
there are no specific remedies to address the issue. The program lacks studies and data to indicate
whether or not specific interventions are needed.
(g) The generalist skills required from doctors need to be better understood, accepted and valued by
themselves, by families, and eventually by medical schools.
- 95 -



Annex 2C
Brazil - Family Heath Project
Environmental Assessment
This annex reports on an analysis of potential adverse environmental impacts of the Brazil Family Health
Extension Project, and proposes actions to minimize them during project implementation. It also highlights
the expected positive environmental impacts of some features of the project. The analysis was conducted
by Pan American Health Organization (PAHO), in cooperation with the Brazilian Ministry of Health.
Appendix 1 presents a classification of waste materials, indicating for each type of residue whether the
various items are generated in the type of basic health care units to be supported under the project.
Appendix 2 presents the results of a case study on cost estimates for implementation of Health Services
Waste Management System. The full report is available in the project files.
IDENTIFICATION OF POTENTIAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
The FHEP has potential for generating both positive and negative environmental impacts. The negative
impacts could result from expansion of the system of basic health units. At the same time, however, the
project has enormous potential for producing positive impacts. This is evident in the very concept of the
project-moving health teams into local communities, and identifying and addressing environmental factors
that could threaten the health of the population. This effort will reduce the environmental degradation in
these communities.
ImDacts on Basic Health Care Units. The project aims to increase basic health care coverage of the
urban population through adjustments in the physical structure and administrative organization of services
(Component 1). Although there is no precise estimate of the dimensions of the alterations to the physical
network through incorporation of new properties and organizational adaptation existing facilities, it is clear
that growth must be significant to reach the targets of increasing coverage to 70 percent of the
municipalities with populations between 100,000 and 500,000, and 50 percent of those with populations of
more than 500,000. Expansion of the PSF also will demand important changes in the practices adopted at
the basic health service units.
In a 1999, the MOH evaluated the population's access to the health services provided by Family Health
Teams within the territory of a particular basic health unit. The results (shown in part below) provide an
idea of the large positive impact that could be generated through basic health units as a consequences of the
expansion of the Family Health Program
*  from 43.8 to 93.7 percent in prenatal care utilization;
*  from 53.1 to 98.3 percent in the utilization of the Child Healthcare Cards in monitoring child growth
and development;
* from 16.1 to 67.5 percent in collection of material by nurses for preventive neck of uterus cancer;
*  from 13.9 to 96 percent in registration of patients with high blood pressure, and from 34.8 to 98.5
percent in treatment;
*  from 12.7 to 93.9 percent in registration of diabetic patients, and from 32.5 to 97 percent in treatment;
*  from 14.8 to 87.3 percent in active searching for patients with tuberculosis.
In terms of potential impact, there are two distinct phases:
- 96 -



*  In the first stage of implementation of the FHEP, demand for specialized outpatient services will
increase because the program will represent a new channel of access to medical services for a
population that did not previously have such access. In this stage, the potential negative impacts are
most highly concentrated in increased generation of waste material at the health units, with the
consequent need for more effective controls. For the most part, these measures involve implementation
of a System of Waste Management that includes minimization of generation plus segregation of waste.
One should also be attentive to the risks that exist in terms of the safety of both patients and
employees. New systems are required to control infection and work accidents. Particular attention
should be given to the possibility of atmospheric pollution and waste generation resulting from the use
of various types of equipment, coupled with measures aimed at controlling the risk of fire and other
emergencies related to the systems of energy utilized. These items are discussed in the document
"Hospital Environment Security," issued by the MOH, to be used as a reference in the implementation
of these programs at health care units.
*  Over the medium-tern, demand for urgent and emergency services will be reduced as health services
are improved; in addition, the number of hospitalizations due to avoidable causes also is expected to
decline. These changes will bring about positive environmental impacts: a decrease in the volume of
waste generated, together with improved health conditions for the population. At the same time, the
waste generated as a result of the current system of acquiring and utilizing medicine products is also
expected to decline, with a lesser volume of expired medicines and implementation of an effective
System of Waste Management.
PROPOSALS ON ENVIRONMENTAL PROCEDURES AT BASIC HEALTHCARE UNITS
The proposed environmental procedures to be used in the framework of the FHEP aim to minimize negative
environmental impacts. These include:
Stratezv and Pilot Prop-ram: As outpatient health service units generate approximately 80 percent less
waste material than large hospitals, and the waste produced at such facilities is considerably less
dangerous, there is a need for simplified waste management procedures suited to the specific circumstances
of basic health care units. Under Component 2 the Borrower will develop a national strategy guidelines,
manual and training modules to aid in the task of managing the waste generated within basic health care
units. The purpose of the manual will be to orient all activities targeted at minimizing the impact of waste
generation at these units. The training module will focus on the effective implementation of waste
management practices, utilizing the waste management manual for basic health care units. The manual and
training modules will be field tested with Family Health Teams in a sample of municipalities. The program
will be gradually expanded in a systematic fashion during future APL phases.
Recommendations for elaboration of a waste management manual for basic health care units.
A manual is to be elaborated to aid in the task of managing the waste generated by basic health care units.
The purpose of this manual will be to orient all activities targeted at minimizing the impacts of waste
generation at these units. This manual should serve as the basis for the minimum procedures to be adopted,
including:
* Characterization of waste material - determination of the volume and quality of the waste generated in
each area of activity, utilizing the classification system issued in IBAMA Resolution 05/93. (The
strategic activities carried out at basic healthcare units, defined according to the terms of NOAS,
- 97 -



already indicate the characteristics of the waste material generated, making it necessary to quantify the
amount produced in each unit.);
*  Segregation - separation of the waste material at the level of the generating unit to avoid contamination
of common waste as a result of contact with infectious waste;
*  Minimization - the volume of waste is to be minimized as a result of more efficient utilization of
material and medicines, while avoiding generation of expired medicine products;
*  Packing - each specific type of waste is to be placed in plastic bags, receptacles or other types of
appropriate packing, according to the characteristics of the waste in question;
* Internal collection and transportation - appropriate movement from the site of generation to the place
of storage;
* Storage - the waste is to be stored under safe and sanitary conditions until external collection and final
disposal.
Recommendations for training in health service waste management. The World Bank (2000) has
developed a proposal for management of waste at small scale health care units, which was used as the basis
of this study. To elaborate and implement a Waste Management Plan, it is essential that those responsible
for the health care unit recognize the importance of good management techniques and designate a special
team to assume supervisory responsibility over the plan. This can be done either by naming a specific team
or taking advantage of an existing group charged with controlling hospital infection.  The waste
management group should at least include the manager of the health care unit and representatives of the
medical doctors, nursing staff, administrative sector and janitorial services. The Waste Management Plan is
to be elaborated by the entire team working within the framework of the ongoing daily activities of the unit.
Aside from providing specific training for the team, the entire staff should be familiarized with the terms of
the plan and aware of each person's role in executing the plan. Training for implementation must include:
*  Basic information on Health Service Waste and awareness of the risks consequent upon inadequate
management of this material;
*  Basic information on the Waste Management Plan;
*  Roles and responsibilities of each employee in the process of waste management;
*  Technical instructions on the measures described in the Plan.
Viewed under this prism, the Project should include a specific training model for waste management
targeted at effective implementation, utilizing the Manual cited above as the basic reference.
CONCLUSIONS
The PSF Project does not involve any large-scale engineering projects. However, it does call for expansion
of the infrastructure network utilizing leased properties. Consequently, the potential for generating negative
impacts as a result of increased generation of waste materials in the early stage of implementation is
considered low. Over the medium-term, control measures are designed to foster improvements in the
current conditions of the basic health care units by reducing demand for services and adequate management
of the generated waste.
To control the negative environmental impacts that would result from increased generation of waste
material, studies carried out by the Brazilian and international scientific community have clearly
demonstrated that the most efficacious solution is adoption of a Waste Management System. This process
depends on a number of factors: authorities must place a high priority on this undertaking; well-trained
- 98 -



teams must be formed and provided with the necessary managerial instruments. Therefore, if the proposed
environmental procedures are incorporated into the project with the dual aims of providing needed
information and enhancing the awareness required for correct formulation and implementation of Waste
Management Plans at all of the PSF facilities, negative impacts will be mitigated and the project will bring
about a sharp reduction in existing problems.
When one considers the emphasis that the FHEP has ascribed to the formation and training of teams and to
development and implementation of adequate managerial instruments, it is clear that the project will make it
possible not only to reduce possible negative impacts, but also to greatly enhance the expected positive
impacts.
Therefore, one can conclude that the environmental impacts of project implementation can be controlled
through adoption of relatively simple measures, and that the positive impact of the project on quality of life
and health for the Brazilian population fully justify implementation.
- 99 -



Appendix 1. Classification of the Waste Stream
The classification below is based on specific nature of the waste material generated. The National Council
of the Environment (CONAMA), presents of waste classification into four categories: Group A is waste
considered a potential risk to public health and the environment due to the presence of biological agents;
Group B is waste considered a potential risk due to chemical characteristics; Group C is waste such as
radioactive materials; and Group D is common waste material.

Infectious waste: 1.28 kg/occupied bed/day
Common waste: 1.89 kg/occupied bed/day
Total waste: 3.17 kg/occupied bed/day (40 percent infectious, 60 percent common).
Costs
In intra-hospital management:
Acquisitions of material - approximately R$ 10,000.00 (140 trash collectors in dimensions of 25,
50 and 100 liters; 2 carts for collection and 10 containers for 240, 660 and 1000 liters for storage
in the external shelter)
Consulting - characterization of waste and planning of activities - and management training
approximately R$ 9,000.00 (300 hours at R$ 30.00 per hour)
Costs of collection and final disposal in municipal landfill: R$ 82.00 per ton of healthcare service
solid waste.
- 101 -



Annex 2D
Brazil: Family Health Project
Institutional Arrangements and Project Management
A.     Cooperation with other Agencies and Institutions
The project will be implemented by the Department of Basic Health (DAB), within the Secretariat of
Health Policies in the Ministry of Health, in partnership with State and Municipal Secretariats of Health.
See Attachment 1 of this Annex for a description of federal, state and municipal roles regarding the PSF.
The states and municipalities actively participate in defining health care policies, in implementing the
FHEP and in articulating political support and strategies to implement and consolidate a sustainable and
integrated health network. To strengthen and facilitate project implementation, there will be a need to
enhance partnerships and cooperation with other institutions to provide technical, scientific and operational
support. The following institutions will have an active role in the implementation of the FHEP and the
PSF activities:
* National Council of State Health Secretaries (CONASS): all states are represented in the Council, with
the objective to articulate and define health policies to be implemented at the state level.
* Council for Municipal Health Secretaries (CONASEMOH): in accordance with the constitutional
principle of decentralization, all municipalities are represented in the council to articulate and define
health policies and programs to be implemented at the municipal level;
*  Tri-Partite Health Management Commission (CIT):  representing federal. state and municipal
governments meet periodically to discuss and define the guiding principles, strategies and institutional
policies to be considered by the Ministry of Health;
* Bi-Partite Health Management Commissions (CIB) - meet periodically to discuss and guide general
and operational strategies related to health institutional policies for the State Secretariats of Health.
* Universities and Research Institutions will actively participate in the preparation of health professionals
(mainly physicians, nurses and related professionals, with specialty in family health) by providing
in-service training for existing health professionals, adapting their curricula to offer family health care
as a specialty in the medical schools, and in implementing the training, internship and scholarship
programs financed under the project. These institutions will also promote operational research,
evaluations and specific studies to generate new products to improve the implementation of the FHEP
and the PSF delivery systems, processes and activities.
*  Government and non-government institutions (NGOs) such as community and social organizations,
Ministries of Labor and Education will provide support, and facilitate the application of pertinent
legislations, protocols and agreements that are necessary for implementing the overall FHEP and PSF
specific activities.
The FHEP will also seek the cooperation of international agencies - UNICEF, PAHO/WHO, UNESCO
and others, to provide technical assistance, and to participate in relevant project activities.
- 102-



B.     Proiect Implementation Arrangements - Management Unit
Federal Level. A Project Coordination Unit (PCU) will be established within the Secretariat of Health
Policies and will respond to the Director of the Department of Basic Health Care (DAB) for the
implementation of the PSF. It will work closely with the existing organizational structure of management,
technical, human resources and evaluation in the DAB which is in charge of the implementation of the
FHEP at all levels. The main function of the PCU is to manage all aspects related to project
implementation, to guarantee that project components are executed according to terms and agreed
timetable. It will be staffed by individuals who have had experience in implementing the FHEP or other
Bank-funded projects. The results of an institutional analysis carried out by the Secretariat of Health
Policies (SPS) and the DAB showed the need for strengthening the Department with additional qualified
staff, and for providing training to existing staff to facilitate internal coordination and implementation of
project activities.
The Family Health Project would finance these new staff, the functioning of the PCU and additional
support staff for the Unit The PCU will have the following functional structure (see Attachment 2 for
Organizational Chart):
1.   PCU General Manager. Planning Specialist, assisted by one professional, will have the responsibility
for overseeing overall project implementation activities, will keep the authorities in the MOH and
municipalities informed on progress made in project implementation, assess project's constraints and
take measures to overcome the constraints.
2.   Technical Management Group. Coordinated by a Public Health Specialist, responsible for planning,
monitoring and implementing project components, and for providing technical assistance to
participating municipalities and agencies to implement project activities. The group will be assisted
by professionals specialized in planning, public health and related areas working in the PCU and
within the DAP structure.
3.   Administrative.  Procurement  and  Financial Management Group.  Coordinated  by  an
administrator/economist, responsible for carrying out procurement, financial management and
administration of project activities, including: agreements and contracts (convenios), procurement,
transfers of funds to implementing agencies, preparation of reports on financial performance and
disbursements. It will also be responsible for tracking project funds, contracting annual audits and to
provide technical assistance in financial management and procurement to participating municipalities
and agencies. The group will be staffed with experienced professionals to help the municipalities and
other executing agencies in implementing the PSF activities.
4.   Information Systems Mana,gement Group. Coordinated by a Systems Analyst, responsible for
developing, adapting or refining and implementing the information system required to monitor the
physical and financial performance of project implementation. The group. would have one additional
systems analyst and programmers to develop or adapt and implement the required project information
system at the municipal level.
5.   Projects Specialist . Coordinated by an administrator, and one additional professional, who would
liaise and follow-up special sub-projects in the SPS, related to project implementation with other
departments or Secretariats within the Ministry of Health, to ensure consistency in the
implementation of health policies and programs. The specialist would be answered directly to the
-103-                I



SPS Secretary, but would maintain close relationship with the PCU, and participate in monitoring the
activities carried out with project funds.
6.   Advisorv Group. The DAB/PCU will coordinate a group of experienced professionals or specialists
who would be hired with project funds on a short term basis, as required, to assist the DAB and
participating municipalities or agencies in implementing the Conversion Plan and other project
components (e.g. technical assistance for institutional modernization, procurement, financial
management, training and information systems).
C. Responsibilities of the PCU
The PCU will have the following main responsibilities:
(a) Promote the project at federal, state and municipal levels, particularly, in the participating
municipalities, institutions and agencies, to ensure a coherent and timely project implementation;
(b) Provide technical assistance to eligible municipalities for the preparation of the Municipal
Conversion Plans and review them prior to their approval by the DAP/ PSF managers;
(c) Approve jointly with DAB the Conversion Plans, and provide technical assistance to municipalities
for the preparation of procurement and financial management plans to implement the approved
Conversion Plan;
(d) Manage administrative and financial resources as reflected in the Loan Agreement; including
maintaining project records and preparing regular implementation reports;
(e) Ensure that municipalities and participating institutions comply with procurement guidelines and
financial management standards as defined in the Operational Manual (OM);
(f) Prepare terms of reference for the hiring of consultants who will assist in implementing project
activities, supervise the consultants' work, review and approve their products, according
procedures defined in the OM.
(g) Coordinate the processing (identification, preparation, appraisal, approval by the DAB/PCU and
supervision) of sub-projects, presented by participating municipalities or institutions, in
compliance with the OM procedures;
(h) Ensure the auditing of project accounts as agreed with the World Bank;
(i) Adapt and operate an information system to track and monitor project performance at all levels;
(j) Organize the monitoring of project's performance indicators and contract impact evaluations as
required under the PSF.
(k) Prepare periodic reports and participate in Bank supervision missions.
- 104-



D.     Operational Manual
An Operational Manual (OM) will detail procedures for monitoring of compliance with performance
benchmarks, reporting, procurement, financial management, disbursement, and auditing procedures for
each of the components. The OM would also contain: a Project Implementation Plan for Components 1, 2
and 3; eligibility criteria to select municipalities, performance indicators, and a draft (blue print) of the
Portaria related to the approval of the Planos de Conversao and Pacto de Adesao. The issuance of the final
version of the OM will be a condition for effectiveness.
E.     Proiect Implementation - Municipal Level
At the municipal level, a small Project Unit would be organized within the Municipal Secretariat of Health,
to ensure timely project implementation. The Unit would work closely with the PCU, and would have the
following functions: (a) coordinate the preparation of annual working and procurement plans among the
FHEP teams to be submitted to the DAB/PCU, as defined in the OM; (b) procure, or monitor the
procurement and delivery of goods and services procured with Project fund; (c) ensure the maintenance of
accounting records for these purchases and provide information to the PCU during audits and reviews; (d)
prepare progress reports as required by the OM; (e) monitors Project implementation. A more detailed
description of the implementation arrangements at the municipal level, as well as, indicators and targets for
each component will be specified in the Operational Manual (OM).
-105 -



Attachment 1 of Annex 2D
Family Health Program: Institutional Structure and Responsibilities
This annex provides a brief overview of the roles and responsibilities of the federal, state and municipal
health systems for the Family Health Program (PSF). The PSF is a relatively new program and the
institutional structure along with definitions of responsibilities of the three levels of government are still
evolving. The program was initiated at the federal level as an important vehicle for carrying out SUS
principles (universality, equity, integrality) and achieving four out of six national health priorities including
reorientation of the health care model towards primary care. The majority of funds for the PSF come from
the federal government and are transferred directly to participating municipalities. Consequently, the
functions of these two levels are more clearly delineated. Early in the development of PSF the states'
responsibilities were limited to coordination and some technical assistance. However, with the passage of
new decentralization regulations (NOAS 01/01) and financing laws (EC29), their role is growing. It is
likely to grow further as performance monitoring and accreditation evolve.
The Federal Health Ministry (MOH) is responsible for:
*  Policy-making to support the success of PSF strategies including the development of incentives,
regulations, and guidelines for PSF implementation; definition of strategic areas and priorities for PSF
training and service delivery; elaboration of norms and protocols for PSF health care practices, and
oversight of PSF training and education.
*  Regulating PSF operations through the passage of a series of MOH regulations (portarias).
*  Federal funding of municipal PSF through the tripartite mechanism including development of necessary
methods for implementation and maintenance of PSF units, definition of priorities for allocation of
federal funds, and negotiation with the tripartite commission of prerequisites for municipal funding and
implementation.
*  Providing technical assistance directly to municipal PSF as well as to states for the implementation
and management of the program. This role is relatively new and national program staff size has
increased, especially in the past year, to support it. The division of responsibility between the state and
national programs is still emerging.
*  Monitoring PSF activities and results, and their impact on health by utilizing data collected by the
information system (SLAB). The national PSF helps states and municipalities set up the necessary
information systems and assure PSF unit registration in SIA/SUS. It controls the compliance of states
and municipalities in fulfilling their obligatory monthly information requirements, consolidates and
analyzes data, and disseminates information.
*  Evaluating the various components of the PSF including health care practices and outcomes at the PSF
unit, municipal, and state levels, and PSF training and education. The evaluation role is evolving
rapidly. The first normative evaluation of all PSF units is currently underway. The national PSF is
also responsible for evaluating PSF training institutions, p6los de capacitacdo, and promoting
innovative curricula for universities for post-graduate PSF training.
*  Coordinating with other MOH programs that address PSF issues and practices, as well as other
sectors. It plays an important role in linking universities and providers for permanent and continuous
training, formation and education of PSF human resources. The national PSF contributes to the
creation of a national/ regional network for sharing experiences and gaining knowledge in family
health. It also develops partnerships with international aid organizations, government and
non-government organizations, and the private sector.
- 106 -



Municipalities implementing the PSF are responsible for:
* Providing funding for PSF in accordance with NOAS 01/01.
*  Municipal planning and decision-making for the prevention and resolution of prevalent health
problems. This includes prioritizing the areas that will benefit from PSF expansion.
*  Implementing PSF units including preparation and maintenance of infrastructure, assurance of
necessary supplies and resources (examining rooms, pharmacy, minor surgery), availability of
information systems and a functioning referral system for higher level care.
*  Managing day to day operations including responsibility for hiring and salaries, maintenance, and
supplies.
*  Responsibility for day to day management of particular units or catchment areas is sometimes
contracted out to NGOs, particularly in larger municipalities.
*  Monitoring and evaluating PSF team/unit activities, results and impact; providing information on a
monthly basis to the state and federal PSF through SLAB and other information systems (mandatory
requirement for inclusion in PSF).
*  Assuring continuing education for PSF personnel.
*  Promoting community participation and social control within the health care system through the
municipal health councils and the PSF. Municipal health plans are submitted for approved to the
municipal health councils.
In reality, the capacity of municipalities to fulfill these roles varies tremendously. The focus has been on
implementation of PSF units in order to increase coverage rapidly and become eligible for the associated
financial incentives.
Definition of state responsibilities regarding the PSF is still evolving. The operational norms of 1996 gave
states a large role in system coordination and monitoring. The passage of NOAS/01 in 2001 further
clarified state organizational and management roles, particularly their key responsibility for the
development and implementation of regional referral networks that involve more than one municipality. To
a certain extent, state responsibilities mimic those of the national PSF, except they pertain to the state level
(e.g. Policy-making, regulation, technical assistance). However, they seem to have little role in the
implementation of PSF units. Specific state responsibilities include:
*  A small part of PSF funding through the tripartite mechanism.
*  Technical assistance and certification of municipalities for "full management of primary care" (gestdo
plena da aten,do basica), a prerequisite for participation in the PSF.
*   Development, implementation and regulation of referral networks (regionalizag,o) for secondary and
tertiary care that involve more than one municipality. This includes the diagnostic / laboratory network.
*  Organization and monitoring of the epidemiologic surveillance system. The PSF unit is one of the basic
providers of information for this system.
*  Oversight of universities that are primarily state institutions. They will therefore have an important role
in the oversight and monitoring of training and education provided by training centers, known as p6los
de capacitaado.
*  Evolving policy, monitoring and regulatory functions in conjunction with the bipartite commission.
*  Coordination with the national PSF in establishing norms and guidelines for the PSF, linkage of
municipal information systems with those at the national level, and coordination with other government
and nongovernmental agencies.
-107 -

Annex 2E
Brazil: Family Health Project
Monitoring and Evaluation
A Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) system will assess performance of the APL program and each of the
its three phases in meeting objectives and achieving the desired outcomes and outputs. It will collect and
analyze data for the indicators in the logical framework and other variables needed to monitor project
activities. The M&E system aims to answer the following questions: (i) Does the program achieve its
development objectives? (ii) Have outcome indicators improved in program areas, and are improvements
greater than in similar areas not covered by the program; (iii) Has the program contributed to improved
effectiveness of primary care providers and improved efficiency of the health care system; (iv) Has the
program built capacity and strengthened municipal management systems and PSF training institutions; (v)
Do program outcomes vary across groups of intended beneficiaries across regions? If so, what factors
contribute to this variation? (vi) what would have happened in the absence of the project (vii) Are there
unintended effects of the program, either positive or negative? (viii) Can program design be altered to
improve impact? and (ix) Did the program justify its cost?
Integrated project and national M&E systems
An important goal of the program is to contribute significantly to the strengthening of the Basic
Health/Family Health Program (Atenqdu Basica/Programa Saude da Familia) (AB/PSF) information and
M&E systems and thus improve municipal performance and the performance of individual family health
providers. Program (and project) M&E will depend on and be intimately related to the AB/PSF M&E
system. The borrower is highly motivated and committed to M&E, and considers improving national and
municipal M&E systems a priority.
Indicators used in the current AB/PSF information system have been elaborated by the national PSF
program to monitor municipal performance and progress in attaining desired health goals. There are 49
indicators and most assess the impact of AB/PSF programs on health outcomes. However, the national
system does not  include the output and process measures necessary for downstream municipal
decision-making to improve performance of programs that are not achieving desired goals. Municipalities
are responsible for AB/PSF implementation and management at the local level. However few, if any, have
developed an M&E system that measures performance of AB/PSF providers. The Phase 1 project will
support strengthening municipal abilities to monitor PSF performance by employing well-designed data
collection instruments to gather reliable information and utilize it for effective management.
Municipal data are transmitted to the national AB/PSF M&E system which in tum will be an important
source of information for monitoring and evaluating the project. Conversely, parts of the project M&E
would form the basis for AB/PSF M&E that currently do not exist, e.g. monitoring, evaluation and
accreditation of PSF training institutions; and monitoring and evaluation of the effectiveness of
referral/counter-referral systems. Consequently, the project M&E will be closely integrated with the
national AB/PSF M&E system. Many of the project indicators are identical to or related to AB/PSF
indicators, but unlike current indicators that are aggregated at the municipal and state levels, are more
oriented to the delivery level. Other aspects of the project M&E will eventually be incorporated into the
AB/PSF M&E and continue beyond the life of the program.
Monitoring: The project will support the design and implementation of a computerized management
information system (MIS) to track progress on activities, products and expenditures during implementation.
- 109-



Drawing on the logical framework the project MIS will collect data on key indicators including inputs,
outputs, processes and outcomes. It will track compliance with training and population targets set for the
extension of coverage by the PSF, and the quantity and quality of the training and the health care services
provided. In Phase I, the project MIS also includes monitoring progress on several key health outcome
indicators (including immunization, prenatal care, and hospitalization rates for ARI).
The project MIS will also permit project personnel to monitor targets that assess progress towards
implementing stronger PSF information and M&E systems at the municipal level. Two major systems will
be developed and tested in participating municipalities by the end of the (Phase I) project and implemented
in all participating municipalities by the end of the program: an M&E system that integrates assessments of
the 'pactos' (federal-state-municipal performance agreements), the implementation of the municipal health
plans and the referral/counter-referral system; and a performance based management system for primary
health care providers. During Phase I the project MIS will track the development and piloting of these
AB/PSF systems.
Project monitoring will draw on the project MIS as well as the information systems currently supporting
AB/PSF M&E including SIAB (Primary Care Info System), SIH (Hospital Info System), SIS-Prenatal
(Prenatal Care Info System), and Vital records. In addition, information for monitoring will be provided by
specific evaluation studies and project reports. In the second and third phases of the program, information
will also be provided by the newly developed municipal M&E and provider performance based
management systems.
Evaluation: Beyond aspects related to financial execution, program evaluation will be increasingly related
over time to the MOH's AB/PSF evaluation. By the end of the program in 2008, the project MIS will be
integrally linked to the AB/PSF M&E. The project both develops capacity at the municipal level to provide
good quality information for use in evaluation, and then uses that information for purposes of evaluating
the project. The goal is a sustainable system that provides the municipal, state and national managers with
the information they need to evaluate the impact of the AB/PSF program on health outcomes.
During Phase l, studies will be carried out evaluating 50% of the PSF teams and 15 PSF training
institutions implemented between 1997 and 2001. This information will provide information for the design
of M&E in Phase 1 as well as for the expansion of PSF training. These evaluation studies will also provide
critical information for Phase 2 which will focus greater attention on the quality and accreditation of both
training institutions and PSF providers. As noted above, Phase 1 will lay the groundwork for the
implementation of information systems that are necessary for the municipal health system to assess the
performance of its providers, and for the project and the national MOH to evaluate management by the
municipal health systems.
An evaluation of the impact of the program on health outcomes will be based on a comparison of baseline
health indicators with those at the end of the program. Baseline indicators will be collected and analyzed
during Phase 1 of the program, supported through Component 3. Baseline and end-of-program indicators
will be collected for participating PSF catchment areas as well as for control areas. This will permit a
before and after comparison of program areas as well as potentially allow comparison with similar areas
that do not have PSF programs. By the end of Phase I baseline data will be available not only for project
indicators, but also for the newly developed municipal performance management systems (process and
outcome), as well as for the national system of evaluating progress through the 'pactos' (primarily
outcome). Availability of key baseline indicators in project municipalities will be a trigger for Phase 2.
However, in order to address some of the questions raised at the beginning of this appendix, special
evaluation studies may be required at the end of the seven-year program.
- 110-



Annex 2F
Performance Indicators for Municipal Conversion Plans
for passing from Phase I to Phase II
Urban municipalities with populations greater than 100,000 are eligible to submit plans for transforming
their primary health care system to the PSF model. Municipalities selected to participate in the project will
sign letters of agreement with the Health Ministry. Municipal conversions will mirror the project in that
they will proceed in three phases. The initial agreement signed will include the following trigger indicators
for passing from Phase I to Phase II. These indicators aim to: (i) monitor progress in the transformation of
the primary health care system to the PSF model, and (ii) set standards for acceptable fiduciary practice.
Technical Indicators
Fixed Indicators:
1.     25% increase in PSF coverage for municipalities with current coverage less than 25%.
2.     25%  increase in the percent of total AP/PSF personnel budget spent on PSF personnel in
municipalities with an current PSF population coverage of less than 25%.
3.     40% of maternity admission in PSF catchment areas are referred by PSF teams.
4.     PSF protocols for referrals/counter-referral and care practices for PSF strategic areas (women's
health, child health, TB, leprosy, hypertension, diabetes, oral health and epidemiologic
surveillance) developed and implemented for 50% of PSF teams.
5.     Appointment centers for consultations, admissions, and diagnostic and therapeutic assistance
established.
6.     Health teams make an average 9 household visits per family annually.
7.     Baseline data (household and provider) collected and analyzed in order to provide baseline
indicators for PSF performance, pacto de indicadores and project monitoring and evaluation.
8.     Performnance-based management system developed and piloted in a sample of PSF teams.
9.     100% of PSF teams formed in Phase I have received in-service training in the PSF strategic areas.
10.    100% of municipal management personnel and team coordinators have received training in
planning, management, and organization.
Variable Indicators (negotiable with municipalities):
1.        % percent increase in PSF coverage in municipalities with current coverage of greater than
25%.
2.        % percent increase in percent of total AP/PSF personnel budget spent on PSF personnel in
municipalities with current coverage of greater than 25%.
- 111 -



3.     _% reduction in number of hospital admission for children <5 from PSF catchment area.
Fiduciary Indicators
The participant municipalities will be excluded from the APL 2 if they exceed the following parameters:
Procurement:
I. No more than 30% of contracting activities in which awarded contracts are subject to judicial challenge
regarding procurement in the first year; no more than 20% in the second year
2. No more than 3 injunctions (liminares) suspending procurement processes during the Project
implementation; and
3. No more than two declared misprocurement actions during the Project implementation. In the local
procedures misprocurement will include: failure to award to then lowest evaluated bidder; lack of
records and information pertaining the bidding and award; disqualification of lowest price without a
sound reason; and deviations from the approved procurement plan without prior PCU and Bank
approval.
4. No more than one downgrade on the procurement risk classification during project implementation.
Financial Management:
1. No more than one downgrade on the financial management risk classification during project
implementation.
2. Comply with submission of financial management reports to PCU in at least 70 percent of cases
according to established due dates.
3. Provide evidence satisfactory to the Bank that appropriate actions have been taken to address auditors'
management letter recommendations during project implementation.
- 112-



Annex 2G
Brazil - Family Heath Project
Eligibility Criteria and Selection Process for Municipalities
Criteria:
The MOH will prepare verification indicators and apply a point system for approving the Conversion Plan
proposals received from interested municipalities. Final version of criteria and process will be included in
the Operational Manual. The MOH has formed a technical group to assist potential applicants.
1. Municipal population greater than 100,000*
2. Participation in decentralization process:*
*  agreement to seek certification as a Full Manager (Gestao Plena) in either Primary Care
or Municipal Health Systems, per MOH regulations (NOAS 01/01)
3. Presentation of municipal health plan that includes:*
* epidemiologic profile, service demands
* existing services, municipal and inter-municipal 'flows' between service levels
* infrastructure and operational capacity
* productivity data, availability of pacto' indicators
* positive and negative aspects of service organization, accessibility, management
* approval by the Municipal Health Council
4. Presentation of seven-year Conversion Plan in three phases that follows MOH guidelines (per manual)
and contemplates:
* coverage goals conform to MOH requirements (based on municipal size: 70% if pop. 100,000 -
500,000; 50% if >500,000); units, teams planned consistent with goals*
* primary health care infrastructure availability and needs
* local realities including capacity, demand, facilitating and limiting factors
* mechanisms to contract personnel for PSF*
*  coherent plans, based on evidence, for meeting needs in: secondary health care (specialists),
diagnostic capacity, therapeutic assistance (pharmaceuticals), transportation network*
* infrastructure and operational capacity of secondary referral centers (specialists), laboratories for
diagnostic assistance, and pharmaceutical capacity.
* organizational structure within municipal MOH for human resource development*
* adherence to performance agreement (pacto) on primary health indicators, Humanized Birth
Program, Cartdo SUS registration program*
* agreement to provide financial indicators required through SIOPS - the transparent Public Budget
Information System on Health*
* proposal for municipal counterpart funds and other types of municipal investments to assure
sustainability of infrastructure and services*
* investment priorities delineated
* demonstration of capacity in bidding processes, procurement and management
*  procurement plan and disbursement schedule.*
5. Agreement to carry-out baseline studies by means of household and provider surveys, collecting data
on social, health status, health seeking behaviors, spending, institutional and other aspects related to
PSF, conversion plan and NOAS/0l implementation.*
- 113 -



6. Agreement to implement fiduciary framework to strengthen financial and acquisitions management and
reporting according to risk assessment performed by PCU.*
Process:
MOH Technical Commission assesses documents in the following phases:
*  first round of eliminations based on eligibility criteria
* clarifications and refinement of remaining conversion plans
*  evaluation of conversion plans by the Bi-Partite Management Commission
*  final classification by the MOH based on point system
* municipalities that don't meet these requirements are automatically eliminated

Annex 4: Cost Benefit Analysis Summary
BRAZIL: FAMILY HEALTH EXTENSION PROGRAM
This annex presents the summary results of the cost-benefit analysis of the Brazil Family Health Extension
Project (FHEP), based upon the project's costs and the measurable economic benefits flowing from the
successful implementation of the proposed project. In addition to the project costs of US$550.0 million,
the analysis includes costs of the capital and recurrent expenditures related to the expansion and
improvement of health services. The expansion and improvement of health services is done through the
Family Health Model, which applies to all districts in the country. Initially, only 100 districts with a
population of 100,000 residents or more will be included. In summary, the project would yield a net
present value of benefits, after investment and recurrent costs, of more than US$1.8 billion, and produce an
internal rate of return (IRR) above 125 percent over a 10-year period.
The main results include:
Table 1. Summary of Estimated Costs and Benefits of the FHP

Benefit:Cost Ratio                   12.8
Note: NPV Benefits is equal to the direct and indirect benefits, minus
total project costs (in USSmillions)

IRR was calculated based on net benefits over 10 years
The FHEP will produce substantial savings over the medium to long-term, primarily through reductions in
hospital costs obtained by shifting demand from expensive hospital care to primary health care facilities.
These changes will reduce the number of hospitalizations as well the number of deaths, as the early
detection and improved treatment of illness reduces the burden of disease. This annex outlines the key
issues related to the economic analysis; the detailed analysis is contained in project files. Projected
recurrent expenditures generated by the project and the estimates of future healthcare spending show that
the project would be financially sustainable for Brazil. The ability to assume the recurrent costs depends to
a large extent on the government's ability to effectively implement EC no. 29 that effectively ensures that
health sector financing will grow in real terms at the same rate as GDP EC no. 29 modifies the formula for
calculating health sector budgets by establishing a floor for the 2000 budget, increasing health spending by
a minimum of nominal GDP growth from 2001 to 2004 and earmarks 12% of state revenues and 15% of
municipal revenues to financing health. .
Summary of Benefits and Costs:
The cost-benefit analysis distributes the benefits over this time horizon into two major groups: direct and
indirect benefits. The direct benefits are the expected tangible benefits accruable to the project from the cost
reduction due to a decline on the number of hospitalizations by the established main causes of morbidity.
Indirect benefits are related to the benefits obtained from the additional life-years for the population of the
targeted districts. The monetary value for this figure is calculated by projecting the present value of GDP
per capita over the total number of additional life-years obtained from the reduced mortality.
- 116-



The cost-benefit analysis assumes that the strengthening and changes made to the health care system will be
targeted to 84.5 million people, and that a ten-year period is required to reflect the flow of costs and
benefits. The project has three components: i) Support to Municipal Conversion Plan [**], ii) Human
Resource Development and iii) Monitoring and Evaluation.
The project focuses on the urban areas of Brazil, which are characterized by weak public health care
systems, and large numbers of families in extreme poverty, confronting severe social risk. Of the 223
municipalities with populations of 100,000 or greater in the country, approximately 100 will participate in
phase one of the project. The strategic aim is to achieve coverage of 70 percent of the population in
municipalities with a population of 100,000 to 500,000 inhabitants; and 50 percent coverage of the
population in cities with a population greater than 500,000 residents. All other municipalities-as well as
the rural areas of the country-will benefit from the activities under the second and third phases of the
project.
In terms of the costs of implementation, the analysis considers two basic elements: (i) the external
investment costs of the World Bank project; and (ii) the recurrent costs. The total cost of the project is of
US$1.9 billion. Of this amount, the World Bank would finance US$550 million and the GOB would
finance US$1.4 billion. The recurrent costs reach nearly US$480 million per year after the seventh year;
once the project is fully operational. This amount includes the recurrent and incremental costs of
investments in infrastructure, equipment and maintenance, as well as the cost of increase in coverage for
basic health services through the primary care network. Assuming a basic package of $8.
Savings in hospitalization are among the most important direct benefits of the project. These cost savings
will be realized through two sources: First, the project will result in reduced hospitalizations due to greater
capacity to resolve medical needs at the local level, thus reducing referrals to hospitals. Second, the project
will reduce risk factors in the target population that increase the demand for hospitalizations. Another
probable source of savings, not explicitly quantified in this study, is the reduction in hospitalization costs
caused by the increased efficiency of health networks due to improved management practices. The project
will shift healthcare provision from expensive hospitals to more efficient and higher quality primary care
centers. This shift will save over 1.3 million unnecessary hospitalizations throughout a 10-year period, and
produce a direct benefit of nearly US$50 million in nominal terms with a present value of US$27 million.
Indirect benefits are calculated based on the reduction of key causes of mortality present in the
municipalities selected. The reduction in mortality is taken as a base line assumption to calculate the
indirect benefits of the project. The mortality rates obtained at the national level will be used as the base
line rates for the municipalities selected. The target of reduced mortality rates will be calculated using the
average reduction rates obtained from the Contagem and Vitoria Da Conquista districts in the Dain et al
study (2001), as a benchmark. Targets of reduced mortality rates will also be based on the international
literature of cost-effectiveness, and according to expert opinion. To calculate the indirect benefits, the
additional life-years saved (attributable to mortality reductions) are then converted to monetary terms by
adding the present value of GDP per capita of the number of additional life-years obtained by each
individual. Table 2 summarizes the results of the analysis for each of the two scenarios.
