As shown in the table, benefits outweigh investments by a ratio or more than two to one. Over a 10-year
horizon, the present value of the project's cost is of nearly US$1.1 billion, while the present value of the
total benefits is estimated at more than US$3.1 billion, thus giving the project a net present value of
approximately US$1.9 billion. The net present value of the project over a five-year time frame is estimated
at US$204 million, thus demonstrating the robustness of the project. With the exception of the first three
years, the project is expected to generate annual savings to the system that outstrip the investment cost,
thereby paying for them. The internal rate of return (IRR) for the project is estimated at 125 percent,
reflecting the extremely high benefits relative to project costs. This ratio can be used to demonstrate the
value of this project relative to other projects in other sectors; any number lower than the discount rate (10
percent) would imply that the project should not be carried out.
Main Assumptions:
The following parameters are considered relevant in estimating the economic benefits of the proposed
project: the length of the project horizon and the time to impact the health of the population, the size of the
target population, the existing patterns of morbidity and mortality, the percentage decrease in mortality, and
the existing cost structure in the health sector. The assumptions are based on a study of the actual impact
of the family medicine model applied to two municipalities, in comparison with national averages for urban
municipalities. This study provides the basis for estimating targets in the reduction of morbidity and
mortality. Applying these changes to the expanding coverage of the model provides the
medium-to-long-term effect of the changes, represented in termns of reduced morbidity. Since these
assumptions are quantified for only a few proxy variables, rather than the full range of interventions and
effects, and the project horizon is only 10 years, results are conservative.
The analysis of the FHEP uses the following assumptions to measure the direct and indirect benefits:
* mortality from diabetes mellitus would be reduced by 20 percent; Based on results documented in
Jamison and Feacham's seminal work on "Disease Priorities in Developing Countries". The collection
of papers in this work document the effect of similar programs in terms of reductions in mortality and
morbidity; in addition, the papers include estimates of the cost-effectiveness of similar programs. For
the infant mortality rates the reduction rate was obtained from the study "Analise de Reestructuracao
dos Modelos Assistenciais de Sauide en Grandes Cidades: Padr6es de Custo e Formas de
Financiamento" elaborated by Sulamis Dain et al.
* mortality from infectious and parasitoid diseases would be reduced by 20 percent;
* mortality from circulatory diseases would be reduced by 20 percent;
* mortality from respiratory diseases would be reduced by 20 percent;
* infant mortality rate reduce from 35 to 23 per 1,000 live births;
* maternal mortality rate reduce from 63 to 51 mothers per 1,000 live births;
* mortality from malnutrition would be reduced by 20 percent;
* a 5.4 percent reduction on the hospitalizations from intra-cranial hemorrhages, cerebral infarction,
CVA not specified The target rates for the reduction of the main causes of mortality are based on the
average reduction rates calculated for the period 1998-2000, from the study of Sulamis Dain et al. The
municipalities considered in the study are taken like a benchmark reference.;
* a 35.5 percent reduction on hospitalizations for hypertension and other related diseases;
* a 12.1 percent reduction on hospitalizations for respiratory diseases;
* a 17.5 percent reduction on hospitalizations for diabetes mellitus;
* Benefits will be provided progressively throughout a six-year period;
- 119-



* The average cost of hospitalization is US$36.76 Obtained from the study of Sulamis Dain et al.;
* Health sector spending would increase in line with projected GDP growth from 2001 to 2007,
estimated at 5 percent nominal and 2 percent real per annum; and
*   Discount rate of 10 percent.
These assumptions are based on a detailed study of the implementation of the primary care model and of
the comparison between municipalities that implemented the family medicine program and control
municipalities where the program has not been implemented. The parameters are consistent with
international literature on similar projects. In the following sections, these assumptions will be used to
evaluate the benefits of the project and to assess, from an economic perspective, the impact of the project
on public finances. All target reductions in morbidity and mortality would be reached after six years.
Sensitivity analysis / Switching values of critical items:
While the above results are meaningful, it is important to test the robustness of the results with regard to
potential delays or reductions in benefits. This is done through the use of sensitivity analysis assuming
delays of two and three years, as well as a reduction in benefits of 20, 30 and 40 percent. The reduction in
benefits may be derived from a lower than projected impact on health indicators or from reaching fewer
people than the a priori estimates. The following table summarizes the results of the sensitivity analysis.
Table 3. Summary of Sensitivity Analysis
Type of Sensitivity Analysis  (USNmill,on)  (I)
Base case                          1,879.1     125%
2 years delay                      1,160.2      95%
3 years delay                       853.1      79%
20% reduction in benefits          1,289.4     104%
40% reduction in benefits           699.7       78%
50% reduction in benefits           404.9       60%
The previous table shows the effect of delays and lower than expected benefits on the NPV and IRR.
Overall, the project would remain justifiable even assuming a 3 year delay in project benefits or a 50
percent reduction in the benefits received, assuming the traditional parameters which dictate that annual
rate of return earned should be greater than or equal to the discount rate of the project, to maintain an
efficient allocation of Bank resources. The high IRR also underscores the cost-effective nature of the
program. The analysis highlights the importance of focusing on measurable results.
Fiscal Impact and Sustainability
The first step in the analysis for the sustainability of the project was to establish the base level expenditures
prior to the implementation of the project. After this evaluation, forecast estimates were made on the
additional costs, which result from this project and the expected revenues accruing to the sector. The
projections of sector revenues are based on the application of the recently approved EC 29 that establishes
minimum health expenditure allocations and provides a framework for increasing spending in line with the
nominal growth in GDP. Macroeconomic projections based on the implementation of the EC 29 indicate
that nominal expenditure growth will be 5.0 percent annually. While this legislation ensures that health
sector revenues will not fall below the levels of 2000, it does not, however, guarantee that real expenditures
keep pace with the increases in recurrent expenditure implied by the proposed project.
- 120-



In 2000, total health expenditures of the Health Ministry in Brazil account for 2 percent of the GDP, or
approximately US$74 per capita. It is important to note that there is a trend toward the decentralization of
services provided through the Sistema Unico de Satude (SUS), from the Federal level to the state and
municipal level. Besides this trend toward decentralization the health sector is being reformed to create
mechanisms to transfer funds from the Federal government to state and municipal governments. Current
legislation indicates that the federal budget will increase the allocations to state and municipal health
budgets by 7 percent in 2000, and by 12 and 15 percent in 2004 for states and municipalities, respectively.
The projections of future public health expenditures are based in the document elaborated by Dain et al
(2001).
Based on the current projections for health expenditures, once the project is fully implemented the
incremental cost is estimated to be equivalent to 2 percent of the public expenditure, from year eight
onwards. More importantly, the sustainability of the project depends on the availability of incremental
spending in the sector that will sustain the recurrent costs attributable to the project. If the sustainability of
the project is analyzed in terms of the overall percentage of annual increases in MOH spending absorbed by
the project's recurrent costs, rather than the total public sector spending, the percentage increases to nearly
60 percent of the annual increases of MOH expenditures. Even with the increase in resource expected from
the implementation of EC 29, the full implementation of the project will imply a continuous effort to target
to family care the projected increases in health sector resources. Sustainability should not be a problem,
with incremental spending around 2 percent of public health spending, however, the project's viability will
be highly dependent on maintaining political support and a priority allocation of incremental budget
revenues.

Annex 6: Procurement and Disbursement Arrangements
BRAZIL: FAMILY HEALTH EXTENSION PROGRAM
Procurement
1. Procurement Arrangements
1.1   Summary of the Procurement Arrangements. The procurement that will be required under the
Project will consist of workshops, training, hiring of consultants, the acquisition of medical equipment,
furniture, and information technology, and payment of the salaries of community agents and professional
staff performing activities under the Basic Care and Family Health Program. For the Basic and Family
Health Conversion Subprojects (Conversion Subprojects) in Component 1 (which will be implemented by
municipalities), the following arrangements will apply: (i) the procurement of smaller packages of goods
and works (below defined thresholds: see Table A2 and A3 below) will follow the procurement procedures
of the local law, which is compatible with Section I of the Guidelines (see below); (ii) procurement of large
packages of goods (which will follow ICB procedures) will follow Bank guidelines; and (iii) procurement
of local and international consultant services will follow the Bank's procurement and consultants'
guidelines with the following selection methods: consulting assignments over $100,000 will folllow QCBS
procedures, assignments below $100,000 may follow direct contracting procedures, health teams may be
hired under 3.19 of the Guidelines, and contracts for individuals below $50,000 are also permitted. For
Components 2, 3, and 4, which will be implemented at the central level, all the procurement activities will
be in accordance to the Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD loans and IDA Credits (January 1995,
revised January and August 1996, and September 1997) and the Guidelines for the Selection and
Employment of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers (January 1997, revised September 1997 and
January 1999).
1.2   National Procurement Environment. The Government of Brazil and the World Bank have recently
completed the first stage of a study on government procurement in Brazil and have concluded that the legal
framework and implementation are generally acceptable and guarantee adequate transparency. Moreover,
at the federal level, modern procurement techniques such as electronic purchasing (pregdo eletronico) and
the registro de precos are used that appear to be economic and efficient, and this Project is likely to benefit
from the use of such methods at the municipal level.
1.3    Use of National Procurement Rules under the Subprojects Pool. Given that the Bank has found
local procurement procedures acceptable and to allow for the use of pooled funds, the Bank will accept the
use of the procurement procedures as set forth in Brazil's national law for the NCB and for the
procurement of goods and works under Conversion Subprojects as approved in the procurement plans
presented by the participating municipalities. Using the local rules for this limited procurement will
minimize any mistakes that might be made by the participating municipalities. This measure was taken
because the national rules for limited procurement are consistent with the principles of economy, efficiency,
equal opportunity, and transparency set forth in the Bank's Guidelines. In addition, this is the only way to
allow the proposed pooling of Bank and government funds.
1.4   National Procurement Rules. The procurement methods set out under Brazil's Bidding and
contracting Law 8.666 (June 21, 1993), its amendments and other related laws are summarized as follows.
Concorrencia Publica involves public bidding open to all suppliers with no restriction on the participation
of foreign firms. The procedure follows a two- or three-envelope system where the qualification of bidders
- 123 -



is established at the beginning of the process, and only the envelopes comprising the proposals of the
qualifying bidders are opened and compared. The award is then made to the qualifying bidder with the
lowest price. Tomada de Pre,os involves public bidding open to all suppliers who previously registered
with the purchaser. The procedures follow the same steps as in the Concorrencia Publica except that the
time interval for the submission of bids is 15 days from the IFB instead of 30 days. New suppliers may
submit applications to the purchaser's register up to 72 hours before the deadline for the submission of
bids. The Convite is the same as the Tomada de Pre,os except that the purchaser invites at least three
suppliers to bid but other interested bidders, who may learn about the contract from the posting of the
invitation (convite), can register up to 24 hours before the deadline for submitting bids. Concurso involves
public bidding for hiring consultants through advertisements and that allows 45 days to submit a bid.
Dispensa de Licita,do allows direct contracting justified on the basis of 24 different situations listed in the
law, including works, other services, and goods costing up to 10 percent of the relevant threshold
established for the Concorrencia Publica. Additionally, there are two more national laws, one related to
the procurement of health inputs, the other allowing the municipalities to use "registro de pre~os " in cases
where prices have been obtained in a previous competitive bidding without requirements for a new bidding.
As mentioned above, the Bank has assessed the way in which these national rules are applied and is
satisfied that they comply with the criteria of open and equal opportunity and transparency.
1.5   Procurement Responsibility. There will be three levels of implementing agencies. At the central
level, the Unidade de Gerenciamento do Projeto (PCU) of the Ministry of Health will hire consultants and
procure larger packages of goods. It will subcontract training institutions, known as Institucoes Polo (IP)
under Component 2 and the other qualified entities, such as research institutions, needed for implementation
of Components 3. Under Component 4, the Borrower may also hire agencies to facilitate procurement
processes. The municipalities participating in Component 1 will carry procurement activities using the
methods established in the national procurement rules (for non-ICB goods and works). The Institu,ces
Polo, contracted through Component 2, will hire individuals and subcontract services (renting of facilities
and transport and paying fees and per diem for participants) using administrative procedures defined in the
Operational Manual (OM). The PCU will be responsible for overall procurement activity under the Project,
including compliance with procedures and timetables agreed with the Bank.
1.6   Procurement Implementing Capacity. The Bank has a Country Procurement Assessment (CPAR)
for Brazil dated 1993, and it is currently preparing a new CPAR in collaboration with the Brazilian federal
authorities. The preliminary results of this review indicate that the judicial protests or recurso continue to
cause major delays in government procurement. Another finding indicates that the use of COMPRASNET
and of E-purchasing (pregao eletronico) and other modern procurement methods will make government
procurement more efficient and transparent. The Bank has completed a capacity assessment of the PCU
and has developed an action plan for its improvement. There is no need to carry out a capacity assessment
of the Institu,ces Polo (IP), as they will be subcontractors for the Ministry of Health in carrying out
training activities. Under the Conversion Subprojects, the Ministry of Health, through the PCU, will be
responsible for upstream, procurement-related activities, including assessing the implementing capacity of
the participants, preparing and reviewing procurement plans, developing and monitoring improvement
action plans, justifying risk classification, coordinating procurement reviews, and preparing management
reports on compliance and prior review activities. In order to discharge these responsibilities, the PCU will
organize a procurement team consisting of at least one senior procurement specialist and one junior
procurement specialist, both of whom should be already familiar with the Bank's procurement guidelines.
This team will assess the implementing capacity of the selected municipalities and determine the level of
risk involved in the case of each beneficiary municipality using a methodology acceptable to the Bank. The
team will prepare the procurement section of the Project's various directives and of its operational manual.
The team and its support staff will participate in a workshop focused on ensuring that the participating
- 124-



municipalities will follow the procurement arrangements set forth in the Loan Agreement. The workshop
will focus on the Bank's procurement policy and procedures and how they apply to the procurement
arrangements planned for the Project.
1.7   Procurement Plan and Plan of Action. The Conversion Subprojects of Component 1 consist of
the conversion plans and the activities financed through the other two grant transfers for the Basic Care
(PABfixed) and the Family Health (PABfam) Programs. The procurement plan for each participating
municipality will consist of three elements that relate only to the sub-pool: (i) activities specified in the
conversion plans; (ii) activities financed through PABfixed; and (iii) activities financed through
PABfam.The procurement plan describes the actions that will trigger disbursements, how procurement
methods should be chosen, and the level of prior review and the performance indicators to be achieved (see
par. 1.22). The PCU prepared an overall procurement plan for the tasks to be carried out during the
Project's implementation. This plan was discussed during the appraisal phase and will be used as a model
for the Project's implementation. This model procurement plan will be included in the Operational Manual
and in other toolkits and instructions on the preparation of the Conversion Subprojects by the
municipalities. The procurement plans of the selected municipalities will form part of the Conversion
Subprojects and will be assessed by the PCU and reviewed by the Bank in order to confirm the prior
review processes. This overall procurement plan was approved by the RPA's office and is in the project
files. It consists of: (i) contract packaging, applicable procedures, and process scheduling; (ii) a plan for
selecting consultants, including contract packaging, applicable procedures, short lists and selection criteria;
and (iii) details of the other pool transfers part of the program. The procurement plan and the consultant
selection plan will be updated every six months. A model plan with similar content will be included in the
Operational Manual, and the participating municipalities will use this model to present the procurement
plan contained in the Conversion Subprojects. The Bank will biannually review each procurement plan
and plan of action and assess the risk level of the municipalities participating in the Conversion
Subprojects. Based on these reviews the Bank, in coordination with the PCU, will upgrade and downgrade
their risk classification (see Para. 1.19 & Table A.3) accordingly. Depending upon their risk classification,
participating municipalities may be required to improve their procurement performance.
1.8    Confirmation of the Plans for Participating Municipalities. The Bank will review on a biannual
basis the procurement plan and plan for selecting consultants, and confirm its approval of the procurement
and financial risk classification of each participating municipality.
1.9   Methods for Bank-financed Procurement.    For the centrally implemented components
(Components 2, 3, and 4), the Bank-financed procurement will be carried out in accordance with provisions
stipulated in the Loan Agreement and in the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement (published in January
1995, revised in January and August 1996, September 1997 and January 1999) for the supply of goods and
the Bank's Guidelines for Selection and Employment of Consultants (published in January 1997, revised
in September 1997 and January 1999) for technical assistance and other consultant assignments. The
Bank's Standard Bidding Documents and Standard Request for Proposals will govern the procurement of
Bank-financed ICB goods and consultant services respectively. The same rules will apply to ICB
procurement and to large contracts with consultant firms as identified in the procurement plans of the
participating municipalities. For the NCB's procurement of goods, the PCU will use the model standard
bidding document issued by SEAIN in November 2000. The pregdo and the electronic pregdo established
by the federal government will be used as alternatives to the NCB for selected items for which customized
designs are not required or for off-the-shelf items and as an alternative to shopping. The procurement
arrangements for each component are summarized in Table Al below.
- 125-



1.10  Bank-financed Goods. The Project will finance medical and non-medical equipment, information
and communications systems, vehicles and furniture. Tables A.2 and A.3 present the procurement methods
to be followed for goods according to municipal risk classification. If the Bank's capacity assessment
establishes that the municipality is low risk (a Type A municipality), it will be allowed higher thresholds
for selecting its procurement method, and will be subject to less prior review by the PCU. Conversely, if the
municipality is classified as high risk (Type C), its thresholds will be lower and its prior review by the PCU
will be more intensive. The initial risk grading of the participating municipalities will be subject to review
(upgrade or downgrade) depending upon their performance as monitored by the PCU. Given that all of the
participating municipalities cannot be known before the onset of the Project (as their participation will
depend upon the application of the selection criteria), no aggregate amounts can be set for the less
competitive procedures to be used by the municipal implementing agencies at this stage. The PCU will
produce summary reports that aggregate the amounts when defining the procurement arrangements under
the next APL phases. Under Components 2, 3 and 4, the PCU will carry out the NCB's procurement on
contracts estimated to cost less than US$300,000 (in an aggregate amount of US$750,000) and shopping
on contracts that do not exceed US$100,000 each (in an aggregate amount of US$1,000,000). Table A2
below describes in detail the proposed thresholds for the procurement of goods and for prior review.
1.11  Bank-financed Works. The works in the Project include small-scale rehabilitation of existing
health installations that the participating municipalities will subcontract directly to contractors when the
individual contracts do not exceed US$50,000. When no such contractors are available in the participating
municipalities, they will select a contractor from outside the local area on the basis of national regulations
as explained above. This type of investment will be specified in the procurement plans contained in the
Conversion Plans presented by the participating municipalities during the Project's implementation. No
procurement of works is anticipated under Components 2-4.
1.12  Bank-financed Consultant Services. The Project will finance consultant services and training up
to an estimated total cost of about US$43 million. Single-sourcing will total $6.2 million and will involve
the hiring of the Institucoes Polo (IP) and other research management entities. IPs will play an important
role as providers of training in the selected municipalities. Most of the IPs were selected competitively
under Ln-4047-BR. Because there would be no particular advantage in replacing the IPs, the existing IPs
will be re-hired on a sole source basis under a new commercial contract based on the lump-sum standard
form of contract with each contract not to exceed US$250,000. The other single-sourcing will involve the
hiring of research management entities up to an aggregate of US$1.2 million. The Project's consultants will
be selected and employed under arrangements acceptable to the Bank, using the Bank's Standard Forms
and Contracts. Most of the Project's studies under Components 2-3, the supervision of its implementation,
and its financial and procurement reviews will be carried out according to Quality and Cost-Based
Selection (QCBS) methods, Quality-Based Selection (QBS) [for research institutions] and individual
consultants, as detailed in Table A3 below. Some of the consultants under Components 2, 3, and 4 will be
hired by selected qualified entities following the Bank's Guidelines and certain regulations established by
presidential decree in February 2001 requiring the advertisement of individual assignments in order to make
the selection of individual consultants more transparent.
1.13  Hiring of Basic Care and Family Health PersonneL Under the Conversion Subprojects, the Bank
will contribute to the financing of activities related to the implementation of Basic Care and Family Health
Programs in the participating municipalities as part of the pooling arrangements. These activities include
compensation (salaries or honoraria) of the technical and professional staff performing activities related to
the organization, supervision, and provision of Basic Care and Family Health Services. The municipalities
will subcontract and pay these workers under several types of contracts, as required by local law.
- 126 -



Independently of the contracting methodology (service regime), these workers should be chosen according
to a competitive process as specified in the national legal regulations, and consistent with Bank Guidelines.
The hiring of these staff will be governed by local regulations for small service assignments reviewed by
and found acceptable by the Bank pursuant to para. 3.19 of the Consultants Guidelines. All contracts for
salaried workers would be financed as incremental operating costs in the Conversion Subprojects
Subproject category while consultants' contracts would be financed as "services" in the same category. All
other consultant contracts will be awarded following the Consultants Guidelines with the following
selection methods: consulting assignments over $100,000 will follow QCBS procedures and assignments
below $100,000 may follow direct contracting procedures.
1.14  Procurement Review and Methods. The Bank's procurement review will be carried out in
accordance with Appendix I of the Guidelines for Procurement. With respect to the participating
municipalities, the PCU will confirm the exact prior review process during its review of the procurement
plan that will be prepared by each municipality, reviewed by the PCU, and confirmed by the Bank. The
review levels should not differ substantially from those established in the Operational Manual, but the PCU
will consider clearly justified variations during the implementation of the Project with the agreement with
the Bank. All ICB will be subject to the Bank's prior review, irrespective of the estimated amount of its
contract. PCU will prior review all stages of these procedures in the case of smaller procurement activities.
Given that the local law will provide the basis for these smaller procurement activities, including those
operational costs under the national Basic Care services and Family Health Programs, the Bank will not
prior review those actions. These will be subject to independent procurement reviews and to ex-post review
by the Bank. With regard to consultant services, before participating municipalities issue any requests for
proposals, they should prepare a complete plan governing the selection of consultants under the Project as
part of the Plano de Conversao. This plan will be reviewed by the PCU and confirrned by the Bank. The
Bank will review the selection of consultants in accordance with the Guidelines for the Selection and
Employment of Consultants and the provisions stipulated in the Loan Agreement. The Bank will prior
review larger procurement activities estimated to cost more than US$100,000 for firms and US$50,000 for
individuals. The Bank will also be informed by the MOH about the terms and conditions of the sole
sourced institutions including IPs or other qualified national or international entities whose fees are not paid
out of the Loan proceeds. A review process similar to that for individual consultants will apply to training
and workshops. Since the participating municipalities will not be known until the application of the
selection criteria, no aggregate amounts have been estimated for less competitive procedures to be used by
the municipal implementing agencies.

1.15   Methods for Non-Bank-financed Procurement. It is expected that the PCU will subcontract the
hiring of individual consultants through one or several qualified entity under a PRODOC document based
on a Management Agreement and Checklist approved by the Bank.
1.16   Advertising. A General Procurement Notice advertising the need for consultant services and for
the ICB's goods and consultant services should be published in United Nations Development Business no
later than March 2002. The Notice will be updated annually to advertise for outstanding consultant
services and ICB goods as specified in the approved Planos de Conversdo. In addition, detailed consultant
services and assignments will be advertised, as they become necessary in at least one national newspaper
with a large circulation and on the web page of United Nations Development Business. Furthermore, the
implementing agencies may also advertise some of the Project's studies in international newspapers or
technical magazines.
1.17    Procurement Records. The PCU will maintain detailed procurement records, reflecting the
Project's supply of goods, works, and consultant services, including records of time taken to complete key
steps in the process and procurement activities related to supervision, review, and audits. These records
will be maintained for at least two years after the Project's closing date. The records for goods and works
will include public notices, bidding documents and addenda, bid opening information, bid evaluation
reports, formal appeals by bidders and outcomes, signed contracts with related addenda and amendments,
records on claims and dispute resolution, and any other useful information. The records for consultant
services will include public notices for expression of interest, requests for proposals and addenda, technical
and financial reports, formal appeals by consultants and outcomes, signed contracts, addenda and
amendments, records on claims and dispute resolutions, and any other useful information. The participating
municipalities will retain all of the bidding documents used, invoices, price comparisons, bids received, bid
evaluation reports, and all of the documentation required under the law and ensure that it is readily
available. The audit and procurement post-review supervision by the Bank and by government institutions
is particularly important because of the pooled funds for the Conversion Subprojects, which would follow
the norrnal flow of funds from the federal government to the municipalities. The filing, record keeping,
- 129 -



auditing, reporting, post-review, and monitoring of the smaller procurement activities are crucial for the
successful application of the funds and will be better performed under the national procurement rules that
the Bank has deemed adequate for ensuring economy, efficiency, and transparency.
1.18   Frequency of procurement supervision missions proposed: The Bank will carry ex-post reviews
through an annual supervision mission on procurement. The PCU and the Bank, including procurement and
financial management staff, will meet biannually to discuss the classification of Municipalities, to review
their procurement and action plans and to carry out the ex-post review. The Bank's project supervision
will retain a part time local implementation officer that would review procurement reports and will carry
out procurement reviews in the participating municipalities.
1.19   Procurement Reviews. In order to ensure compliance with the national procurement rules, the
participating municipalities will be subject to independent procurement reviews as often as is determined by
their risk classification (See Table A.2 above). The reviews will focus on the smaller procurement
activities, including the NCB, but will also review other procurement by the municipalities, including ICB,
hiring of consultants, progress on the implementing capacity action plan, and the terms and conditions of
the field staff for the Conversion Subprojects . The PCU will provide the Bank with procurement records
reviewed by independent procurement experts who are acceptable to the Bank no later than six months after
the end of each year or in accordance with Table A2 above. The terms of reference for the independent
procurement reviews will be in accordance with internationally accepted standards, and specified in the
Operatinal Manual.
1.20   Procurement Risk Assessment. The Ministry of Health has well-established implementing
agencies; thus, the PCU will benefit from the expertise of staff from the existing agencies. A risk level will
be accorded to each of the participating municipalities based on a self-assessment by the municipality and a
review by qualified staff at the PCU (see Table A.4 below). The Bank will confirm the resulting estimate of
risk in each case. Based on this approach, the PCU will prepare a capacity assessment for each
participating municipality before any disbursement is made under the Conversion Subprojects. The PCU
has been trained in the use of the Bank's toolkit for determining the procurement implementing capacity
and assessing risk. The Bank's standard capacity assessment includes interviews and tests covering about
50 items. The items are activities or resources available in the unit that performs the procurement
processing. These include filing, ethics, organization, staff, remuneration, codes, and manuals. The PCU
will be using the same questionnaire and methodology used by the Bank in classifying the risk of the
participating municipalities. Four or more items classified as High Risk will result in the municipality being
given a High Risk classification. Two or three items classified as High Risk will give it an Average Risk
classification. One or no items classified as High Risk will give it a Low Risk classification. If the
municipality has five or more High Risk items in the capacity assessment, it will not be eligible for a
Conversion Subproject, and will receive a D rating. The estimate is that about 50 percent, 25 percent, and
25 percent of municipalities will be classified as respectively average, high, and low risk. According to this
classification, the assessed the PCU's risk as average provided that it implements the action plan in the
project files. The capacity assessment of the PCU as well as the action plan for improving its procurement
capacity includes providing office and filing space, hiring specialized staff, and maintaining an information
system.

1.21   Remedies and Performance Indicators. The greatest risk involved in pooling resources is that the
Bank will largely be unable to track the use of its share of the pooled money all the way to the final
supplier and contractor. In order to minimize this risk, the Conversion Subprojects will build on the
existing Ministry of Health rules governing the transfer of funds, including control, supervision, auditing
and reporting, as the Bank believes that these rules are competent and robust. To complement these
measures, MOH has agreed to:
*  Classify the implementing risk of the participating municipalities using the same methodology used by
the Bank to assess its clients. This methodology was tested in three municipalities during the Project's
appraisal phase.
*  Hire a procurement expert to assess the implementing capacity of participating municipalities, classify
its risk, and monitor its action plan for improving its capacity.
*  Present the Bank with these assessments together with the recommendations of risk and the
procurement plan for each participating municipality. The Bank will formally approve those documents
in periodic review meetings.
*  Supervise the performance of the municipalities through visits and reporting tasks. If the MOH detects
changes in the elements that determined the municipality's initial risk classification, then it will propose
to upgrade or downgrade that classification. Monitor how the municipalities are implementing their
procurement responsibilities and identify any non-compliance with agreed performance indicators (see
below).
1.22   Misprocurement. The MOH will declare that misprocurement has occurred when any contract
has not been handled in accordance with the agreed procedures in the Loan Agreement. Consequently, any
contract subject to the Bank's procurement or consultant guidelines that violates those guidelines will be
declared misprocured, and the Bank will cancel the portion of the Loan that is allocated to the contract.
Under the Conversion Subprojects , in the case of smaller procurement activities that are subject to local
procedures, misprocurement will consist of a failure to award the contract to the lowest evaluated bidder, a
lack of records or information pertaining to the bidding and award, the disqualification of the lowest priced
bid without a sound reason, deviations from the approved procurement plan without prior approval, and
impeding audits or ex-post reviews.
1.23   Performance Indicators. The Bank will monitor and evaluate the procurement activities under the
Project using three performance indicators. The participating municipalities will be excluded from the APL
2 if they exceed the following parameters:
- 131 -



(a)  No more than 30% of contracting activities in which awarded contracts are subject to judicial
challenge regarding procurement in the first year; no more than 20% in the second year.
(b) No more than 3 injunctions (liminares) suspending procurement processes during the Project
implementation;
(c)  No more than two declared misprocurement actions during the Project implementation. In the local
procedures misprocurement will include: failure to award to then lowest evaluated bidder; lack of
records and information pertaining the bidding and award; disqualification of lowest price without a
sound reason; and deviations from the approved procurement plan without prior PCU and Bank
approval; and
(d)  No more than one downgrade on the procurement risk classification during project implementation.
2. Financial Management Arrangements
2.1    Brazil's Financial Management System as Applied to PARB Transfers. Brazil has a well-developed
and centralized system of public financial management. A well functioning centralized budget information
system SIDOR (Sistema Integrado de Dados Orcamentarios) is used by MOP (Ministerio do Planejamento
Orcamento e Gestao) to develop the national budget and to monitor its implementation. The actual
execution of the budget in terms of making cash payments, monitoring cash flows, and preparing financial
statements is done by a centralized financial management information system SIAFI (Sistema Integrado de
Administracao Financeira) operated by STN (Secretaria do Tesouro Nacional). There is a high level of
fiscal transparency, which is reflected in the public availability of budget documents, laws, budget
monitoring reports, and the public accounts. Two major audit, institutions, the Court of Accounts of the
TCU (Tribunal de Contas da Uniao) which reports to the Congress, and the Secretariat of Internal Control
of the SFC (Secretaria Federal de Controle Intemo) which reports to the Ministry of Finance, are
professional in their approach, have adequate operational independence from political interference, and
have the power to monitor the transfers from the MOH through to municipalities.
2.2    PAB transfers from the National Health Fund (FNS) to the municipalities follow the same financial
management norms and procedures as all of the other federal programs implemented at the state or local
level by any of the Ministries. The federal financial management system with respect to planning,
budgeting, financial administration, accounting, and internal controls is organized basically within three
secretariats in two ministries as follows:
* The Federal Planning and Budgetary System (SFPO - Sistema Federal de Planejamento e Orcamento)
administered by the Ministry of Planning
* The Federal Financial Administration System, administered by Treasury Secretariat (STN) in the
Ministry of Finance (MOF)
* The Federal Accounting System (SLAFI), administered by STN
* The Federal Internal Control System (SCI-Sistema de Controle Intemo) administered by SFC.
2.3    The MOH is responsible for preparing and executing its budget, which includes the PABtransfers.
The budget is prepared using SIDOR, and all budget data are centralized and consolidated by the Federal
Budget Secretariat (SOF-Secretaria de Orcamento Federal) together with the data from other spending
ministries. The SOF is responsible for formulating the annual federal budget proposal and progranmming its
execution once it is approved by Congress. The STN is responsible for controlling and accounting for
budget execution in terms of cash receipts and payments and for the financial programming needed for this,
- 132 -



as well as for preparing the financial statements. It uses the SIAFI central accounting system for these
purposes.
2.4    The Budget execution decree issued jointly by the SOF and STN provides for monthly cash
releases to the MOH based on forecasts of cash receipts and payments by the STN. This is designed to
ensure (a) the timely release of funds to administrative units to carry out their work and (b) the maintenance
of a balance between revenues and expenditure during the year. Overall responsibility for the release of
funds to the MOH on basis of the budget implementation plan rests with the STN. The STN transfers funds
directly from the single Treasury account maintained at Banco do Brasil to the FNS.
2.5    Responsibility for GOB accounting, reporting and cash management also falls under the STN,
which uses the SIAFI to perform these functions. The SIAFI is centralized and operates through an
extensive telecommunications network connected to the spending units of all agencies of the Federal
Administration. The SIAFI is used from the registration of the initial budget to the issue of monthly and
annual reports. The SIAFI (i) financial programs and implements the Budget, (ii) centrally processes and
records all budget payments, (iii) controls the implementation of the budget, (iv) produces the accounting
statements and reports required by different management levels, (v) maintains and manages the single
treasury account, and (vi) provides access and reporting services to financial managers of administrative
units.
2.6    For projects financed by the World Bank, the STN is responsible for maintaining the Special
Accounts in US dollars, distributing funds to the implementing ministries for making eligible expenditures,
reconciling the Special Account, and reviewing Statement of Expenditures and disbursement applications
prior to their final submission to the Bank. The MOH is responsible for ensuring that project accounting
information is given to the SIAFI on a timely basis, as the projects do not have a direct connection to the
system.
2.7    The SFC carries out audits of the PAB transfers on a regular basis. These transfers are also
subject to audit by the TCU at any time.
2.8    Financial Management Assessment. As required by OP/BP 10.02, a CMU Financial Management
Specialist carried out a financial management assessment during the Project's pre-appraisal phase.
Subsequently, in view of the Project's innovative financing and disbursement arrangements, a Senior
Financial Management Specialist from Bank Headquarters joined the CMU FMS to complete the FM
Assessment. The CMU Disbursement Analyst also provided guidance in matters within her competence.
2.9    The assessment was carried out on site at the SPS (Secretaria de Politicas de Saude), with the
Project Implementing Unit or Unidade de Gerenciamento do Projeto (PCU), their
Financial/Administrative team, and related Health Ministry representatives during the Bank's pre-appraisal
mission from September 24th to October 5th 2001 and its appraisal mission from November 12th to 16th
2001. The assessment involved discussions with the PCU, the SPS, and financial staff members, a review
of the financial systems to be used for project accounting, financial administration, controlling, and
reporting, and the intemal controls and administration procedures to be implemented to control and monitor
the Project's execution and audit arrangements.
2.10   Executive Summary. The overall conclusion of this FM Team was that, under the management of
the PCU, the Project would be able to maintain financial management arrangements compatible with
present Bank requirements in this regard. An action plan is presented,summarizing the Bank's agreements
with the PCU on the standards to be met by the financial statements in the PMR format and the time frame
- 133 -



for their submission after being certified by the auditors (the SFC). The borrower is the Federal
Government of Brazil, and, as per the Risk Assessment Matrix below, no major risks were identified that
would require special recommendations or actions other than those that were already in place and/or were
included in the action plan as per paragraph 2.30 below.
2.11   Summary of Project Financial Management Arrangements. The administration of the Project will
be decentralized to participating municipalities in Component 1 but will be centralized at the PCU (
Unidade de Gerenciamento do Projeto) in Components 2, 3, and 4. The PCU will be located in the Health
Policies Secretariat (SPS). For Component 1, funds will be transferred up to 40 municipalities (for APL
Phase I) to support the transitional costs of converting from traditional, facility-based care to a proactive,
outreach model of care in the Conversion Subprojects. Each municipality will be responsible for
implementing the subproject, including procuring goods and services. The PCU will supervise their
activities and require that the municipalities maintain appropriate financial management arrangements and
provide timely reporting on the financial and physical implementation of the Project to ensure
accountability and good governance.
2.12   In view of the wide variety of different characteristics of the municipalities that will be
implementing Component 1, the team developed a methodology to properly control and monitor the funds
financed by the Bank. This methodology classifies the implementing agencies by risk level based on their
existing management capacity. The classification consists of three levels: Level A means low-risk, Level B
means medium--risk, and Level C means high-risk. There will be also a fourth category, Level D meaning
not eligible, for those candidate agencies that clearly will not be able to fulfill the minimum financial
management requirements during the first phase of the project. (A municipality will not be eligible for
Conversion Subprojects if it is classified as Type D risk for either procurement or Financial management.
The municipalities will be classified on the basis of criteria established by the MOH and by the Bank and
according to assessments conducted by PCU staff and reviewed by the Bank. The Project's Operational
Manual will include the guidelines for assessing the financial management capacity of the municipalities.
These guidelines will encompass all of the financial administrative procedures and controls required for the
sound financial management of the Conversion Subprojects and will follow the principles established in the
Guidelines to Staff on Assessment of Financial Management Arrangements in World Bank-Financed
Projects issued by the Financial Management Sector Board on June 30 2001.
2.13   The level and detail of the internal controls and procedures to be established for each implementing
agency as well as the frequency of follow-up reports, the scope of audits, and the preconditions for
transferring funds will depend on the relevant risk grading. Considering the multiple systems and
procedures used by the MOH to control the transfers from FNS, PCU's monitoring of the implementing
agencies will be permanent and constant, thus allowing them to change a municipality's risk level according
to its performance. The PCU Financial Manager and a World Bank FMS will make joint field visits to
selected municipalities to test the risk assessment instrument. Because of the direct financing of
municipalities under Component 1 of this Project, the Bank will work closely with the PCU to supervise
municipalities in their efforts to enhance their financial management systems.
2.14   Table B presents the various control procedures for the executing agencies according to each
respective risk level.

2.15   Country Issues. According to preliminary conclusions of Brazil's CFAA: "Brazil has a well
developed and centralized system of public financial management. Overall it is able to reliably track
budget expenditures. However the institutional arrangements and processes are complex. While they
achieve good results in terms of aggregate fiscal control, they are less successful in achieving good
expenditure prioritization and operational efficiency. " Brazil's public financial management system yields
reliable information and adequate management and tracking of the receipt and use of funds at the national
level and is well able to support Bank's lending programs. The issues that could affect the Project's
financial management system and/or the fiduciary responsibilities of the implementing agencies are
manageable.
2.16   Risk Analysis. A detailed risk assessment questionnaire has been completed by the FMS based on
sample observations and is in the Project files together with the financial management assessment working
papers. No major risks were identified as presented in Table B 1 below, except for eventual potential
- 135-



weaknesses in the financial management arrangements of some municipalities which do not have adequate
financial management systems in place as described in paragraph 2.19.

2.17   The PCU, a unit within the SPS, will be a well-structured entity with adequate personnel to
implement and manage the project. A well-proven accounting/monitoring system will be used, and some
adaptations will be made that are included in the action plan to further enhance its efficiency. At the PCU
level, the Bank considers that the risks related to the financial administration of the Project are acceptable,
and it requires no other additional control procedures other than those that already exist or that will be
implemented as agreed during the appraisal mission. As for the decentralized executing agencies, their risk
levels will be assessed individually as explained in paragraph 2.6 above.
2.18   Strengths and Weaknesses. The Project is likely to benefit greatly from a financial management
point of view from: (a) the experience of the MOH and the PCU in implementing and managing
Bank-financed projects, with good results; (b) only staff with well-proven experience will be hired to work
for the PCU; and (c) the FM system to be used is a system developed by the MOH's IT Department, which
is currently being used in the VIGISUS project and which was assessed and approved by the Bank for
PMR-based disbursement.   The FM   system  is related to the government's Integrated Financial
Management System (SIAFI), and the information in the one system has to be consistent and reconcilable
with the information in the other system. The Bank's Country Financial Accountability Assessment
(CFAA) for Brazil, soon to be released, indicates that: "there is a well-established legal framework for the
development, implementation, and monitoring of the budget, starting with the Constitutional provisions
concerning financial management generally, the multi-year National Plan (PPA), each year's Budget
Guidance Law (LDO) and Annual Budget Law (LOA), along with the Law on Fiscal Responsibility
(LFR). These legal provisions are observed."
- 136-



2.19   One potential weakness of the Project, from a financial management perspective, may result from
the fact that it will directly allocate resources to the municipalities. These bodies vary widely in terms of
their management skills, existing capacity, and financial administration systems. This situation is being
addressed by means of the risk assessment to be performed in each municipality that will result in it being
given a risk classification as described above. The Project's financial/administrative manual will establish
adequate remedies for each municipality according to its risk classification. Three major remedies and
preventive actions are applicable: (i) different control procedures for type A, B, and C executing agencies
as shown in paragraph 2.8 above; (ii) the suspension of fund transfers by MOH to executing agencies that
do not comply with timely reporting requirements, as described in paragraphs 2.15 - 2.19 below (Flow of
Funds, Component 1); (iii) independent audits to be done in all of the participating municipalities under
Component 1; and (iv) the use of financial management performance indicators as described in paragraph
2.29 below, which will trigger the exclusion of any under-performing municipality from Phase 2 of the
project. In addition, if a municipality is found to have made any ineligible expenditures, these must either
be substituted for by other eligible expenditures that are appropriately documented or the municipality must
refund 30 percent of their total cost to the Bank at the yearly review of auditor's report. In case a
misappropriation of funds occurs in any given municipality, the municipality must return a corresponding
amount to the FNS, and further transfers will be suspended until the municipality has taken adequate action
to correct any weakness in its intemal control system.
2.20   Implementing Entity and Staffing. The PCU will be organized within the SPS, and its staff will
be recruited from among the staff of the SPS. Technicians will be contracted for specific tasks within the
Project. The PCU will have three Departments: (a) a Technical Department to be headed by a Public
Health Specialist who will be responsible for planning and monitoring the actions involved in implementing
the three components of the Project; (b) a Financial/Administrative Department that will be responsible for
coordinating all administrative actions including the preparation of contracts (convenios) with the executing
agencies, procurement, and the flow of funds; and (c) an Information Technology Department responsible
for the implementation and maintenance of the Management/Financial system.  An experienced
financial/Administrative Manager will be in charge of all accounting, reporting, disbursement, and audit
arrangements and for the support and supervision of the financial activities of the municipalities and other
implementing agencies. The Financial Departmnent will have about 10 staff members with the following
functions: one disbursement specialist, one specialist to check disbursement reports from implementing
agencies, one procurement specialist, three or four technicians to support implementing agencies, and one
financial management specialist to assess and evaluate the financial/administrative capacity of the
implementing agencies. The Financial/Administrative team, part of which is already in place, has proven
experience with the implementation of Bank-financed projects in the health area, and in due course they will
participate in financial seminars run by the Bank. The Bank considers the staffing of the PCU to be
adequate for this project.
2.21   Flow of Funds. Under the FHEP, the MOH will continue to use its current arrangements to finance
projects. This involves the transfer of funds from the National Treasury Secretariat (STN) to the National
Health Fund (FNS) at the MOH. This way of making transfers has been successfully used in previous
Bank-financed projects. The FNS has multiple other sources of funding, including revenues from tax levies
and other social contributions. All these sources together with the Bank financing constitute the total
funding available at the FNS for health programs in Brazil.
2.22   The first Bank disbursement, out of the Loan Account, will be to fund the Special Account, with an
initial deposit of US$1.5 million. The Bank will make subsequent reimbursements of funds to the Special
Account upon receipt of withdrawal applications prepared by the PCU and processed through the Budget
and Planning Secretariat of the MOH on the basis of the SOTs for Component 1 and SOEs for
- 137 -



Components 2-4.   Funds advanced by the STN to the FNS will subsequently be replenished by
withdrawals from the Special Account on the basis of the SOTs prepared by the PCU. These SOTs will be
prepared on the sole basis of the fund transfers to the eligible municipalities. The transfers will follow the
general procurement plans that govern the Conversion Subprojects.
2.23   On the other hand, funds will be transferred from the FNS to the Municipal Health Funds (FMS)
after the municipality in question has qualified according to the rigorous MOH rules to this effect.
Periodically, the municipalities account to the PCU for the funds that they have received through reports
that include documentation as required by the MOH. The pre-qualification conditions also establish
reporting requirements and overall fiduciary rules and regulations. The PCU will prepare quarterly Project
Management Reports (PMRs) on the basis of the information that they receive from the various recipients
of project funds. These reports will be submitted to the Bank and to the independent auditors for
supervision purposes and for the preparation of audits.
2.24   The Operational Manual of the Project will include details about the flow of funds, including the
rules and procedures governing fund transfers, record keeping and report preparation, audits, and
non-compliance remedies among other related topics. (Consistent with the Bank's Fiduciary
responsibilities, the Project's Operational Manual will establish procedures that in case the Municipalities
do not submit detailed expenditures reports up to a 150 days period. In this case, within 60 days the MOH
shall suspend additional transfers linked to the conversion plans. In case that the delay extends beyond 240
days, the MOH will initiate the proceedings to suspend transfers linked to PABfixed and PAB variable in
accordance with existing rules and regulations of the FNS. The MOH shall inform the Bank within 30 days
of the occurrence of the above circumstances. In this case, the Banke expects to discount from future
disbursements the equivalent amounts for the balance of the transfers involved).
2.25   Component 1 - Municipal Conversion Plans. This component will comprise three different
programs. Basic Care Services consists of transfers to municipalities in fixed, per capita amounts to cover
part of the general costs of providing primary care. The Family Health Program will consist of variable
transfers (depending on the extent of population coverage) to municipalities to finance the implementation
of services and activities. Finally, there will be variable transfers to municipalities to finance the Municipal
Conversion Plans to transform their primary delivery models into models based on the Family Health
Program. These three transfers are deposited into a single bank account of the relevant municipality, but
with a separate indication regarding the relevant program. However, these transfers are accounted
separately both by the municipality and MOH through the SIOPS system, described in paragraph 2.29
below.The Bank will finance 30 percent of the costs of goods, works, services and operationl costs needed
to implement Conversion subprojects and which are included in the total amount of the funds transferred in
these three programs. The PCU will prepare the SOT on the basis of the actual cash transfers made by the
FNS to the municipalities, and it will be reviewed and approved by the STN, which in turn will withdraw
the funds from the Special Account to replenish the initial transfers to the municipalities, which are always
pre-financed by the government.
2.26   In order to receive fund transfers from the FNS, municipalities (FMS) will have to pre-qualify on
the basis of rules and procedures established by the Health Policies Secretariat at the MOH. The criteria
for qualification are very detailed and essentially refer to the ability of the municipalities to provide a
number of services to their populations, having installed the necessary control mechanisms such as
appropriate accounting and internal controls, information systems and auditing, and appropriate staffing.
Municipalities will prepare the plans for converting to the Family Health Program to be reviewed and
approved by the MOH. The conversion plans and related procurement plans must clearly specify the
- 138 -



outputs, a detailed budget, and a timetable for implementation in such a form to allow the MOH to review
the subsequent performance of municipalities and to compare their actual expenditures to the budgeted
amounts. Subsequently, these items will be included in a signed MOH-municipal agreement.
2.27   Component 2 - Human Resources Development, Component 3 - Monitoring and Evaluation --
Component 4 - Project Management. For Components 2 and 3, the Bank will finance 90 percent of total
project costs, while for Component 4, the Bank's portion of financing will be 50 percent. The costs
associated with these components will be paid directly from the FNS. Since these components will be
managed and implemented directly by the PCU, there will not be any transfers to the FMS. The PCU will
prepare the relevant SOEs, submit them to the STN, which will in turn withdraw funds from the Special
Account and deposit them in the FNS.
2.28   Information and Financial/Accounting Systems. The PCU will operate a fully integrated with the
PMR management system for monitoring and controlling project execution and financial management,
including project accounting, reporting, special account control, disbursements, and the SOT/SOE. This
system was originally developed for the VIGISUS project (L.4394 BR). It was assessed by the Bank and
considered to be suitable for reporting and disbursement via Project Management Reports (PMRs). It was
again assessed and approved for this Project. The system is able to record budgets, estimates, and
objectives and compare these with actual figures and performance. It complies with all operational and
financial reporting requirements. The system will be used to record all project expenditures, aggregated by
project component and major project activities within each component. PMiRs will be prepared quarterly
for PCU management so they can review and assess overall progress on the project, including both
financial and physical progress. It was agreed with the Project Coordinator at the PCU that the PCU will
prepare PMRs showing cumulative quarterly/annual and life-of-the-project-to-date balances to be
submitted to the Bank for each quarter of the calendar year no later than 45 days after the closing date of
each quarter. These PMiRs will reconcile the SOTs on an annual basis with the actual expenditures made
by municipalities based on their procurement plans.
2.29   In addition, the following systems will also be used to enhance the financial management of the
Project: GESCON - Sistema de Gestao Financeira e de Convenios: The GESCON is a financial system
for managing "convenios" (sub-project contracts). This is a system designed to control and manage all
funds and resources used to finance the health programs of the MOH. The purpose of this system is to
monitor the complete sub-program (convenio) cycle starting from the initial registration of the potential
beneficiary agency up to final submission of completion and expense reports.
SIOPS - Sistema de Informacoes de Orcamento Publico em Saude: This system consists of a database
developed by the MOH to control all investments made in the municipalities in the health area by the three
levels of government - federal, state, and municipal. This system formally accounts for the health
expenditures of public agencies on the basis of financial data provided by these agencies. These financial
data are entered in the database by the source of the financing and by the health care activities to which
they are applied. It also accounts separately the money transfers made to the municipalities under the
PABfixed, PABfam and PABconv grants.
SIAFI - Sistema de Administracao Financeira Integrada do Governo Federal: As part of the federal
financial/accounting and administration system of the Brazilian government, all project transactions will
also be recorded in the SLAFI. The SIAFI is centralized in Brasilia and is interconnected with federal
government agencies nationwide and abroad. It was implemented in 1987 and is the largest and most global
instrument of public financial management in the country. It makes it possible for Brazilians to follow up
and control the federal government's financial activities on line and in real time. By means of data
- 139 -



extraction software, it will be possible to prepare specific reports from data in the SIAFI and to compare
these with other reports issued directly from the Project's financial system as deemed necessary by the
MOH. The PCU will reconcile the balances on the Project's records with those of the SIAFI on a regular
basis and will present any irreconcilable differences to the SFC for further scrutiny, review, and reporting
as necessary.
2.30   The Bank's overall assessment of the Project's financial management systems indicates that they
are adequate and sufficient. All systems are fully operational and will record all of the Project's financial
transactions from its inception. Internal controls include financial administration procedure manuals
applicable both to the PCU and to the decentralized implementing agencies.
2.31 Internal Audits. Internal audits for the project will be performed by the MOH's Internal Audit
Department. These internal audits will be perforned in accordance with their regular audit program (for
projects and transfers), and also according to agreements between project management staff and the SFC to
ensure that generally accepted auditing standards are met. This will enable the SFC to rely on these audits
for its own overall yearly audit. The financial implementation of the Subprojects will be monitored through
ESCON and SIOPS. PCU technicians will make physical inspections in the field to check that the
implementing agencies are complying with their plans and timetables. Both the intemal audit reviews and
the reports of the PCU field inspections must be presented to the Bank as requested.
2.32   External Audit Arrangements. Audits will be carried out yearly by the Federal Control Secretariat
(SFC) in compliance with a protocol of understanding signed between the Bank and the federal government
in 1998. Terms of reference (TOR) for project-specific audits will be agreed between the Bank and project
management who in turn will instruct the SFC to carry out the audit accordingly. The TOR will include
specific instructions to the auditors to audit the amounts transferred via the SOT as opposed to actual
expenditures and to certify the eligibility of the expenditures financed by the Bank. The SFC audit will
encompass all of the financial transactions under all four Project components. Under Component 1, the
SFC will audit all of the municipalities involved and all transfers done under MOH's basic care and other
health care programs including PABfixed, PABvariable and PABconversion plans. The Internal Audit
Department of the MOH will coordinate its audit work with that of the SFC to avoid any unnecessary
duplication. This will be particularly necessary in the case of the audit of Component 1 in connection with
transfers to the municipalities and their appropriate application to eligible expenditures under the funding
agreements signed between the MOH and the municipalities. Municipalities that benefit from the PABfixed
and the PABfam grants have their health programs audited as part of the regular audit of MOH programs
by the SFC. The Bank's FM Specialists will supervise the initial stages of the Project's implementation to
provide specific guidance on the scope, procedures, and reporting of the audit. This support will be part of
the overall technical assistance that the Bank expects to provide in this operation related to some of its
novel financial management arrangements.
The following are some salient features of the audit process:
* The objective of the SFC audit is to judge the accuracy of the financial statements prepared by the
PCU for the year-end in the PMR format. The auditor's report should also include an opinion on the
eligibility of the Project's expenditures and the adequacy of the internal controls. Any weakness should
be reported in the auditors' management letter together with the recommendations for improvement.
- 140 -



*  All the municipalities (100 percent) receiving funds under Component I will be audited at least once a
year or more if considered necessary by SFC, MOH or the Bank. The audits will be done year round,
and the scope of the audit for each municipality involved will be determined on the basis of its risk
classification, which in Component 1 will be determined by assessments carried out prior to the process
of ascertaining whether the municipality qualifies for funding. The audits will cover the totality of the
transfers made by the MOH to the municipalities. The TOR will determine the extension of tests in
project transactions and what specific audit steps should be taken in view of the municipality's risk
classification. The SFC will report the results and findings of these audits to the MOH once they are
completed.
*  The PCU will report on any audit exceptions and internal control weaknesses, and will check to see if
the necessary corrective actions have been taken every quarter.
*  The Bank will monitor the whole auditing process as described above as part of its overall financial
Management supervision of the Project.
2.33   In summary, due to the operational characteristics and pooling feature, the Project's audit
arrangements are designed to ensure appropriate accountability and good governance through effective
managerial interventions and adequate inspection coverage of expenditures documented to deternine and
ensure the eligibility of the expenditures financed by the Bank. The Federal Court of Accounts or Tribunal
de Contas da Uniao (TCU) may also audit the project at their own discretion. Table B2 summarizes the
audit reporting requirements.

2.34   Reporting and Monitoring. The project will report in the PMR format on a quarterly basis as per
the models already agreed with the Bank. The reports will be generated directly from the Financial
Management System maintained at the PCU. The quarterly PMRs will show the expected figures, the
amounts transferred via SOTs, and actual expenditures and will reconcile between these amounts. Thus,
the PMRs will provide sufficient evidence and links between the transfers and actual expenditures. The
Bank will review the PMR, giving special attention to the reconciliation between the SOT transfers and
actual expenditures to ascertain the eligibility of the expenses subject to the Bank's financing. These
statements (PMRs) can also be used for disbursement purposes if the Project's management decides to use
the periodic disbursement method and obtains clearance to this effect from the Bank. The municipalities
will report to the PCU about the outputs of their health programs as established by the MOH in the relevant
signed agreements with participating municipalities. Also, the municipalities will provide financial
statements to the PCU using a simplified PMR format, to report actual financial data compared with the
- 141 -



estimated figures that will have appeared in the Conversion Plans. The municipalities will maintain the
necessary documentation, which will be subject to inspection by the PCU and the Bank's supervision
missions and also to being audited by the SFC. Other reports may be issued to report on the physical,
financial, and managerial execution of the Subprojects according to MOH guidelines and/or as deemed
necessary by the supervision missions of the Bank.
2.35   Financial Management Performance      Indicators:  The  following  financial management
performance indicators have been agreed to ensure the sustainability of the qualification credentials. In case
any given municipality falls short of any of the financial performance indicators, it will be excluded from
Phase 2 of the project.
*   It must have received no more than one downgrade in the financial management risk classification
during the implementation of the Project.
*   It must comply with the requirement to submit financial management reports to the PCU in at least 70
percent of cases by the established due dates set for the release of Component I funds.
*   It must provide evidence satisfactory to the Bank that it has taken appropriate actions to address all of
the recommendations contained in the auditor's management letter during the implementation of the
Project.

comply with all financial management requirements.
